Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Dilemma of God's Knowledge in Eternity
Maturidi presents a dilemma on God's knowledge in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that arises if one denies God's eternal knowledge: either God knew His state or He did not, leading to necessary consequences.

Core Idea
Denying eternal knowledge forces a false choice between ignorance and createdness.

Maturidi begins by posing a direct question to those who deny that God's knowledge is eternal. If God did not know His own state in pre-eternity, then He would be ignorant—an impossibility. This introductory dilemma sets the stage for deeper arguments against the denial of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Note the structure of the dilemma.
The passage uses a conditional structure.
2
Identify the two horns
The passage presents two possibilities: either God knew His state in eternity or He did not. Find each and see the conclusion drawn.
3
Understand the underlying principle
Notice that the opponent defines 'knower' as negation of ignorance. This is key to the dilemma: if not knowing, then ignorant.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يُسأل: كيف كان في الأزل؟ إن علم أنه كان كذلك في الأزل، فيلزمه الاسم كذلك، أو علم أنه لم يكن، فيلحقه اسم الجهل. وهو بقولهم لازم؛ لأن تأويل العالم عندهم نفي الجهل، فإذا لم يكن عالما في القديم فهو إذا عند ذلك كان جاهلا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم يُتكلّم في العلم، إذ لم يكن حتى كان بأن حدث، فيجب ذلك في كل شيء؛ مع ما يقال: كيف حدث به ولم يكن له قدرة؟ أو بغيره، فيبطل به توحيدهم؟ ثم يقال له في الفصل الذي ذكرتُ أنه كان يعلم ذاته قبل الخلق: إذ لم يكن له علم في الحقيقة كيف كان يعلم ذاته؟ فإن علمه عالما بطل قوله بحدث الاسم. وإن قال: غير عالم ولا قادر عليه، دخل عليه جميع ما ذكرت، مع إحالة الوصف له بالعلم به في الأزل مع فساد ما بينا في الحدث. وإن قال من بعد بغيره، رآه ممن يعترض فيه العوارض التي بها تكوّن العالم، وفي ذلك موافقة الدهرية في الطينة، وأصحاب الهيولى والثنوية في كون العالم باعتراض العوارض في الأصل ولا قوة إلا بالله. وهذه المسألة مسألة الصفات في التحقيق، وقد بينا ذلك.
Then it is asked: How was He in eternity? If one knows that He was thus in eternity, then the name (i.e., 'Knower') is necessitated for Him likewise; or if one knows that He was not thus, then the name of ignorance attaches to Him. And that is necessary according to their statement, because the interpretation of 'Knower' with them is the negation of ignorance. So if He was not knowing in pre-eternity, then He was at that time ignorant. And there is no power except with Allah. Then one speaks about knowledge: since it did not exist until it came to be by way of origination, that necessitates the same in everything. Along with what is said: How did it come to be by Him when He had no power? Or by something else, which would invalidate their tawhid? Then it is said to him in the section I mentioned: He knew His essence before creation. Since He did not have actual knowledge, how did He know His essence? If He knows it as knowing, his statement about the createdness of the name is invalidated. And if he says: He is not knowing nor powerful over it, then all that I mentioned applies to him, along with the impossibility of describing Him with knowledge of it in eternity, and the corruption of what we explained about createdness. And if he says after that by something else, he is seen as one who affirms that accidents affect Him, by which the world is constituted. And in that is agreement with the Dahriyya concerning primordial matter, and the supporters of hyle and the dualists concerning the constitution of the world by the intervention of accidents in the origin. And there is no power except with Allah. This question, in reality, is the question of attributes, and we have explained that.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers against the deniers of eternal attributes. The first objection: either God knew His state in eternity or He did not. If He knew, then the name 'Knower' applies eternally; if He did not know, then He was ignorant. That is a dilemma. Next, discussion about knowledge: if it is originated, then everything would be originated similarly. Then a further objection: how did knowledge come to be if He had no power, or if by another, that invalidates tawhid. Then the final objection: if the opponent says God knew His essence before creation without having actual knowledge, then ask how He knew. If He knew as knowing, then createdness of name is false. If He says He was not knowing, then all contradictions apply. If the opponent attributes this to something else, that leads to the view that accidents affect God, which aligns with materialists (Dahriyya) and dualists. Maturidi concludes that this is truly the issue of attributes, and he has clarified it.

Key Terms
القديم
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Maturidi is suggesting God could be ignorant.
Correction: Maturidi is refuting the denial of eternal knowledge by showing it leads to the impossible conclusion of ignorance, not endorsing that possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the interpretation of 'Knower' (العالم) held by those being refuted?
A) One who possesses knowledge
B) Negation of ignorance
C) One who knows all things
D) One who is aware of the unseen

Key Takeaway
Denying that God knows His own state in eternity forces a false dilemma that leads to the impossible attribution of ignorance to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's knowledge of you—past, present, future—does it reassure you or frighten you? Why? What does that reveal about your trust in His perfect knowledge?
?
This passage uses a dilemma to show the impossibility of denying God's eternal knowledge. Are there areas of your life where you act as if God didn't know—as if you can hide from His sight? What step will you take today to live in the awareness that He sees and knows all?
?
The argument hinges on the definition of 'Knower' as negation of ignorance. When you think about God's knowledge, is it an abstract concept, or does it change how you live this week? Commit to one action that acknowledges His omniscience.

Objections Against Denying Eternal Knowledge
Maturidi presents a chain of objections against originated knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's series of objections against the view that knowledge is originated, culminating in showing that such a view aligns with materialists.

Core Idea
If knowledge is created, then God would need power or another creator, leading to contradictions.

Maturidi continues his refutation by examining the claim that knowledge came into existence through origination. He raises questions about the source of this knowledge—if through God without power, or through something else—and then applies the same reasoning to God's self-knowledge. Ultimately, he shows that such views reduce to agreement with materialists and dualists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the series of rhetorical questions and objections.
The passage is dense; take time to follow each point.
2
Identify the main objections
Find the objection about origination, the question of power, the self-knowledge problem, and the final consequence.
3
Understand the flow of argument
See how each objection builds on the previous, leading to the conclusion that the denial of eternal attributes leads to agreement with false sects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يُسأل: كيف كان في الأزل؟ إن علم أنه كان كذلك في الأزل، فيلزمه الاسم كذلك، أو علم أنه لم يكن، فيلحقه اسم الجهل. وهو بقولهم لازم؛ لأن تأويل العالم عندهم نفي الجهل، فإذا لم يكن عالما في القديم فهو إذا عند ذلك كان جاهلا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم يُتكلّم في العلم، إذ لم يكن حتى كان بأن حدث، فيجب ذلك في كل شيء؛ مع ما يقال: كيف حدث به ولم يكن له قدرة؟ أو بغيره، فيبطل به توحيدهم؟ ثم يقال له في الفصل الذي ذكرتُ أنه كان يعلم ذاته قبل الخلق: إذ لم يكن له علم في الحقيقة كيف كان يعلم ذاته؟ فإن علمه عالما بطل قوله بحدث الاسم. وإن قال: غير عالم ولا قادر عليه، دخل عليه جميع ما ذكرت، مع إحالة الوصف له بالعلم به في الأزل مع فساد ما بينا في الحدث. وإن قال من بعد بغيره، رآه ممن يعترض فيه العوارض التي بها تكوّن العالم، وفي ذلك موافقة الدهرية في الطينة، وأصحاب الهيولى والثنوية في كون العالم باعتراض العوارض في الأصل ولا قوة إلا بالله. وهذه المسألة مسألة الصفات في التحقيق، وقد بينا ذلك.
Then it is asked: How was He in eternity? If one knows that He was thus in eternity, then the name (i.e., 'Knower') is necessitated for Him likewise; or if one knows that He was not thus, then the name of ignorance attaches to Him. And that is necessary according to their statement, because the interpretation of 'Knower' with them is the negation of ignorance. So if He was not knowing in pre-eternity, then He was at that time ignorant. And there is no power except with Allah. Then one speaks about knowledge: since it did not exist until it came to be by way of origination, that necessitates the same in everything. Along with what is said: How did it come to be by Him when He had no power? Or by something else, which would invalidate their tawhid? Then it is said to him in the section I mentioned: He knew His essence before creation. Since He did not have actual knowledge, how did He know His essence? If He knows it as knowing, his statement about the createdness of the name is invalidated. And if he says: He is not knowing nor powerful over it, then all that I mentioned applies to him, along with the impossibility of describing Him with knowledge of it in eternity, and the corruption of what we explained about createdness. And if he says after that by something else, he is seen as one who affirms that accidents affect Him, by which the world is constituted. And in that is agreement with the Dahriyya concerning primordial matter, and the supporters of hyle and the dualists concerning the constitution of the world by the intervention of accidents in the origin. And there is no power except with Allah. This question, in reality, is the question of attributes, and we have explained that.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers against the deniers of eternal attributes. The first objection: either God knew His state in eternity or He did not. If He knew, then the name 'Knower' applies eternally; if He did not know, then He was ignorant. That is a dilemma. Next, discussion about knowledge: if it is originated, then everything would be originated similarly. Then a further objection: how did knowledge come to be if He had no power, or if by another, that invalidates tawhid. Then the final objection: if the opponent says God knew His essence before creation without having actual knowledge, then ask how He knew. If He knew as knowing, then createdness of name is false. If He says He was not knowing, then all contradictions apply. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi is proving that knowledge is created.
Correction: Maturidi is refuting the claim that knowledge is created; he shows it leads to absurdities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does affirming that accidents affect God lead to?
A) Agreement with the Dahriyya
B) Agreement with the Mu'tazila
C) Agreement with the Ash'ariyya
D) Agreement with the Jahmiyya

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that denying eternal knowledge leads to a cascade of contradictions, ultimately aligning with materialist and dualist views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever hesitated to affirm that Allah's knowledge is eternal, what does this logical cascade reveal about the hidden assumptions in your heart about Allah's perfection? Will you repent of any doubt and submit to the clear truth that His knowledge has always been complete?
?
How does the argument that denying eternal knowledge aligns with materialist and dualist views expose the danger of dismissing foundational Islamic beliefs? Are there areas in your life where you accommodate worldly philosophies that undermine Allah's attributes?
?
When you truly consider that Allah knew every sin you would commit before creating you—and still created you with mercy—does your heart break in gratitude or shrink in fear? How will you let this truth transform your relationship with Him today?

The Dilemma of Ignorance
Maturidi presents the core dilemma: no eternal attribute equals ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that denying eternal attributes leads to the absurd conclusion that God was ignorant in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Denying eternal attributes makes God ignorant.

Maturidi opens his refutation of Jahm by exposing a devastating consequence: if God had no attributes in eternity, then He was not knowing, which for the Jahmiyya definitionally implies ignorance. This dilemma forms the foundation of the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the logical moves.
2
Identify the dilemma
The passage presents two options: either one knows that God was knowing in eternity (which attaches the name 'Ālim eternally) or one knows He was not knowing (which attaches the name 'ignorant'). Find these two options and see how they trap the opponent.
The second option leads to an impossibility, forcing the first.
3
Reflect on the definition of 'Ālim
Maturidi clarifies that for the Jahmiyya, 'Ālim means 'not ignorant'. Therefore, if God was not knowing in pre-eternity, He was ignorant. This is the core of the dilemma.
This shows that the denial of attributes contradicts their own definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأزل. وفي غيريّة الاسم فساد التوحيد. والأصل على قول منكري الصفات - إذ لم يكن له هذا الاسم ولم يكن له صفة هي علم يعلم ذاته في الأزل - [أن] يجب ما قاله جهم بنفي الأسماء والصفات وحدثها، فيكون غير عالم ولا قادر ثم علم . جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى. ثم يُسأل : كيف كان [ في الأزل] ؟ إن علم أنه كان كذلك في الأزل، فيلزمه الاسم كذلك، أو علم أنه لم يكن [كذلك]، فيلحقه اسم الجهل. وهو بقولهم لازم؛ لأن تأويل العالم عندهم نفي الجهل، فإذا لم يكن عالماً في القديم فهو إذا عند ذلك كان جاهلاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم يُتكلّم في العلم ، إذ لم يكن حتى كان بأن حدث، فيجب ذلك في كل شيء؛ مع ما يقال : كيف حدث به ولم يكن له قدرة؟ أو بغيره، فيبطل به توحيدهم؟ ثم يقال له في الفصل الذي ذكرتُ أنه كان يعلم ذاته قبل الخلق: إذ لم يكن له علم في الحقيقة كيف كان يعلم ذاته ؟ فإن علمه عالما بطل قوله بحدث الاسم. وإن قال : غير عالم ولا قادر عليه، دخل عليه جميع ما ذكرت، مع إحالة الوصف له بالعلم به في الأزل [و] مع فساد ما بينا في الحدث. وإن قال من بعد بغيره، رآه ممن يعترض فيه العوارض [التي] بها" تكوّن العالم، وفي ذلك موافقة الدهرية في الطينة، وأصحاب الهيولى والثنوية في كون العالم باعتراض العوارض في الأصل ولا قوة إلا بالله . وهذه المسألة مسألة الصفات في التحقيق، وقد بيّنا ذلك.
Eternity. And in the otherness of the name is the corruption of tawhid. The principle according to the deniers of attributes—since He did not have this name and He did not have an attribute that is knowledge by which He knows His essence in eternity—[is] that what Jahm said by negating names and attributes and their temporal origination (ḥudūth) is necessary, so He would be not knowing and not powerful then [became] knowing. Exalted is Allah above that and greatly exalted. Then he is asked: How was He [in eternity]? If he knows that He was thus in eternity, then the name attaches to Him likewise; or if he knows that He was not [thus], then the name of ignorance attaches to him. And it is necessary according to their statement; because their interpretation of 'Ālim is the negation of ignorance, so if He was not knowing in pre-eternity, then He was at that time ignorant. And there is no power except by Allah. Then discourse is held about knowledge: it was not [existing] until it came to be by temporal origination, so that is necessary in everything; along with what is said: How did it come to be through Him while He had no power? Or through other than Him, so their tawhid is nullified by that? Then it is said to him in the section that I mentioned that He knew His essence before creation: since He had no knowledge in reality, how did He know His essence? For if he knows Him as knowing, his statement about the temporal origination of the name becomes invalid. And if he says: not knowing nor powerful over it, then all that I have mentioned enters upon him, along with the impossibility of describing Him as having knowledge of it in eternity and along with the corruption of what we have shown about temporal origination. And if he says afterwards [that it comes] from other than Him, he would find himself among those who hold that accidental attributes (al-aʿrāḍ) affect it, by which the world comes to be, and in that is agreement with the Dahrīs concerning matter, and with the proponents of hyle and the dualists concerning the world's coming to be by the effect of accidents in the origin. And there is no power except by Allah. And this issue is the issue of attributes in actuality, and we have clarified that.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument refuting Jahm ibn Ṣafwān's doctrine that God's names and attributes are temporally originated (ḥādith). The author (Maturidi) begins by stating a key principle: positing that God's attribute of knowledge (or any attribute) is 'other' than His essence in a way that implies temporal origination corrupts tawḥīd. The argument proceeds as follows: Jahm denies eternal attributes; this leads to the consequence that in pre-eternity (al-azal) God was neither knowing nor powerful, and then 'became' knowing—which is impossible. The author then poses a dilemma: either one knows that God was knowing in eternity (so the name 'Ālim applies eternally) or one knows He was not knowing (which implies ignorance, since for the Mu'tazila/Jahmiyya 'Ālim is defined as 'not ignorant'). So Jahm's view entails ignorance of God in the past, a clear absurdity. Next, the author addresses the specific case of God's knowledge: if knowledge is temporally originated, then either it arose by God's power (but He had no power before it existed) or by another—both alternatives are problematic. The first implies God was powerless, the second undermines tawḥīd by making an external cause. The author also presses the point that if God knew His essence before creation, that presupposes eternal knowledge; if not, how could He know it? The argument concludes by warning that Jahm's position leads to agreement with the Dahrīs (materialists) and dualists who attribute the world's existence to accidental causes. The author then asserts that this entire discussion is truly about the divine attributes (al-ṣifāt) and that he has already clarified the matter (likely earlier in the book).

Key Terms
الأزل
غيريّة الاسم
جهم
العالم
الجاهل
الحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that denying eternal attributes only affects names, not God's essence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the denial directly implies that God was ignorant, which is a deficiency in essence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, what is the consequence if one says God was not knowing in pre-eternity?
A) God was simply not knowing, which is acceptable.
B) God must have been ignorant, because 'Ālim means 'not ignorant'.
C) God's knowledge is temporally originated.
D) The name 'Ālim still applies.

Key Takeaway
If God was not knowing in pre-eternity, then He was ignorant, which is impossible, so His knowledge must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God has always known you—every thought, every sin, every struggle—does that terrify you or comfort you? Why? And how would your daily actions change if you lived as if this were absolutely true?
?
The Jahmites argued that God became knowing later, after not knowing. Have you ever acted as if God only knows some things about you, or as if He doesn't know your secret intentions? Repent of that and resolve to live with full awareness of His eternal knowledge starting today.
?
The passage says denying eternal attributes leads to the corruption of tawḥīd. Examine your own understanding: is there any area where your belief about God's attributes is unclear or compromised? What specific step will you take to correct it and strengthen your tawḥīd?

The Problem of Origination of Knowledge
Maturidi poses a dilemma about the temporal origination of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if God's knowledge is temporally originated, it poses a dilemma: either it came from God's power (impossible before having knowledge) or from another (breaking tawhid).

Core Idea
Originated knowledge implies either impotence or polytheism.

After establishing the ignorance dilemma, Maturidi turns to the specific claim that knowledge is temporally originated. He shows that this leads to an even deeper problem: how could knowledge come to be without prior power? The argument forces the opponent into an impossible choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the question format and the two alternatives.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possibilities for how knowledge could come to be: either through God (but He had no power) or through other than God. Find these and see why both are problematic.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on why each alternative is impossible: the first implies God was powerless before knowledge existed; the second undermines tawhid by making an external cause.
This argument strengthens the necessity of eternal knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأزل. وفي غيريّة الاسم فساد التوحيد. والأصل على قول منكري الصفات - إذ لم يكن له هذا الاسم ولم يكن له صفة هي علم يعلم ذاته في الأزل - [أن] يجب ما قاله جهم بنفي الأسماء والصفات وحدثها، فيكون غير عالم ولا قادر ثم علم . جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى. ثم يُسأل : كيف كان [ في الأزل] ؟ إن علم أنه كان كذلك في الأزل، فيلزمه الاسم كذلك، أو علم أنه لم يكن [كذلك]، فيلحقه اسم الجهل. وهو بقولهم لازم؛ لأن تأويل العالم عندهم نفي الجهل، فإذا لم يكن عالماً في القديم فهو إذا عند ذلك كان جاهلاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم يُتكلّم في العلم ، إذ لم يكن حتى كان بأن حدث، فيجب ذلك في كل شيء؛ مع ما يقال : كيف حدث به ولم يكن له قدرة؟ أو بغيره، فيبطل به توحيدهم؟ ثم يقال له في الفصل الذي ذكرتُ أنه كان يعلم ذاته قبل الخلق: إذ لم يكن له علم في الحقيقة كيف كان يعلم ذاته ؟ فإن علمه عالما بطل قوله بحدث الاسم. وإن قال : غير عالم ولا قادر عليه، دخل عليه جميع ما ذكرت، مع إحالة الوصف له بالعلم به في الأزل [و] مع فساد ما بينا في الحدث. وإن قال من بعد بغيره، رآه ممن يعترض فيه العوارض [التي] بها" تكوّن العالم، وفي ذلك موافقة الدهرية في الطينة، وأصحاب الهيولى والثنوية في كون العالم باعتراض العوارض في الأصل ولا قوة إلا بالله . وهذه المسألة مسألة الصفات في التحقيق، وقد بيّنا ذلك.
Eternity. And in the otherness of the name is the corruption of tawhid. The principle according to the deniers of attributes—since He did not have this name and He did not have an attribute that is knowledge by which He knows His essence in eternity—[is] that what Jahm said by negating names and attributes and their temporal origination (ḥudūth) is necessary, so He would be not knowing and not powerful then [became] knowing. Exalted is Allah above that and greatly exalted. Then he is asked: How was He [in eternity]? If he knows that He was thus in eternity, then the name attaches to Him likewise; or if he knows that He was not [thus], then the name of ignorance attaches to him. And it is necessary according to their statement; because their interpretation of 'Ālim is the negation of ignorance, so if He was not knowing in pre-eternity, then He was at that time ignorant. And there is no power except by Allah. Then discourse is held about knowledge: it was not [existing] until it came to be by temporal origination, so that is necessary in everything; along with what is said: How did it come to be through Him while He had no power? Or through other than Him, so their tawhid is nullified by that? Then it is said to him in the section that I mentioned that He knew His essence before creation: since He had no knowledge in reality, how did He know His essence? For if he knows Him as knowing, his statement about the temporal origination of the name becomes invalid. And if he says: not knowing nor powerful over it, then all that I have mentioned enters upon him, along with the impossibility of describing Him as having knowledge of it in eternity and along with the corruption of what we have shown about temporal origination. And if he says afterwards [that it comes] from other than Him, he would find himself among those who hold that accidental attributes (al-aʿrāḍ) affect it, by which the world comes to be, and in that is agreement with the Dahrīs concerning matter, and with the proponents of hyle and the dualists concerning the world's coming to be by the effect of accidents in the origin. And there is no power except by Allah. And this issue is the issue of attributes in actuality, and we have clarified that.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument refuting Jahm ibn Ṣafwān's doctrine that God's names and attributes are temporally originated (ḥādith). The author (Maturidi) begins by stating a key principle: positing that God's attribute of knowledge (or any attribute) is 'other' than His essence in a way that implies temporal origination corrupts tawḥīd. The argument proceeds as follows: Jahm denies eternal attributes; this leads to the consequence that in pre-eternity (al-azal) God was neither knowing nor powerful, and then 'became' knowing—which is impossible. The author then poses a dilemma: either one knows that God was knowing in eternity (so the name 'Ālim applies eternally) or one knows He was not knowing (which implies ignorance, since for the Mu'tazila/Jahmiyya 'Ālim is defined as 'not ignorant'). So Jahm's view entails ignorance of God in the past, a clear absurdity. Next, the author addresses the specific case of God's knowledge: if knowledge is temporally originated, then either it arose by God's power (but He had no power before it existed) or by another—both alternatives are problematic. The first implies God was powerless, the second undermines tawḥīd by making an external cause. The author also presses the point that if God knew His essence before creation, that presupposes eternal knowledge; if not, how could He know it? The argument concludes by warning that Jahm's position leads to agreement with the Dahrīs (materialists) and dualists who attribute the world's existence to accidental causes. The author then asserts that this entire discussion is truly about the divine attributes (al-ṣifāt) and that he has already clarified the matter (likely earlier in the book).

Key Terms
العلم
الحدث
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two ways knowledge could be temporally originated?
A) Through God's essence or through His attributes.
B) Through God (with His power) or through other than God.
C) Through creation ex nihilo or through emanation.
D) Through God's will or through necessity.

Key Takeaway
Temporal origination of God's knowledge forces one to choose between divine impotence and a partner in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's knowledge, do you ever subconsciously imagine it as something that came into existence or changed over time? How does Maturidi's dilemma expose that thought as a form of shirk?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's knowledge is eternal and not acquired, what would change about how you speak about Him in your daily conversations? Are your words consistent with this belief?
?
Consider a time you doubted that Allah knew something before it happened. How did that doubt affect your relationship with Him? What would it mean to fully accept that His knowledge has no beginning?

God's Knowledge of His Essence
Maturidi presents a conditional argument about God's self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if God knew His essence before creation, that presupposes eternal knowledge, contradicting the claim that knowledge is temporally originated.

Core Idea
If God knew His essence, He must have had eternal knowledge.

Maturidi tightens the argument by considering God's knowledge of His own essence before creation. If Jahm admits that God knew His essence, then that knowledge must be eternal, refuting temporal origination. If he denies it, the consequences are even worse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the conditional structure.
2
Identify the conditional argument
The passage presents two conditions: if Jahm says God knew His essence, then knowledge is eternal; if he says God did not know, then all previous consequences apply. Find these two branches.
3
Reflect on the outcome
Understand why both options are problematic for Jahm: either his core claim (temporal origination) is refuted, or he falls into the earlier dilemmas.
This shows the impossibility of denying eternal knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأزل. وفي غيريّة الاسم فساد التوحيد. والأصل على قول منكري الصفات - إذ لم يكن له هذا الاسم ولم يكن له صفة هي علم يعلم ذاته في الأزل - [أن] يجب ما قاله جهم بنفي الأسماء والصفات وحدثها، فيكون غير عالم ولا قادر ثم علم . جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى. ثم يُسأل : كيف كان [ في الأزل] ؟ إن علم أنه كان كذلك في الأزل، فيلزمه الاسم كذلك، أو علم أنه لم يكن [كذلك]، فيلحقه اسم الجهل. وهو بقولهم لازم؛ لأن تأويل العالم عندهم نفي الجهل، فإذا لم يكن عالماً في القديم فهو إذا عند ذلك كان جاهلاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم يُتكلّم في العلم ، إذ لم يكن حتى كان بأن حدث، فيجب ذلك في كل شيء؛ مع ما يقال : كيف حدث به ولم يكن له قدرة؟ أو بغيره، فيبطل به توحيدهم؟ ثم يقال له في الفصل الذي ذكرتُ أنه كان يعلم ذاته قبل الخلق: إذ لم يكن له علم في الحقيقة كيف كان يعلم ذاته ؟ فإن علمه عالما بطل قوله بحدث الاسم. وإن قال : غير عالم ولا قادر عليه، دخل عليه جميع ما ذكرت، مع إحالة الوصف له بالعلم به في الأزل [و] مع فساد ما بينا في الحدث. وإن قال من بعد بغيره، رآه ممن يعترض فيه العوارض [التي] بها" تكوّن العالم، وفي ذلك موافقة الدهرية في الطينة، وأصحاب الهيولى والثنوية في كون العالم باعتراض العوارض في الأصل ولا قوة إلا بالله . وهذه المسألة مسألة الصفات في التحقيق، وقد بيّنا ذلك.
Eternity. And in the otherness of the name is the corruption of tawhid. The principle according to the deniers of attributes—since He did not have this name and He did not have an attribute that is knowledge by which He knows His essence in eternity—[is] that what Jahm said by negating names and attributes and their temporal origination (ḥudūth) is necessary, so He would be not knowing and not powerful then [became] knowing. Exalted is Allah above that and greatly exalted. Then he is asked: How was He [in eternity]? If he knows that He was thus in eternity, then the name attaches to Him likewise; or if he knows that He was not [thus], then the name of ignorance attaches to him. And it is necessary according to their statement; because their interpretation of 'Ālim is the negation of ignorance, so if He was not knowing in pre-eternity, then He was at that time ignorant. And there is no power except by Allah. Then discourse is held about knowledge: it was not [existing] until it came to be by temporal origination, so that is necessary in everything; along with what is said: How did it come to be through Him while He had no power? Or through other than Him, so their tawhid is nullified by that? Then it is said to him in the section that I mentioned that He knew His essence before creation: since He had no knowledge in reality, how did He know His essence? For if he knows Him as knowing, his statement about the temporal origination of the name becomes invalid. And if he says: not knowing nor powerful over it, then all that I have mentioned enters upon him, along with the impossibility of describing Him as having knowledge of it in eternity and along with the corruption of what we have shown about temporal origination. And if he says afterwards [that it comes] from other than Him, he would find himself among those who hold that accidental attributes (al-aʿrāḍ) affect it, by which the world comes to be, and in that is agreement with the Dahrīs concerning matter, and with the proponents of hyle and the dualists concerning the world's coming to be by the effect of accidents in the origin. ?

?

?

The Slippery Slope to Materialism
Maturidi warns that Jahm's view coincides with materialist and dualist theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's warning that Jahm's position leads to agreement with materialists and dualists, undermining Islamic monotheism.

Core Idea
Denying eternal attributes aligns with materialist and dualist worldviews.

Maturidi concludes his refutation by showing that the consequence of Jahm's view is not just inconsistency but actual agreement with pre-Islamic materialists (Dahrīs) and dualists. This strengthens the argument that the denial of eternal attributes is a dangerous deviation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the groups mentioned.
2
Identify the groups
The passage mentions three groups: the Dahrīs (materialists), the proponents of hyle (prime matter), and the dualists. Find each group and understand their views as described.
3
Understand the connection
Reflect on why Maturidi says Jahm's position leads to agreement with these groups. The key is the role of accidental attributes (al-aʿrāḍ) in bringing about the world.
This shows the serious consequences of denying eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Is there any attribute of Allah—such as His knowledge, power, or mercy—that you are tempted to minimize or explain away to make your faith more 'reasonable'? ?
The materialists see the world's events as mere accidents affecting matter. Do you ever catch yourself attributing the good or bad things in your life to 'luck' or 'coincidence' rather than to Allah's decree? How can you train your heart to see His hand in every moment, even the painful ones? Set a daily reminder to pause and attribute each significant event directly to Him.
?
Maturidi warns that a seemingly small theological error (Jahm's denial of eternal attributes) has massive, damning consequences. Is there a belief or practice in your own life that you have dismissed as 'minor' or 'not that important'? What would it cost you to examine it honestly in light of the Qur'an and Sunnah? Delay not—resolve to investigate it this week.

First Opinion: God on the Throne
Maturidi presents the first group's view that God is on the Throne with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first group's opinion that God is established on the Throne and the scriptural proofs they use.

Core Idea
The first group argues that God is physically on the Throne based on Quranic verses.

Maturidi begins his discussion of the dispute about God's relation to place by presenting the view of those who affirm that God is established on the Throne. This group relies on apparent meanings of Quranic verses about the Throne and the act of istiwa'. In this lesson, we examine their position and the evidence they cite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the structure: definition of Throne, then scriptural proofs.
2
Identify the key scriptural proofs
Look for the Quranic verses quoted: about the Throne being carried and angels surrounding it, and the verse 'al-Rahman ala al-arsh istawa'.
These are the primary textual evidence.
3
Note the logical inference from practice
The group also uses the common practice of raising hands to heaven in supplication as evidence that God is above.
4
Understand the interpretation of 'thumma'
They interpret 'thumma istawa ala al-arsh' as temporal sequence, meaning He came to the Throne after not being.
This is a key point of contention.
5
Recognize this as a reported opinion
Maturidi is reporting this view, not endorsing it. This is the first of several opinions.
Keep in mind the broader context of differing views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة بيان العرش قال أبو منصور رضي الله عنه: ثم اختلف أهل الإسلام في القول بالمكان. فمنهم من زعم أنه يوصف بأنه على العرش مستو؛ والعرش عندهم السرير المحمول بالملائكة المحفوف بهم، بقوله: ويحمل عرش ربك فوقهم يومئذ ثمانية، وقوله: وترى الملائكة حافين من حول العرش، وقوله: الذين يحملون العرش ومن حوله الآية. واحتجوا للقول به بقوله: الرحمن على العرش استوى، ويرفع الناس إلى السماء بالدعوات أيديهم وما يأملون من الخيرات. ويقولون: هو صار إليه بعد أن لم يكن، لقوله: ثم استوى على العرش.
Issue: Explanation of the Throne. Abu Mansur (may Allah be pleased with him) said: Then the people of Islam differed concerning the statement about place. Some of them claimed that He is described as being established on the Throne; and the Throne in their view is the physical throne carried by the angels surrounding it, based on His saying: 'And eight will bear the Throne of your Lord above them on that day' and His saying: 'And you see the angels surrounding the Throne' and His saying: 'Those who bear the Throne and those around it' the verse. And they argued for this by His saying: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' and that people raise their hands to the sky in supplication and in hope of good things. And they say: He came to it after not being, based on His saying: 'Then He established Himself upon the Throne'.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the first group's opinion in the dispute about God's relation to place. Maturidi begins by stating that the people of Islam differed on this issue. The first group claims that God is described as being established (mustawin) on the Throne. For them, the Throne is a physical throne carried by angels, as indicated by Quranic verses. They cite verses about the Throne being carried and the angels surrounding it. Then they support their claim with the verse 'al-Raḥmān 'alā al-ʿarsh istawā' and the fact that people raise their hands to heaven in prayer, implying God is above. They also argue that God came to the Throne after not being, interpreting the word 'thumma' (then) in 'thumma istawā 'alā al-ʿarsh' as temporal sequence. This is the first opinion, which Maturidi is reporting without yet endorsing.

Key Terms
عرش
استواء
مكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Throne is a physical location that confines God.
Correction: This is the first group's opinion; Maturidi is presenting it as one view among several, not necessarily the correct one. The passage does not endorse this view.

Checkpoint
What do the first group (منهم من زعم) use as evidence for God's being on the Throne?
A) Rational arguments only
B) The verse 'الرحمن على العرش استوى' and others
C) The argument that God is everywhere
D) The negation of place altogether

Key Takeaway
The first group argues for God's establishment on the Throne using explicit Quranic verses and common practice, but this is a reported opinion, not Maturidi's own.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'al-Rahman 'ala al-arsh istawa', what picture forms in your mind? Are you willing to let that picture remain unsettled, trusting that Allah is beyond your imagination?
?
Do you ever find yourself wanting to locate God—in the sky, in your heart, or somewhere—out of a need to feel close? How does this lesson challenge you to seek closeness without confining Him to a place?
?
If you have ever assumed that 'thumma' in the Quran implies change in God, pause now: are you willing to submit to the fact that Allah's actions are not like our actions, and that His 'coming' is beyond temporal sequence?

Second Opinion: God is Everywhere
Maturidi reports the view that God is everywhere with scriptural and rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second group's scriptural and rational arguments for God being everywhere and see how they attempt to safeguard divine transcendence.

Core Idea
If God were in one place, He would be limited—so He must be everywhere.

After presenting the majority view that God is in heaven, Maturidi now turns to a second opinion. This group argues that God is everywhere, using both Qur'anic verses and rational reasoning. Their core concern is to protect God from any notion of limitation, which they see as a defect. Let's examine their arguments closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four Qur'anic proofs
The group cites four verses. Read each one in the Arabic and translation. Notice how they interpret 'nearness' and 'being with' as omnipresence.
2
Analyze the rational argument
The group argues that if God were in one place, He would be limited (ḥadd), and limited things are deficient. They see limitation as a defect (āfa). Understand this logical chain.
3
Consider the implied criticism
The passage ends with Maturidi's exaltation of God. Reflect on whether the second group's view fully avoids the problems they identify. This sets up Maturidi's own position.
Watch for the phrase 'exalted is our Lord above that'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من يقول: هو بكل مكان، بقوله: ما يكون من نجوى ثلاثة إلا هو رابعهم الآية، وقوله: ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد، وقوله: ونحن أقرب إليه منكم ولكن لا تبصرون، وقوله: وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله؛ وظنوا أن القول بأنه في مكان دون مكان يوجب الحد، وكل ذي حد مقصر عما هو أعظم منه، وذلك عيب وآفة؛ وفي ذلك إيجاب الحاجة إلى المكان، مع ما فيه إيجاب الحد، إذ لا يحتمل أن يكون أعظم من المكان لما هو سخف في المتعارف أن يختار أحد مكانا لا يسعه؛ فيصير حد المكان حده، جل ربنا عن ذلك وتعالى.
And among them are those who say: He is in every place, based on His saying: 'There is no secret conversation of three but He is their fourth' the verse, and His saying: 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein', and His saying: 'And We are nearer to him than you, but you do not see', and His saying: 'And He is the God in the heaven and the God on the earth'. And they thought that saying He is in one place without another imposes limitation (ḥadd), and every limited thing is deficient relative to what is greater than it, and that is a defect and blemish. And in that is the imposition of need for place, along with what it contains of imposing limit, since it is not plausible that He should be greater than place, because it is absurd in common sense that one should choose a place that does not contain Him. So the limit of place becomes His limit—exalted is our Lord above that and highly exalted.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the second group's opinion. They argue that God is everywhere, citing verses that speak of God's presence with people, His nearness, and His being God in both heaven and earth. Their rational argument is that if God were in one specific place, that would impose a limit (ḥadd) on Him, and anything limited is less than what is beyond it—a deficiency. They also argue that being in one place would imply need for that place, and it would be absurd for Him to be confined by a place that cannot contain Him. Thus they conclude that God cannot be limited to any single place. Maturidi reports this view and ends with 'exalted is our Lord above that and highly exalted', which may be his own affirmation of God's transcendence.

Key Terms

حد
آفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying spatial limits denies God's transcendence.
Correction: The second group sees limit as a deficiency, so they affirm transcendence by denying limit.

Checkpoint

A) That would imply God is needy of space
B) That would make God less than the space
C) That would impose a boundary on God
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The second group argues that God is everywhere because restricting Him to one place would impose a limitation, which is a defect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which spaces in your life have you unconsciously excluded from His presence, and how does the group's insistence that He is everywhere (even there) make you uncomfortable?

Will you intentionally cultivate awareness of His constant nearness in the mundane—the shower, the commute, the argument with your spouse?
?
The group argued that being confined to a place implies dependency on that place. Where in your heart have you placed conditions on God's ability to help you—insisting He can only act in certain ways or through certain means? How will you dismantle that hidden limit you have placed on the Almighty?

Maturidi's Interpretation of Divine Attributions: Beyond Spatial Language
Maturidi endorses the negation of place and classifies divine attributions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi explains that attributions of things to Allah (both general and specific) are expressions of majesty and honour, not literal spatial or possessive relationships.

Core Idea
Divine attributions signify honour, not location.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a third opinion that denies any spatial description of Allah. He then provides a comprehensive hermeneutical principle: all divine attributions—whether general (like 'Lord of the heavens') or specific (like 'House of Allah')—are to be understood as expressions of Allah's highness, majesty, and special favour, not as literal spatial or possessive relations. This insight protects against anthropomorphism while affirming the scriptural language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi first presents the third opinion and then gives his own summary.
2
Identify the third opinion
Find the view that negates describing Allah with place, and note the exception for metaphorical language meaning 'Preserver' and 'Sustainer'.
3
Distinguish general and specific attributions
Maturidi divides attributions into two types: general (e.g., 'Lord of the heavens') and specific (e.g., 'House of Allah'). Identify the examples and notice what each type expresses.
General attributions indicate highness and majesty; specific attributions indicate honour and favour.
4
Understand the hermeneutical rule
Maturidi emphasizes that these divine attributions should not be understood like human relations. Even in creation, specific attributions can denote honour (e.g., 'the king's horse') rather than possession. Realize that this principle safeguards tawhid.
This is the key takeaway: scriptural language about Allah is always figurative of honour, never literal spatial or possessive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال بنفي الوصف بالمكان، وكذلك بالأمكنة كلها إلا على مجاز اللغة بمعنى الحافظ لها والقائم بها. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: وجملة ذلك أن إضافة كلية الأشياء إليه، وإضافته عز وجل إليها يخرج مخرج الوصف له بالعلو والرفعة ومخرج التعظيم له والجلال، كقوله: وله ملك السموات والأرض الآية، ورب السموات والأرض، وإله الخلق، ورب العالمين، وفوق كل شيء ونحوه. وإضافة الخاص إليه يخرج مخرج الاختصاص له بالكرامة والمنزلة والتفضل له على من هو بجوهره، نحو قوله: وإن الله مع الذين اتقوا الآية، وقوله: وأن المساجد لله، وناقة الله، وبيت الله، وغير ذلك. ولا يخرج شيء من ذلك على مثل المفهوم من إضافة الخلق بعضهم إلى بعض؛ ولا على قطع احتمال مثله في الخلق، إذ قد تخرج أيضا إضافة التخصيص مخرج التفضيل، والعموم مخرج فضل السلطان والولاية.
And among them are those who said by negating the description with place, and likewise with all places, except metaphorically in language meaning the Preserver of them and the Sustainer of them. Sheikh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The summary of that is that the attribution of all things to Him, and His attribution (exalted is He) to them, comes out as a description of Him with highness and elevation and as a description of magnification and majesty for Him, like His saying: 'And to Him belongs the dominion of the heavens and the earth' the verse, and 'Lord of the heavens and the earth', and 'God of creation', and 'Lord of the worlds', and 'Above everything', and the like. And the specific attribution to Him comes out as an attribution of distinction for Him with honour and station and favour for Him over what is by His essence, like His saying: 'And indeed Allah is with those who fear Him' the verse, and His saying: 'And that the mosques belong to Allah', and 'the she-camel of Allah', and 'the House of Allah', and other than that. And none of that comes out according to the understood meaning of the attribution of creation to one another; nor does it cut off the possibility of its like in creation, since the specific attribution may also come out as an attribution of favour, and the general attribution as an attribution of the favour of sovereign authority.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the third opinion and Maturidi's own elaboration. The third group negates describing God with any place, allowing only metaphorical language where 'place' means God's preservation and maintenance of all places. Maturidi then gives the summary: all attributions of things to God—whether general like 'Lord of the heavens' or specific like 'House of God'—are expressions of God's highness, majesty, and special honour, not literal spatial relationships. General attributions indicate His sovereignty and exaltation; specific attributions indicate His favour and distinction to certain entities. He emphasizes that these attributions should not be understood as they are between creatures. Even in creation, specific attributions may denote honour (e.g., 'the king's horse') rather than possession. Therefore, no anthropomorphism or spatial limitation is implied. This is Maturidi's hermeneutical principle for interpreting such verses.

علو
اختصاص

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Phrases like 'House of Allah' mean Allah literally dwells in the Kaaba or possesses it like a human owns a house.
Correction: Maturidi explains that such specific attributions are for honour and favour, not literal possession or spatial relation. They indicate distinction and honour for the entity, not a literal belonging.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that all divine attributions in the Qur'an must be understood as expressions of Allah's majesty and honour, not as literal spatial or possessive relations.

The she-camel was honoured solely because of its connection to Allah. Have you ever reduced that honour to mere habit or social custom? How can you renew your intention to honour them for Allah's sake alone starting today?

In your own life, which of your possessions or relationships do you treat as if they were merely yours by ownership? God Without Place and Changeless
Maturidi states the principle that God existed without place and cannot change, proving His eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's foundational principle that God exists without place and is free from change, and why change implies contingency.

Core Idea
God's changelessness proves His eternity, and change is a mark of creation.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi lays down the foundational principle for understanding God's relation to place and time. He argues that God existed eternally without any place, and that all places are contingent while God remains unchanging. This principle is crucial for establishing God's transcendence and refuting any notion of change in the Divine Essence. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully along with its literal translation to grasp Maturidi's core assertion.
Notice the concise style and logical structure.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the terms مكان (place), تغيّر (change), and حدث (contingency). Understand how they relate to the argument.
These terms are central to Maturidi's proof.
3
Trace the argument on change
Follow Maturidi's reasoning: if God changed from one state to another, that would imply His first state was not essential to Him, thus indicating contingency.
This is the core logical move.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why the possibility of change would make God subject to accidents and transient states, which is incompatible with His eternality.
5
Summarize the principle
Articulate in your own words Maturidi's principle and its significance for divine transcendence.
Check your understanding against the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: الأصل فيه أن الله سبحانه كان ولا مكان، وجائز ارتفاع الأمكنة وبقاؤه على ما كان فهو على ما كان، وكان على ما عليه الآن. جل عن التغيير والزوال والاستحالة والبطلان؛ إذ ذلك أمارات الحدث التي بها عرف حدث العالم ودلالة احتمال الفناء؛ إذ لا فرق بين الزوال من حال إلى حال - يعلم أن حاله الأولى لم تكن لذاته، إذ لا يحتمل زوالها ما لزمت ذاته - وبين ذاته التي ليست لذاته، لما احتمل هو قبول الأعراض وانتقال الأحوال. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle in this is that Allah, glorified is He, existed while there was no place, and it is permissible for places to cease to exist while He remains as He was. So He is as He was, and He was as He is now. He is exalted above change, passing away, alteration, and annihilation; for these are signs of contingency by which the temporality of the world is known and indications of the possibility of extinction. For there is no difference between transition from one state to another—it is known that His first state was not essential to Him, since its passing away would not be possible if it had adhered to His essence—and His essence which is not essential to Him, since He would then be susceptible to accidents and the transition of states. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage contains Maturidi's foundational principle (al-aṣl) regarding God and place. He asserts that God existed eternally without any place, and it is conceivable that all places cease to exist while God remains as He always was. God is unchanged and unchanging. Change, passing away, alteration, and annihilation are signs of contingency (ḥudūth)—they indicate that something is created and subject to perishing. Maturidi argues that if God were to change from one state to another, that would prove that His first state was not essential to Him, because if it were essential, it could not cease. Similarly, if His essence were susceptible to receiving accidents (aʿrāḍ) and transitioning between states, that would indicate contingency. Since God is eternal, He is free from all such change. This argument reinforces the doctrine that God is not in place, because being in place would entail change and contingency.

Key Terms
مكان
تغيّر
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi's argument is that place is impossible for God because place implies spatial limitation.
Correction: Actually, Maturidi argues from changelessness: if God were in place, He would be subject to change, which is a sign of contingency. The argument is about transcendence over change, not spatial containment.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that if God changed state, that would indicate His first state was not essential to Him?
A) Because change is a sign of contingency
B) Because God cannot change by definition
C) Because the first state would have ended
D) Because change implies a cause

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle establishes that God's eternity requires His transcendence over place and change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life seeking stability in people, places, and possessions. But this lesson teaches that all such things are subject to change and extinction. What is one place or situation you consider essential to your happiness? Are you willing to see it as non-essential, as temporary, and anchor your heart solely on the Eternal who never changes?
?
The passage says change is a sign of contingency. Think of your own self—your moods, your beliefs, your body are always changing. If change points to a beginning, does your own experience of constant change remind you that you are not self-sufficient? When did you last pause to reflect that your very existence is borrowed from the Changeless One, and that your dependence on Him is total?
?
The lesson asserts that God was without place and is unchanged. Your daily life is full of places that define your identity: your home, your mosque, your city. But if all places could cease and God remains, what does that mean for your attachment to these places? Would you still feel secure if your most cherished place vanished? What would it mean to worship a Lord who is not tied to any place, not even the Kaaba? How does that challenge your sense of spiritual comfort?

General vs Specific Attribution to Allah
Maturidi distinguishes between two types of idafa to Allah: honor and creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi’s distinction between general and specific attributions to Allah, and why specific attributions indicate honor rather than literal possession.

Core Idea
Specific attribution to Allah signifies honor, not ownership.

Maturidi begins by listing the many general attributes of Allah, such as 'Lord of the worlds.' He then turns to specific attributions like 'the House of Allah' and explains that they are not like human possession but rather a mark of honor and favor. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice the list of general attributes and the specific examples.
Pay attention to how Maturidi structures his argument.
2
Identify the two types of idafa
Find where Maturidi mentions general addition (like 'kingdom of the heavens') and specific addition (like 'the she-camel of Allah'). Note the characteristics of each.
General addition emphasizes dominion; specific addition emphasizes honor.
3
Understand the contrast with human possession
Maturidi says specific addition to Allah does not resemble how creatures attribute things to each other. Reflect on what this means for understanding divine attributes.
This contrasts the limited ownership of creatures with the honorific attribution to Allah.
4
Consider the broader theological significance
Why does Maturidi make this distinction? How does it protect the transcendence of Allah? The commentary will help answer.
This distinction guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله ملك السموات والأرض، ورب السموات والأرض، وإله الخلق، ورب العالمين، وفوق كل شيء ونحوه. وإضافة الخاص إليه يخرج مخرج الاختصاص له بالكرامة والمنزلة والتفضل له على من هو بجوهره، نحو قوله: وإن الله مع الذين اتقوا، وقوله: وأن المساجد الله، وناقة الله، وبيت الله، وغير ذلك. ولا يخرج شيء من ذلك على مثل المفهوم من إضافة الخلق بعضهم إلى بعض؛ [و] لا [على] قطع احتمال مثله في الخلق، إذ قد تخرج أيضا إضافة التخصيص مخرج التفضيل، والعموم مخرج فضل السلطان والولاية
And to Him belongs the kingdom of the heavens and the earth, and the Lord of the heavens and the earth, and the God of creation, and the Lord of the worlds, and above everything, and similar expressions. And the specific addition to Him emerges in the sense of specification for Him of honor, rank, and favor over those who are by their essence, such as His saying: 'And indeed Allah is with those who fear Him,' and His saying: 'And that the mosques belong to Allah,' and 'the she-camel of Allah,' and 'the house of Allah,' and other such. And none of that emerges in the same way as the addition of some created beings to others, nor [does it] cut off the possibility of similar [addition] among creatures, since the addition of specification may also emerge in the sense of preference, and generality in the sense of the ruler's authority and dominion.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of attribution (idafa) to Allah. Maturidi begins by listing expressions like 'kingdom of the heavens and the earth,' 'Lord of the heavens,' etc., which are general attributions. He then states that when a specific thing is attributed to Allah, like 'the mosque of Allah' or 'the she-camel of Allah,' it is a form of honor and specification, not literal possession. He contrasts this with how created beings attribute things to each other. The key point is that such idiomatic additions do not imply the same relationship as possession among creatures. He further notes that this type of specification can also indicate preference or authority, as when a ruler is said to have 'authority,' so the meaning is aligned with honor and majesty, not limitation or need.

Key Terms
إضافة
خاص
عموم
تخصيص
تفضيل
كرامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributing a specific object to Allah (e.g., 'the she-camel of Allah') implies that Allah possesses it as a human possesses property.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies it is a mark of honor, not literal ownership, and such addition does not resemble human possession.

Key Takeaway
When Allah is attributed to a specific thing, it is a sign of honor and majesty, not literal possession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Think of something you have dedicated to Allah—your prayer, your charity, your time. Do you view it as a transaction where you give and expect return, or as a sign of honor that Allah has chosen to associate with you? What would need to shift in your intention to align with the lesson’s truth?

If a simple camel was called 'the camel of Allah' out of honor, what does that tell you about how Allah elevates humble things? Is there a person or situation in your life you dismiss as lowly? How can you see them through the lens of Allah’s honoring attribute today?

Maturidi's Interpretation of Istiwa: Majesty Over Location
Maturidi argues that istiwa denotes majesty, not physical elevation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi interprets the Quranic phrase 'al-Rahmanu 'ala al-'arshi istawa' as an expression of God's majesty and greatness rather than a physical sitting or rising.

Core Idea
Physical elevation does not signify true greatness; istiwa points to God's majesty, not location.

Maturidi argues that interpreting istiwa as physical rising or sitting would be anthropomorphic and diminish God's transcendence. Instead, he explains that the Throne is magnified through the verse to highlight God's authority, just as great matters are attributed to the greatest objects in language. This lesson unpacks his rational and linguistic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of Maturidi's argument. Then read the English translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the structure: first a rejection of physical interpretation, then two positive reasons for the Throne's mention.
2
Understand the argument against physical elevation
Maturidi argues that rising to a high place does not confer honor or greatness when essences are equal. This refutes any literal interpretation of 'istiwa' as sitting or rising.
This is a rational argument: height alone doesn't make something great.
3
Examine the two reasons for attributing istiwa to the Throne
Maturidi gives two aspects: (1) to magnify the Throne because it appears after mention of God's dominion, and (2) to specify the Throne as the greatest creation, following linguistic custom of attributing great matters to the greatest objects.
The Throne is compared to a king's throne in language, not literal sitting.
4
Reflect on the analogies from language and Quran
Maturidi uses examples: a king establishing himself over a land, the completion of religion with the conquest of Mecca, and sending messengers to Pharaohs and Mecca. These show highlighting greatness, not literal location.
The key point: 'istiwa' in these contexts conveys authority, not physical position.
5
Connect to the key takeaway
The Throne is a created object mentioned to convey God's grandeur, not a place for Him. This safeguards divine transcendence.
This interpretation avoids anthropomorphism and aligns with Quranic language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه ليس في الارتفاع إلى ما يعلو من المكان للجلوس أو القيام شرف ولا علوّ ولا وصف بالعظمة والكبرياء، كمن يعلو السطوح أو الجبال، إنه لا يستحق الرفعة على من دونه عند استواء الجوهر. فلا يجوز صرف تأويل الآية إليه، فيما فيها ذكر العظمة والجلال . إذ ذكر في قوله تعالى : وإن ربكم الله الذي خلق السموات والأرض الآية؛ فذلك على تعظيم العرش، أي [هو] شيء كان من نور أو جوهر لا يبلغه علم الخلق. وقد روى عن نبي الله أنه وصف الشمس : إن جبريل يأتيها بكف من ضوء العرش فيلبسها كما يلبس أحدكم قميصه كل يوم تطلع . وذكر في القمر «كفاً من نور العرش فإضافة الاستواء إليه / لوجهين أحدهما على تعظيمه، بما ذكره على إثر ذكره سلطانه في ربوبيته وخلقه ما ذكر . والثاني على تخصيصه بالذكر بما هو أعظم الخلق وأجله ؛ على المعروف من إضافة الأمور العظيمة إلى أعظم الأشياء ؛ كما يقال : تم لفلان ملك بلد كذا واستوى على موضع كذا، لا على خصوص ذلك في الحق، ولكن معلوم أن من له ملك ذلك فما دونه أحق. وعلى ذلك قوله تعالى : اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم الآية، بما صارت له أم القرى وأيس الذين كفروا من دينه . وكذا ما ذكر من إرسال الرسل إلى الفراعنة وإلى أم القرى لا يتخصص ذلك ولكن يذكر عظم الأمر ، فمثله أمر العرش.
Afterwards, there is no honor or highness in rising to a high place for sitting or standing, nor description of majesty and greatness, like one who ascends roofs or mountains; he does not deserve elevation over those below him when the essence is equal. So it is not permissible to interpret the verse according to that, in what it contains of mention of majesty and grandeur. For [He] mentioned in His saying 'Indeed your Lord is God who created the heavens and the earth' the verse; that is for the magnification of the Throne, meaning it is something of light or essence that the knowledge of creatures does not reach. And it has been narrated from the Prophet of God that he described the sun: 'Gabriel brings it a handful of light from the Throne and clothes it as one of you clothes his shirt every day it rises.' And he mentioned regarding the moon 'a handful of light from the Throne.' So the attribution of istiwa to it is for two aspects: one is for its magnification, by what He mentioned after His mention of His dominion in His lordship and His creation as mentioned. And the second is for specification by mentioning that which is greatest and most magnificent of creation, based on the known custom of attributing great matters to the greatest of things, as it is said: 'So-and-so's kingdom of such-and-such a land is completed and he established himself upon such-and-such a place' – not by the specificity of that in reality, but it is known that whoever has dominion over that, then what is below it is more fitting. And similarly His saying: 'Today I have perfected for you your religion' the verse, because the Mother of Cities became his, and those who disbelieved despaired of his religion. And likewise what was mentioned of sending messengers to the Pharaohs and to the Mother of Cities – that does not specify, but it mentions the greatness of the matter. So similar to that is the matter of the Throne.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of the verse of istiwa (the Throne) as indicating majesty and greatness, not physical location. Maturidi first argues that physical elevation to a high place does not confer true honor or greatness; rather, it is the inherent nature that matters. He then states that the verse mentioning the Throne is meant to magnify the Throne, which is a created thing of light or essence beyond human knowledge. He cites a hadith about the sun and moon being clothed with light from the Throne. The attribution of istiwa to the Throne has two aspects: (1) to magnify the Throne because it follows mention of God's dominion, and (2) to specify the Throne as the greatest of created things, similar to how great matters are attributed to the greatest objects, like saying a king established himself over a place to emphasize his dominion, not that he is physically located there. He gives examples from the Quran, such as the completion of religion and the despair of disbelievers being tied to the conquest of Mecca (Umm al-Qura), and the sending of messengers to Pharaohs and to Mecca – all to highlight the greatness of these events. Similarly, the Throne is mentioned to convey God's greatness, not a physical position.

Key Terms
استواء (istiwa)
عرش (Throne)
عظمة (majesty)
جلال (grandeur)
تأويل (interpretation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the word 'istiwa' literally means sitting or rising physically, implying God occupies a location.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'istiwa' denotes power and majesty, not physical placement. The Throne is mentioned to magnify God's authority, not to indicate a spatial relationship.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the Throne specifically mentioned in the verse of istiwa?
A) Because God sits on it.
B) Because it is the largest physical object in the universe.
C) To magnify the Throne and highlight God's greatness.
D) To indicate that God needs a place to rest.

Key Takeaway
Istiwa in reference to God's Throne signifies His supreme majesty and authority, not a physical location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously imagined God as sitting on a throne in the sky? Maturidi shows that such mental pictures actually diminish God's majesty. Will you commit to purifying your concept of God from any spatial or bodily images, starting with your next prayer?
?
The lesson teaches that true greatness is not about physical height or position. Look at your own ambitions—are you chasing status, recognition, or material elevation? How can you redirect that energy to seeking nearness to God through humility and submission?
?
Maturidi uses the example of a king's dominion to explain that 'istiwa' implies authority, not location. Is there any area of your life where you act as if God's authority is distant or irrelevant (e.g., your finances, relationships, or daily choices)? What one concrete change can you make today to affirm His absolute sovereignty over that area?

Understanding Istawa and Arsh as Sovereignty
Maturidi interprets 'istawa' and 'arsh' using linguistic evidence and poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses linguistic evidence and poetry to interpret the Qur'anic terms 'istawa' and 'arsh' as referring to God's absolute sovereignty.

Core Idea
In Arabic, 'arsh' can mean 'kingdom' — God's 'istawa' means His sovereign control.

Maturidi anchors his interpretation of divine attributes in the Arabic language itself. Before accepting anthropomorphic readings of 'istawa' and 'arsh', he shows from pre-Islamic poetry that these words carry the meaning of sovereignty and dominion. In this passage, you'll see how a theologian uses linguistic evidence to clarify God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the poetic lines – they are key evidence.
2
Notice the interpretation of 'istawa'
Maturidi begins by explaining that 'istawa ila al-sama'' means 'created', analogous to a deliberate act. This sets the stage for a non-literal reading.
3
Examine the three poetic examples
Each poet uses 'arsh' in contexts of power, loss of kingdom, or dominion. Identify how each example shows that 'arsh' can mean 'kingdom' or 'sovereignty'.
The first poet speaks of thrones being demolished, the second of thrones perishing, the third directly mentions 'kingdom' alongside 'throne'.
4
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that 'istawa 'ala al-arsh' means God's absolute possession and sovereignty over all creation. He supports this with the Qur'anic phrase 'Lord of the Mighty Throne' meaning 'Mighty Kingdom'.
Note also the implication that other thrones exist (e.g., of angels), which fits the metaphorical reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استوى إلى السماء بمعنى خلق، على التمثيل بفعل الخلق فيما يتلو فعلهم فعل أن يكون بالقصد، وإن كان لا يقال له قصد. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقال الشاعر: ظننت أن عرشك لا يزول ولا يغير. إذا ما بنو مروان ثلت عروشهم وأؤذوا كما أودت إياد وحمير. وقال النابغة: عروش تفانوا بعد عز وأنهم هووا بعد ما نالوا السلامة والغنى. وقال آخر: الملك. عرشه والحاربين تؤملون فلاحا؟ بعد ابن جفنة وابن ماثل عر. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم الوجه في ذلك - لو كان على الاستيلاء، والعرش هو أنه مستول على جميع خلقه؛ وعلى هذا التأويل المحمول على غير هذا؛ يدل على الأمرين قوله: وهو رب العرش العظيم بمعنى الملك العظيم، وفيه إثبات عروش غيره؛ فذلك يحتمل ما يحمل ويحف به الملائكة. والله الموفق.
He established Himself to the heaven with the meaning of 'created', in the manner of the act of creation in what follows their act, an act that is by intention, even if it is not called intention. And there is no power except with Allah. And the poet said: I thought that your throne (arsh) does not disappear nor change. When the sons of Marwan, their thrones were demolished and they were harmed as the tribes of Iyad and Himyar perished. And al-Nabigha said: Thrones they perished after might, and they fell after attaining safety and wealth. And another said: The kingdom, its throne, and the warriors do you hope for success? After Ibn Jafna and Ibn Mithal, thrones. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then the face of the matter is that if it were upon possession, the throne is that He is the possessor of all His creation; and upon this interpretation carried upon other than this; it indicates both matters by His saying: 'And He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne' meaning the Mighty Kingdom, and in it is the affirmation of other thrones; so that bears what is borne and the angels surround it. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a theological and linguistic analysis by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi regarding the meaning of 'istawa' (He established Himself) and 'arsh' (throne) in the Qur'an. Maturidi begins by noting that 'istawa ila al-sama'' (He directed Himself to the heaven) can have the meaning of 'creation' (khalq), analogizing it to a deliberate act. He then presents poetic evidence from pre-Islamic poets to show that 'arsh' can mean 'kingdom' or 'sovereignty', not a physical throne. The first poet uses the metaphor of a throne being overturned to describe loss of power. The second, al-Nabigha, describes thrones perishing, again referring to authority. The third poet mentions 'al-mulk' (kingdom) in conjunction with 'arsh'. Maturidi then gives his conclusion: the correct interpretation is that God's 'istawa' upon the 'arsh' means His absolute sovereignty and possession of all creation. He supports this by citing the Qur'anic verse 'Lord of the Mighty Throne' interpreting it as 'Mighty Kingdom'. He also notes that the verse implies the existence of other thrones (e.g., the thrones of angels or created beings), which fits the metaphorical reading. The passage ends with an invocation for divine success.

Key Terms
استوى (istawa)
العرش (al-arsh)
الملك (al-mulk)
الاستيلاء (al-istila')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some mistakenly take 'istawa' and 'arsh' literally as physical sitting or a chair.
Correction: Maturidi shows from Arabic usage that they signify sovereignty.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does 'arsh' in the Qur'anic verse 'istawa 'ala al-arsh' primarily mean?
A) A physical throne
B) The kingdom and sovereignty
C) The sky
D) The angels

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves through Arabic poetry that 'arsh' means kingdom, so God's 'istawa' upon the 'arsh' is His absolute sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly submit to Allah's absolute sovereignty over every detail of your life, including your failures and losses, or do you still cling to the illusion that your own 'throne' can stand? What would it look like to surrender that area to Him today?
?
When you encounter verses about Allah's attributes, do you examine the linguistic and contextual evidence like Maturidi did, or do you accept interpretations without question? What is one belief about Allah that you should verify from the sources right now?
?
If you really believed that every power and authority belongs only to Allah, how would that change the way you react to injustice or disappointment from people? Will you let this truth reshape your heart starting now?
