The Meaning of Tamam and 'Uluww in Creation for Humanity
Maturidi explains that God's completion and elevation in creation are for the sake of humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's interpretation that the completion and elevation of creation are realized through the creation of humans as the intended purpose and beneficiaries of the universe.

Core Idea
Creation's completion and elevation are achieved through and for human beings.

Maturidi delves into the deeper meaning of the Qur'anic verses describing creation in six days and God's 'istawa'. He argues that the 'completion' (tamam) and 'elevation' (uluww) of the heavens and earth are not merely sequential events but are intrinsically tied to the creation of humanity. This passage unpacks that relationship, showing how humans are the intended center of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Familiarize yourself with the key terms tamam and uluww.
Notice the Qur'anic citations embedded in the argument.
2
Trace Maturidi's argument
Follow Maturidi's reasoning: from the six-day creation to the meaning of istawa, to the role of humans as al-mumtahan (the tested one).
Pay attention to how each quoted verse builds the case.
3
Connect to Ibn Abbas' report
Consider the report that humans were created on the seventh day. How does that complete the meaning of tamam?
This ties the purpose of creation directly to humanity.
4
Synthesize the purpose
Summarize Maturidi's conclusion that all creation is subjected to humans for worship.
Reflect on the theological implications for human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على التمام والعلو فهو أن الله تعالى قال: قل أئنكم لتكفرون بالذي خلق الأرض في يومين إلى قوله: فقضاهن سبع سموات، فأخبر بخلق ما ذكر في ستة أيام على التفاريق، ثم أجملها في موضع فقال: إن ربكم الله الذي خلق السموات والأرض في ستة أيام ثم استوى على العرش لمعنى خلق الممتحن من خلق الأرض والسموات، فبهم ظهر تمام الملك وعلا وارتفع، إذ هم المقصودون من خلق ما بينا، فبذلك تم معنى وعلا إذا وصل إلى الذين لهم. وقد قيل هذا في خلق البشر خاصة، بقوله: هو الذي خلق لكم ما في الأرض الآية، وقوله: وسخر لكم الليل والنهار، وقوله: وسخر لكم ما في السموات وما في الأرض الآية. وذكر ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما أن البشر خلق اليوم السابع. فيه التمام والعلو؛ إذ خلق لهم كل شيء، وهم لعبادة الله ولحق بهم الجن بقوله: وما خلقت الجن والإنس إلا ليعبدون؛ لكن المقصود البشر، إذ تسخير ما ذكرت كله لهم، ثم بما يرجع إلى منافعهم. والله الموفق.
And as for completion and elevation, it is that Allah Most High said: 'Say, Do you indeed disbelieve in He who created the earth in two days' until His saying: 'And He completed them as seven heavens.' So He informed of the creation of what was mentioned in six days in parts, then He summarized it in one place and said: 'Indeed, your Lord is Allah who created the heavens and the earth in six days, then He established Himself upon the Throne' for the meaning of creating the tested one from the creation of the earth and the heavens, for through them the completion of the kingdom appeared and it rose and elevated, since they are the intended ones from the creation of what we have explained. So by that the meaning of completion and elevation was achieved when it reached those to whom it belongs. And it has been said that this is specifically in the creation of humans, by His saying: 'He is the one who created for you all that is on the earth' and His saying: 'And He subjected to you the night and day' and His saying: 'And He subjected to you what is in the heavens and what is on the earth.' And Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, mentioned that humans were created on the seventh day. In it is completion and elevation, since He created everything for them, and they are for the worship of Allah, and the jinn are joined to them by His saying: 'And I did not create the jinn and humans except to worship Me'; but the intended are humans, since the subjugation of all that I mentioned is for them, then what returns to their benefits. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is an explanation by Maturidi of the meaning of 'tamam' (completion) and 'uluww' (elevation) in the context of the creation verses. Maturidi argues that the creation of the heavens and earth in six days, culminating in the creation of humans, demonstrates God's complete and elevated sovereignty. He quotes the Qur'anic verses from Surah Fussilat and Surah al-A'raf which mention creation in six days and then 'istawa 'ala al-arsh'. He explains that the phrase 'istawa 'ala al-arsh' here has the meaning of completing the creation for the sake of the human being, who is the one tested (al-mumtahan). Through humans, the kingdom's completion is manifested. Maturidi then cites Ibn Abbas's report that humans were created on the seventh day, implying that the creation of the universe was not complete until humans were created, as everything else was created for their benefit. He reinforces this with Qur'anic verses stating that all things on earth and in the heavens are subjected to humans and that the purpose of creation of jinn and humans is worship. However, the primary intended are humans, as everything is subjugated to them. The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms
التمام (al-tamam)
العلو (al-uluww)
الممتحن (al-mumtahan)
العبادة (al-ibada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's 'istawa' in the six-day creation verse is a separate event unrelated to humans.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'istawa' here indicates completion of creation for the sake of humans, who are the purpose of creation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, in what sense does the creation of the heavens and earth achieve 'tamam' and 'uluww'?
A) By being created in six days exactly
B) By being created for the sake of humans, the tested ones
C) By the angels surrounding the throne
D) By the earth being created before the heavens

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that creation's completion and elevation are realized through human creation, as everything is subjected for human worship of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are the one for whom Allah completed creation and elevated it. Do you live as though everything exists for your purpose of worshipping Him? What specific change can you make today to align your life with that purpose?
?
Maturidi says through you the completion of the kingdom appears. In what area of your daily routine do you fail to reflect that dignity? Will you commit to practicing mindfulness of Allah in that area starting now?
?
Creation is subjected for you – night, day, heavens, earth. Are you using these gifts for anything other than the worship they are meant to facilitate? Identify one creation you misuse and decide how to repurpose it for Allah's obedience today.

God's Nearness: A Multifaceted Concept
Maturidi enumerates five modes of divine nearness with scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine nearness in the Quran and Sunnah is expressed through multiple distinct modes, such as responding to prayer, supporting the righteous, and preserving creation.

Core Idea
God's nearness is not spatial but has many meanings.

Imam Maturidi, in this passage from Kitab al-Tawhid, clarifies a key principle: when the Quran and hadith speak of God being 'near,' they do not mean physical proximity. Instead, they refer to various modes of divine attention and care. Let's explore these modes as outlined by Maturidi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation, noting how each mode is accompanied by a Quranic verse.
Maturidi's style is to state each mode and then cite evidence.
2
Identify the five modes
List out each mode of nearness Maturidi mentions: 1) by responding, 2) by victory and assistance, 3) by drawing near in rank, 4) by preservation, 5) by knowledge.
See the Quranic verses he cites for each.
3
Reflect on the evidence
For each mode, consider how the cited verse illustrates that aspect of God's nearness. For example, Quran 2:186 shows nearness in answering prayer.
4
Understand the purpose
Ask yourself why Maturidi lists these modes. The answer is to prevent literal interpretations of divine nearness as spatial or physical.
This connects to the broader theme of interpreting ambiguous verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا فيما تقدم وصف قربه وذلك بالإجابة، كقوله تعالى: وإذا سألك عبادي عني فإني قريب الآية، وبالنصر والمعونة، كقوله تعالى: إن الله مع الذين اتقوا والذين هم محسنون [النحل، ١٢٨/١٦]، وبالتقرب إلى المنزلة والمحل، كقوله تعالى: واسجد واقترب [العلق، ١٩/٩٦]، وما روي: أن من تقرب إلى شبرا تقربت إليه ذراعا، إلى آخر ذلك، وقوله: وابتغوا إليه الوسيلة [المائدة، ٣٥/٥]؛ وفي الكلاءة والحفظ، كقوله: وربك على كل شيء حفيظ، وهو على كل شيء وكيل، وقوله: أفمن هو قائم على كل نفس بما كسبت [الرعد، ٣٣/١٣]، وبالعلم، بقوله: يعلم سركم وجهركم [الأنعام، ٣/٦]، وغير ذلك. فعلى مثل بعض هذه الوجوه المجيئ والذهاب والقعود. مع ما كان مجيء الأجسام
And we have already explained previously the description of His nearness, and that is by responding, as His saying (Exalted is He): 'And when My servants ask you about Me, then indeed I am near' (Quran 2:186), and by victory and assistance, as His saying: 'Indeed, Allah is with those who fear Him and those who are doers of good' (Quran 16:128), and by drawing near in rank and station, as His saying: 'Prostrate and draw near' (Quran 96:19), and what is narrated: 'Whoever draws near to Me by a handspan, I draw near to him by an armspan,' etc., and His saying: 'And seek a means of nearness to Him' (Quran 5:35); and in preservation and guardianship, as His saying: 'And your Lord is over all things a Guardian' (Quran 34:21), and He is over all things a Disposer of affairs, and His saying: 'Then is He who is standing over every soul for what it has earned' (Quran 13:33), and by knowledge, by His saying: 'He knows your secret and your public' (Quran 6:3), and other than that. So in some of these senses are coming, going, and sitting. Even though the coming of bodies...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of God's nearness in various modes and an interpretation of anthropomorphic terms. Maturidi states that he has already described God's nearness, and here he enumerates its types: (1) Nearness in answering prayer, citing Quran 2:186. (2) Nearness in victory and support, citing Quran 16:128. (3) Nearness in rank and station, citing Quran 96:19 and the hadith about drawing near, as well as Quran 5:35. (4) Nearness in preservation and guardianship, citing Quran 34:21 and Quran 13:33. (5) Nearness in knowledge, citing Quran 6:3. Then he concludes that the scriptural terms like 'coming,' 'going,' and 'sitting' are to be understood in these senses, not as physical movements. The text ends abruptly with 'even though the coming of bodies,' suggesting a contrast between God's attributes and physical movement.

Key Terms
الإجابة (responding)
النصر (victory)
التقرب (drawing near)
الحفظ (preservation)
العلم (knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's nearness means He is physically close in space.
Correction: Maturidi shows that nearness has many meanings such as responding, support, and knowledge, not physical proximity.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned by Maturidi as a mode of God's nearness?
A) Nearness in answering prayer
B) Nearness in support
C) Nearness in time
D) Nearness in knowledge

Key Takeaway
God's nearness is expressed through multiple modes: responding, supporting, elevating, preserving, and knowing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which of these five modes of nearness do you most need to experience God's presence today? Will you specifically ask Him for that form of nearness in your next prayer?
?
When have you felt spiritually distant from God? Which mode of nearness (responding, victory, rank, preservation, or knowledge) does your current situation call you to actively seek?
?
If you truly believed that God is 'standing over every soul for what it has earned' (Quran 13:33), what habit or thought would you be compelled to change immediately?

Interpreting Anthropomorphic Terms: Coming, Going, and Sitting
Maturidi applies the modes of nearness to interpret anthropomorphic terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that anthropomorphic terms like 'coming', 'going', and 'sitting' in the Quran and hadith are to be interpreted in the sense of divine actions, not physical movements.

Core Idea
Words like 'coming' and 'going' refer to divine acts, not bodily motion.

Maturidi has just explained the various modes of divine nearness. Now he applies the same principle to specific anthropomorphic expressions: 'coming', 'going', and 'sitting'. These are not to be taken literally, but understood in terms of God's actions such as responding, supporting, and so on. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the contrast Maturidi draws with the 'coming of bodies'.
The text ends abruptly, but the point is clear.
2
Connect to the previous modes
Recall the five modes of nearness. Maturidi says that these terms ('coming', 'going', 'sitting') are to be understood in some of those senses.
For example, 'coming' might mean responding to prayer or granting victory.
3
Understand the contrast
Maturidi mentions 'even though the coming of bodies' to highlight that God's coming is not like physical movement. This affirms a metaphorical interpretation.
This guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا فيما تقدم وصف قربه وذلك بالإجابة، كقوله تعالى: وإذا سألك عبادي عني فإني قريب الآية، وبالنصر والمعونة، كقوله تعالى: إن الله مع الذين اتقوا والذين هم محسنون [النحل، ١٢٨/١٦]، وبالتقرب إلى المنزلة والمحل، كقوله تعالى: واسجد واقترب [العلق، ١٩/٩٦]، وما روي: أن من تقرب إلى شبرا تقربت إليه ذراعا، إلى آخر ذلك، وقوله: وابتغوا إليه الوسيلة [المائدة، ٣٥/٥]؛ وفي الكلاءة والحفظ، كقوله: وربك على كل شيء حفيظ، وهو على كل شيء وكيل، وقوله: أفمن هو قائم على كل نفس بما كسبت [الرعد، ٣٣/١٣]، وبالعلم، بقوله: يعلم سركم وجهركم [الأنعام، ٣/٦]، وغير ذلك. فعلى مثل بعض هذه الوجوه المجيئ والذهاب والقعود. مع ما كان مجيء الأجسام
And we have already explained previously the description of His nearness, and that is by responding, as His saying (Exalted is He): 'And when My servants ask you about Me, then indeed I am near' (Quran 2:186), and by victory and assistance, as His saying: 'Indeed, Allah is with those who fear Him and those who are doers of good' (Quran 16:128), and by drawing near in rank and station, as His saying: 'Prostrate and draw near' (Quran 96:19), and what is narrated: 'Whoever draws near to Me by a handspan, I draw near to him by an armspan,' etc., and His saying: 'And seek a means of nearness to Him' (Quran 5:35); and in preservation and guardianship, as His saying: 'And your Lord is over all things a Guardian' (Quran 34:21), and He is over all things a Disposer of affairs, and His saying: 'Then is He who is standing over every soul for what it has earned' (Quran 13:33), and by knowledge, by His saying: 'He knows your secret and your public' (Quran 6:3), and other than that. So in some of these senses are coming, going, and sitting. Even though the coming of bodies...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of God's nearness in various modes and an interpretation of anthropomorphic terms. Maturidi states that he has already described God's nearness, and here he enumerates its types: (1) Nearness in answering prayer, citing Quran 2:186. (2) Nearness in victory and support, citing Quran 16:128. (3) Nearness in rank and station, citing Quran 96:19 and the hadith about drawing near, as well as Quran 5:35. (4) Nearness in preservation and guardianship, citing Quran 34:21 and Quran 13:33. (5) Nearness in knowledge, citing Quran 6:3. Then he concludes that the scriptural terms like 'coming,' 'going,' and 'sitting' are to be understood in these senses, not as physical movements. The text ends abruptly with 'even though the coming of bodies,' suggesting a contrast between God's attributes and physical movement.

الذهاب (going)
القعود (sitting)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some people believe that God literally comes, goes, and sits like a human.
Correction: Maturidi explains that these terms refer to divine actions such as responding or supporting, not physical movements.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how should we understand terms like 'coming' and 'going' applied to God?
A) As literal physical movements
B) As metaphors for divine actions
C) As purely symbolic with no meaning
D) As describing His essence

Key Takeaway
Anthropomorphic terms are understood in the sense of divine actions, not physical motion.

?
Think of a moment when you felt God was 'near' or 'far'. Did you imagine a spatial distance? Starting today, how will you replace that mental picture with the awareness that nearness to God is through obedience and knowledge, not location?

Metaphorical Interpretation of God's Coming and Going
Maturidi explains the metaphorical meanings of divine attributes of movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi interprets divine attributes of movement (coming, going, sitting) as metaphorical expressions of manifestation and annihilation, not literal physical motion.

Core Idea
God's coming is not movement but manifestation of His power.

Maturidi has already explained how God's nearness is understood through responses, aid, and protection. Now he applies the same principle to the attributes of coming and going. By distinguishing between the coming of bodies (physical transition) and the coming of al-Haqq (manifestation), he preserves divine transcendence. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Recall the earlier discussion of nearness
Before diving into coming/going, Maturidi reminds us of how divine nearness is understood metaphorically: through response, victory, rank, protection, and knowledge. This sets the template for interpreting other attributes.
Notice the variety of meanings: nearness does not imply physical proximity.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'coming'
Maturidi contrasts the coming of bodies (which implies spatial transition) with the coming of al-Haqq (which implies manifestation, as in 'Say: The truth has come'). This is the key distinction.
The same term carries different meanings depending on the subject.
3
Apply the same to 'going'
Similarly, the going of falsehood means its annihilation, while the going of a body means its physical transition. This parallelism reinforces the metaphorical interpretation.
The pattern is consistent: physical entities undergo change, but for God the terms signify something else.
4
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that Allah is exalted above both meanings (physical movement and change), so we must not attribute to Him the meanings that apply to created bodies. This guards against anthropomorphism.
The final sentence 'And there is no power except through Allah' is a humble acknowledgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا فيما تقدم وصف قربه وذلك بالإجابة، كقوله تعالى: وإذا سألك عبادي عني فإني قريب الآية، وبالنصر والمعونة، كقوله تعالى: إن الله مع الذين اتقوا والذين هم محسنون [النحل، 128]، وبالتقرب إلى المنزلة والمحل، كقوله تعالى: واسجد واقترب [العلق، 19]، وما روي: أن من تقرب إلي شبرا تقربت إليه ذراعا، إلى آخر ذلك، وقوله: وابتغوا إليه الوسيلة [المائدة، 35]، وفي الكلاءة والحفظ، كقوله: وربك على كل شيء حفيظ وهو على كل شيء وكيل، وقوله: أفمن هو قائم على كل نفس بما كسبت [الرعد، 33]، وبالعلم بقوله: يعلم سركم وجهركم [الأنعام، 3]، وغير ذلك. فعلى مثل بعض هذه الوجوه المجيئ والذهاب والقعود. مع ما كان مجيء الأجسام يفهم منه الانتقال؛ ثم مجيئ الحق يفهم منه الظهور، كقوله: قل جاء الحق؛ وعلى ذلك ذهاب الباطل بطلانه؛ وذهاب الجسم انتقاله. فهذا محل المجيئ والذهاب في المعروف من الأعراض والأجسام. والله يتعالى عن المعنيين جميعا، لم يجز أن يفهم من المضاف إليه ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And we have already explained previously the description of His nearness, and that is through response, as His saying: 'And when My servants ask you concerning Me, then indeed I am near' [al-Baqarah 186], and through victory and aid, as His saying: 'Indeed Allah is with those who fear Him and those who are doers of good' [al-Nahl 128], and through drawing near in rank and station, as His saying: 'Prostrate and draw near' [al-`Alaq 19], and what was narrated: 'Whoever draws near to Me by a span, I draw near to him by a cubit,' etc., and His saying: 'And seek means of approach to Him' [al-Ma'idah 35], and in protection and preservation, as His saying: 'And your Lord is over all things a Guardian, and He is over all things a Disposer of affairs' [Saba' 21?], and His saying: 'Then is He who is standing over every soul for what it has earned' [al-Ra`d 33], and through knowledge, by His saying: 'He knows your secret and your public' [al-An`am 3], and other than that. So upon the model of some of these aspects are the coming, the going, and the sitting. While the coming of bodies implies transition; then the coming of al-Haqq (the Truth) implies manifestation, as His saying: 'Say: The truth has come' [al-Isra' 81]; and upon that, the going of falsehood is its annihilation; and the going of a body is its transition. So this is the place of coming and going in what is known of accidents and bodies. And Allah is exalted above both meanings; it is not permissible that what is added to Him be understood as that. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how to understand the divine attributes of coming (maji'), going (dhahāb), and sitting (qu'ūd) that appear in the Qur'an and hadith. Maturidi begins by recalling his earlier discussion of God's nearness (qurb), which is understood through various modalities: response to prayer, victory and aid, drawing near in rank, protection, knowledge, etc. These are all ways in which God's nearness is manifested without implying physical proximity. Then he applies the same principle to the attributes of coming and going. He distinguishes between the coming of bodies, which implies spatial movement (intiqāl), and the coming of al-Haqq (God), which implies manifestation (zuhūr) — as in the verse 'Say: The truth has come and falsehood has vanished' (Q. 17:81). Similarly, the going of falsehood means its annihilation (buṭlān), while the going of a body means its physical transition (intiqāl). Thus, the literal sense of these terms as used for physical objects does not apply to God. Rather, they are understood metaphorically in a way befitting His transcendence. Maturidi concludes that Allah is exalted above both meanings (physical movement and physical change), and it is not permissible to attribute to Him the meanings that apply to created bodies. The passage guards against anthropomorphism (tashbīh) by affirming that the scriptural expressions are to be interpreted in a manner consistent with divine transcendence.

Correction: Maturidi explains that divine coming implies manifestation (e.g., the truth coming) and divine going implies annihilation (e.g., falsehood vanishing), not physical motion.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes of movement must be understood metaphorically to preserve God's transcendence, as exemplified by the distinction between physical transition and divine manifestation.

?
You claim that God does not move like bodies, but do you still catch yourself imagining Him in human terms when you pray? The next time you say 'Allahu Akbar,' commit to consciously stripping away any mental image of Him coming or going. Will you actually do that, or will you remain comfortable with inadequate thoughts of your Creator?
?
The passage shows that even falsehood's 'going' is its annihilation—a lesson in how opposites vanish. You have false beliefs or habits that you have not annihilated. Two Proofs for the Possibility of Seeing God
Maturidi enumerates two Qur'anic proofs for the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two Qur'anic proofs that Allah can be seen in the Hereafter while remaining beyond full comprehension.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is possible; encompassing Him is not.

Maturidi presents two clear scriptural arguments that establish the possibility of seeing Allah without comprehension. The first from the verse negating idrāk, the second from Moses' request. This lesson unpacks both proofs and shows how they affirm vision while denying full grasp.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi states his position first, then gives two proofs.
The passage uses very concise argumentation.
2
Understand Maturidi's position
Maturidi affirms that belief in seeing Allah is necessary and true, but without comprehension (idrāk) or interpretation (tafsīr). Make sure you grasp this distinction.
This is the foundational claim.
3
Analyze the first proof
The verse 'No vision can grasp Him' (6:103) is used. Maturidi argues that if Allah were not seen at all, negating comprehension would be pointless. Since created things are comprehended only after being seen, the negation implies vision is possible.
Focus on the logic of negation.
4
Analyze the second proof
Moses asked to see Allah (7:143). A prophet would not ask for something impossible. This shows vision is possible in principle, even if not in this world.
The request itself proves possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ رؤية الله ] قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : القول في رؤية الرب عز وجل عندنا لازم وحق من غير إدراك ولا تفسير. فأما الدليل على الرؤية: 1 - فقوله تعالى : لا تدركه الأبصار وهو يدرك الأبصار [الأنعام، 103]؛ ولو كان لا يرى لم يكن لنفي الإدراك حكمة، إذ لا يدرك غيره بغير رؤية. فموضع نفي الإدراك وغيره من الخلق لا يدرك إلا بالرؤية لا معنى له. وبالله التوفيق. 2 - والثاني قول موسى عليه السلام: رب أرني أنظر إليك [الأعراف، 143]؛ ولو كان لا يرى لما سأل.
Question: [Vision of Allah]. Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The statement concerning the vision of the Lord, Almighty and Majestic, is for us necessary and true, without comprehension and without interpretation. As for the proof for vision: 1 - His saying, Exalted is He: 'No vision can grasp Him, but He grasps all vision' [al-An'am 103]; and if He were not seen, the negation of comprehension would have no wisdom, since nothing other than Him is comprehended except by vision. So the place of the negation of comprehension—while other created beings are not comprehended except by vision—has no meaning. And success is from Allah. 2 - And the second is the saying of Moses, peace be upon him: 'Lord, show me that I may look at You' [al-A'raf 143]; and if He were not seen, he would not have asked.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'yat Allāh) in the Hereafter, a key Ash'ari-Maturidi doctrine. Maturidi first states his position: the belief in seeing Allah is necessary (lāzim) and true (ḥaqq), but without comprehension (idrāk) and without interpretation (tafsīr). This means that believers will see Allah with their eyes, but without encompassing or grasping Him as one comprehends a physical object. The first proof is from the Qur'an: 'No vision can grasp Him, but He grasps all vision' (Q. 6:103). Maturidi argues that if Allah were not seen at all, then negating comprehension (idrāk) would be pointless, because in the case of unseen things we do not even speak of 'comprehension'. The verse specifically negates idrāk, not ru'ya. Since created things can only be comprehended after being seen, the negation of idrāk makes sense only if vision is possible — otherwise the negation would apply to something impossible, which would be meaningless. The second proof is from Moses' request: 'Lord, show me that I may look at You' (Q. 7:143). Moses, a prophet, knew that Allah cannot be seen in this world (as the later verse 'You will not see Me' shows), but his request indicates that vision of Allah is possible in principle. If it were impossible, a prophet would not ask for it. Thus both proofs establish that Allah can be seen while affirming that He is not comprehended. The passage guards against two extremes: denying vision outright (as Mu'tazila do) and claiming comprehension (as anthropomorphists might).

Key Terms
رؤية الله
إدراك
تفسير
لا تدركه الأبصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'No vision can grasp Him' (Q. 6:103) denies all vision of Allah.
Correction: Maturidi explains that the verse denies comprehension (idrāk), not vision (ru'ya). If vision were impossible, the negation of comprehension would be meaningless, since we only speak of comprehension for things that can be seen.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the purpose of the verse 'No vision can grasp Him'?
A) To negate the possibility of seeing God
B) To affirm that God is seen but not encompassed
C) To deny both vision and comprehension
D) To compare God to created beings

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses two Qur'anic proofs—the negation of idrāk and Moses' request—to show that vision of Allah is possible without full comprehension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment, does the possibility of seeing Him fill you with awe or fear? What does your heart answer to the question: 'Do you long to see your Lord?' And what does that longing (or lack of it) reveal about your relationship with Him today?
?
Moses, a mighty prophet, asked to see Allah. Have you ever truly asked Allah to show you something of His reality? Why do you avoid such a request, and what excuse will you give on the Day when you stand before Him and He says, 'I am able to be seen, but you did not ask'?
?
The negation of comprehension protects Allah's transcendence. Yet you claim to know Him. What does your 'knowledge' of Allah actually look like? Is it a sense of comprehension that you secretly cling to, or is it a humble admission that He is beyond your grasp even as you hope to see Him?

The Vision of God: Necessity and First Proof
Maturidi affirms the necessity of discussing God's vision and gives the first proof from Quran 6:103.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's affirmation that discussing God's vision is necessary and true, and his first proof from Quran 6:103.

Core Idea
The vision of God is real but not sensory perception.

Imam Maturidi begins his discussion on the vision of God (ru'ya) by asserting its necessity and truth, but immediately qualifies it as beyond physical perception. He then presents his first proof, a subtle argument from the negation of perception in Quran 6:103.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once and then the translation. Let the author's own words sink in before interpreting.
Notice the density of the argument.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the terms ru'ya (vision), idrak (perception), and tafsir (interpretation). Why does Maturidi specify 'without perception or interpretation'?
These terms are central to the debate.
3
Trace the proof
Follow the argument from the verse to the conclusion. How does negating perception (idrak) imply the possibility of vision (ru'ya)?
The proof hinges on the wisdom (hikma) of the negation.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument preserves both the transcendence of God and the reality of seeing Him in the afterlife.
This guards against both anthropomorphism and denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : القول في رؤية الرب عز وجل عندنا لازم وحق من غير إدراك ولا تفسير. فأما الدليل على الرؤية: فقوله تعالى: لا تدركه الأبصار وهو يدرك الأبصار؛ ولو كان لا يرى لم يكن لنفي الإدراك حكمة، إذ لا يدرك غيره بغير رؤية. فموضع نفي الإدراك ـ وغيره من الخلق لا يدرك إلا بالرؤية ـ لا معنى له. وبالله التوفيق.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The statement concerning the vision of the Lord, Mighty and Majestic, with us is necessary and true, without perception (idrak) and without interpretation (tafsir). As for the proof for the vision: His saying, the Exalted: "Sight does not perceive Him, but He perceives sight" (Quran 6:103); and if He were not seen, there would be no wisdom in negating perception, since other than Him is not perceived without vision. So the place of negating perception—while other created beings are not perceived except by vision—has no meaning. And success is from Allah.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage contains Maturidi's opening statement on the vision of God (ru'ya) and his first proof. He affirms that discoursing on the vision of God is necessary (lazim) and true (haqq), but without perception (idrak) or interpretation (tafsir). The phrase 'without perception' means that the vision of God in the afterlife is not a sensory perception like our seeing physical objects, because God is not limited by space or direction. The first proof is from Quran 6:103: 'Sight does not perceive Him, but He perceives sight.' Maturidi argues that if God were not seeable at all, there would be no point in negating perception (idrak) specifically, because other beings are not perceived except through vision. The negation of perception is meaningful only if it is possible to see God in some sense, but that perception is different from how we perceive physical things. If God were absolutely invisible, the verse would be superfluous. So the verse implies that God can be seen, but not in a way that encompasses Him (idrak implies encompassing). This is a classic Maturidi argument for the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الإدراك
التفسير
الأبصار
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Sight does not perceive Him' means God cannot be seen at all.
Correction: Maturidi shows the negation of perception (idrak) is meaningful only if vision (ru'ya) is possible, but that vision is not encompassing perception.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the verse 'Sight does not perceive Him' implies the possibility of seeing God?
A) Because it negates perception only, not vision
B) Because if God were not seen, negating perception would be pointless
C) Because the verse says 'He perceives sight'
D) Because the verse is in the negative form

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's first proof for the vision of God relies on the logical implication of Quran 6:103: negating perception only makes sense if vision is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that the vision of Allah is 'without perception and without interpretation.' Have you ever allowed your mind to reduce Allah to a concept you can fully grasp, rather than a being you long to see? What would it take for you to move from intellectual assent to heartfelt yearning for that vision?
?
If the negation of perception in the verse is a proof for the vision, then how many other verses in the Quran act as subtle signs pointing you toward a direct encounter with Allah? Are you reading the Quran as a roadmap to that encounter, or merely as a set of rules?
?
The proof depends on the wisdom (hikma) of Allah's speech. Do you live as though every word of the Quran is perfectly wise and personally addressed to you? What would change in your life today if you truly believed that Allah's words are never wasted and always point you to Him?

Quranic Evidence for the Vision of Allah (Ru'ya)
Maturidi interprets verses to prove ru'ya and refutes Mu'tazila interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's Quranic arguments for the vision of Allah in the afterlife and his refutation of the Mu'tazila interpretation of 'nazira' as waiting.

Core Idea
The Qur'an affirms seeing Allah in the afterlife; interpreting it as waiting is linguistically and contextually invalid.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents a powerful defense of the Sunni belief that believers will see Allah in the hereafter. Focusing on key Quranic verses, Maturidi dismantles the Mu'tazila reinterpretation of 'nazira' as waiting, showing through linguistic analysis and theological consistency that the literal meaning of vision is intended. The lesson will guide you through his four main arguments and the parallel with other divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses and Maturidi's arguments
Start by reading the verse from Surat al-Qiyama and then Maturidi's four reasons why it cannot mean waiting. Note how each reason builds on the last.
Pay attention to the use of the preposition 'ila'.
2
Understand the analogy with other attributes
Maturidi compares the vision verse to verses about Allah's speech, action, and istiwa'. He argues that just as we affirm these attributes without similarity, we affirm vision.
This is crucial for avoiding anthropomorphism.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila misinterpretation
Maturidi mentions that the Mu'tazila interpret 'nazira' as waiting. Consider why they do this and how Maturidi's arguments refute them.
The verse 'للذين أحسنوا الحسنى وزيادة' also supports vision.
4
Reflect on the broader theological principle
The passage teaches that metaphorical reinterpretation without evidence is imposing on Allah. The correct method is to affirm what Allah says while negating any similarity to creation.
This is a key principle of Sunni theology.

وأيضا قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ * إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة، ٢٢/٧٥-٢٣]. ثم لا يحتمل ذلك الانتظار لأوجه أحدها أن الآخرة ليست لوقت الانتظار إنما هي الدنيا دار الوقوع والوجود إلا وقت الفزع. وقيل: أن يعاينوا في أنفسهم ما له حق الوقوع. والثاني قوله: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ﴾ وذلك وقوع الثواب. والثالث قوله: ﴿إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ و«إلى» حرف يستعمل في النظر إلى الشيء لا في الانتظار. والرابع أن القول به يخرج مخرج البشارة، وتعظيم ما نالوه من النعم، والانتظار ليس كذلك الصرف عن حقيقة المفهوم قضاء على الله؛ فيلزم القول بالنظر إلى الله كما قال، على نفي جميع معاني الشبه عن الله سبحانه؛ على مثل ما أضيف إليه من الكلام والفعل والقدرة والإرادة، يجب الوصف به على نفي جميع معاني الشبه، وكذلك القول بالهستية. فمن زعم أن الله تعالى لا يقدر أن يكرم أحدا بالرؤية فهو يقدر بالرؤية التي فهمها من الخلق. وإن كان القول بـ ﴿الرَّحْمَـٰنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى﴾ [طه، ٥/٢٠] وغير ذلك من الآيات لا يجب دفعها بالعرض على المفهوم من الخلق، بل يحقق ذلك على نفي الشبه، فمثله خبر الرؤية. والله الموفق. وأيضا قوله تعالى: ﴿لِّلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَىٰ وَزِيَادَةٌ﴾ [يونس، ٢٦/١٠]. وجاء في غير خبر واحد أن المعتزلة فسرت كلمة «ناظرة» بالانتظار، أي انتظار ثواب الله.
And also His statement: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [Al-Qiyama 75:22-23]. Then that waiting is not possible for several reasons: One: the afterlife is not a time of waiting—the world is the abode of occurrence and existence, except the time of terror. And it is said: they witness in themselves what has the right to occur. The second: His statement 'Faces on that day are radiant'—that is the occurrence of reward. The third: His statement 'looking at their Lord'—and 'ila' (to) is a preposition used for looking at a thing, not for waiting. The fourth: that saying it comes as good news and magnification of the blessings they have attained, and waiting is not like that; diverting from the true meaning is imposing on Allah; therefore it is necessary to affirm looking at Allah as He said, negating all meanings of similarity from Allah, like what is added to Him of speech, action, power, and will—these must be described negating all meanings of similarity, and likewise the affirmation of existence. So whoever claims that Allah is unable to honor someone with vision, he estimates vision by what he understands from creation. And if the statement 'The Most Merciful established Himself above the Throne' [Ta-Ha 20:5] and other such verses are not to be rejected by comparing to what is understood from creation, but rather they are affirmed negating similarity, then likewise the report of vision. And Allah is the grantor of success. And also His statement: 'For those who do good is the best, and more' [Yunus 10:26]. And it has come in more than one report that the Mu'tazila interpreted the word 'nazira' as waiting, i.e., waiting for Allah's reward.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the vision of Allah in the afterlife from Quranic verses. Maturidi presents four reasons why interpreting 'nazira' as waiting is invalid: (1) the afterlife is not a time of waiting but of actual occurrence; (2) the word 'nāḍira' (radiant) indicates reward already realized; (3) the preposition 'ila' indicates direction of sight, not waiting; (4) interpreting it as waiting contradicts the joyful announcement of reward. He then likens the verse about ru'ya to other ambiguous attributes like istiwa': they are affirmed without tashbih. Finally, he cites the verse 'al-husna wa ziyada' as another proof, noting that the Mu'tazila misinterpret 'nazira' as waiting, but the correct understanding is actual vision.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'nazira' in the verse means waiting (intizar) for Allah's reward.
Correction: Maturidi argues that 'nazira' means looking/seeing, as indicated by the preposition 'ila' and the context of reward already occurring.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue about the preposition 'ila' in the verse 'ila rabbiha nazirah'?
A) It indicates waiting for reward
B) It is used for looking at a thing, not waiting
C) It is ambiguous and should be ignored
D) It means direction of motion

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an clearly affirms that believers will see Allah in the afterlife, and interpreting such verses metaphorically without evidence is a form of imposing on Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely long to see Allah more than anything else in Paradise, or do your desires focus on other pleasures? What does your daily struggle against sin reveal about your real longing?
?
The Mu'tazila denied ru'ya because they could not imagine it without creaturely likeness. Are there any of Allah's attributes that you struggle to accept because they feel uncomfortable or too transcendent? How will you train yourself to affirm them without tashbih?
?
If you truly believe that one day you will look at your Lord with your own eyes—not metaphorically—how does that change the way you pray, repent, or speak His name today? Are you living as one who is earnestly preparing for that encounter?

Four Reasons Against Waiting Interpretation
Maturidi refutes the waiting interpretation with four reasons and affirms vision while denying anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's fourth argument against interpreting 'nāẓirah' as waiting, including four specific reasons and the principle of affirming attributes while negating anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Interpreting vision as waiting contradicts grammar, context, and divine honor.

Maturidi presents his most detailed refutation, systematically dismantling the Mu'tazilite interpretation. He offers four linguistic and contextual reasons that prove the verse speaks of literal vision, not anticipation. He then connects this to the broader creedal principle: we affirm all of Allah's attributes as He described, while negating any resemblance to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the four numbered reasons in the Arabic.
2
Identify each of the four reasons
Find each reason Maturidi gives and note what aspect it addresses (time, reward, grammar, purpose).
The reasons are clearly marked: 'one of them', 'the second', 'the third', 'the fourth'.
3
Focus on the grammatical argument
Pay attention to the third reason: the preposition 'ilā' is used for looking at something, not waiting. This is a key linguistic point.
The word 'nāẓirah' itself can mean looking or waiting, but the accusative with 'ilā' specifies direction.
4
Study the concluding analogy
Read the final part where Maturidi compares the vision verse to the throne verse (Taha 5). Both must be affirmed without anthropomorphism.
This principle applies to all of Allah's attributes.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how this argument strengthens the case for the beatific vision and the proper method of interpreting divine attributes.
Maturidi shows that denying vision limits God's power by human standards.

ولا قوة إلا بالله . ٤ - وأيضا قوله تعالى : وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة [القيامة , ٢٢/٧٥-٢٣]. ثم لا يحتمل ذلك الانتظار لأوجه أحدها أن الآخرة ليست لوقت الانتظار - إنما هي الدنيا - دار الوقوع والوجود إلا وقت الفزع . وقيل : أن يعاينوا في أنفسهم ما له حق الوقوع. والثاني قوله: ﴿وجوه يومئذ ناضرة ﴾ [القيامة , ٢٢/٧٥] وذلك وقوع الثواب . والثالث قوله : إلى ربها ناظرة [القيامة, ٢٣/٧٥]؛ و«إلى» حرف يستعمل في النظر إلى الشيء لا في الانتظار . والرابع أن القول به يخرج مخرج البشارة ، وتعظيم ما نالوه من النعم، والانتظار ليس ما كان الصرف عن حقيقة المفهوم قضاء على الله ؛ فيلزم القول بالنظر إلى الله كما قال، على نفي جميع معاني الشبه عن الله سبحانه؛ على مِثْل ما أضيف إليه من الكلام والفعل والقدرة والإرادة، يجب الوصف به على نفي جميع معاني الشبه ، وكذلك القول بالهستية. فمن زعم أن الله تعالى لا يقدر أن يُكرم أحدا بالرؤية فهو يُقَدِّر بالرؤية التي فهمها من الخلق. وإن كان القول بـ الرحمن على العرش استوى [طه , ٥/٢٠] وغير ذلك من الآيات لا يجب دفعها بالعرض على المفهوم من الخلق، بل يُحقَّق ذلك على نفي الشبه، فمثله خبر الرؤية. والله الموفق.
And there is no power except with Allah. 4 - Also His saying, the Most High: 'Faces on that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord' (Al-Qiyamah 22-23). Then that waiting is not possible for reasons: one of them is that the Hereafter is not a time of waiting—rather this world is the abode of occurrence and existence except the time of terror. And it was said: that they should witness in themselves what has the right to occur. The second: His saying: 'Faces on that Day are radiant' (Al-Qiyamah 22) and that is the occurrence of reward. The third: His saying: 'looking at their Lord' (Al-Qiyamah 23); and 'ilā' is a particle used for looking at a thing, not for waiting. The fourth: that the statement comes in the form of glad tidings, and magnification of the blessings they have attained, and waiting is not that which diverts from the true meaning, a judgment against Allah; so the statement of looking at Allah is necessary as He said, while negating all meanings of similitude from Allah, the Exalted; similar to what is attributed to Him of speech, action, power, and will, description is necessary while negating all meanings of similitude, and likewise the statement of [al-haystiyya?]. So whoever claims that Allah, the Most High, is not able to honor anyone with vision, he restricts [Allah] by the vision he understood from creation. And if the statement 'The Most Merciful established Himself above the Throne' (Taha 5) and other verses are not to be repelled by comparing to what is understood from creation, but rather that is affirmed while negating similitude, then likewise is the report of vision. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a proof (argument 4) refuting the Mu'tazilite interpretation of 'nāẓirah' in the Quranic verse as 'waiting' for reward rather than literal vision of Allah. Maturidi presents four irrefutable reasons. First, the Hereafter is not a time of anticipation; that belongs to this world. The verse describes the actual state of the blessed. Second, the word 'nāḍirah' (radiant) indicates the reward has already occurred, not awaited. Third, the preposition 'ilā' (to/at) in 'ilā rabbihā nāẓirah' grammatically denotes looking at something, not waiting for it. Fourth, the announcement is a glad tiding magnifying the favor they receive; waiting is not a cause for such exaltation. Maturidi then argues that we must affirm attributes like vision while negating any anthropomorphism, just as we affirm other attributes (speech, power). Denying the ability to grant vision limits God by human standards. He analogizes to the verse about the Throne: we accept it without comparison to creation. Thus, the report of vision is similarly accepted.

The Verse 'For Those Who Do Good is the Best and More' (Argument 5)
Maturidi presents a Quranic proof for vision of Allah via tafsir consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the verse 'For those who do good is the best and more' as evidence for the vision of Allah, and the logical structure of his argument from tafsir and consensus.

Core Idea
Maturidi argues that the interpretation of 'ziyādah' as vision of Allah was so well-known that it became evidence itself.

This lesson examines Maturidi's fifth argument for the vision of Allah, drawn from the Quranic verse in Surah Yunus. He contends that the widespread understanding among early Muslims that 'ziyādah' means seeing Allah provides strong support for the doctrine, even if alternative interpretations exist. The argument hinges on the principle that the accepted interpretation of a verse reflects the consensus of the righteous predecessors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully along with its literal translation. Notice Maturidi's concise style and the key terms he uses.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify 'al-ḥusnā' (the best) and 'ziyādah' (increase) in the verse. Understand that 'ziyādah' is the focus of the argument, interpreted as vision of Allah.
The term 'al-naẓar ilā Allāh' is crucial.
3
Analyze the logical move
Maturidi argues that because the interpretation of 'ziyādah' as vision was widely accepted among early Muslims, it must be valid. Consider why he thinks this is a strong proof.
Think about the principle of consensus (ijmā') in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ه - وأيضاً قوله تعالى للذين أحسنوا الحسنى وزيادة . وجاء في غير خبر أن الزيادة هي النظر إلى الله . وقد يحتمل غير ذلك مما جاء فيه التفسير، لكنه لولا أن القول بالرؤية كان أمرًا ظاهرا لم يُحتمل صرف ظاهر - لم يجئ فيها ـ إليها و [الأمكن أن] يُدفع به الخبر . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
5 - Also His saying, the Most High: 'For those who do good is the best and more' (Yunus 26). And it came in other than one report that the increase is the vision of Allah. And it may admit other than that from what has come in its interpretation, but were it not that the statement of vision was a manifest matter, the apparent meaning would not have been subject to diversion to which it did not come, and [the most possible] that the report may be repelled by it. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a proof (argument 5) from the Quranic verse 'For those who do good is al-ḥusnā (the best) and ziyādah (increase)'. Maturidi notes that in transmitted reports, 'ziyādah' is interpreted as the vision of Allah. He acknowledges there are other interpretations, but argues that the acceptance of this interpretation (i.e., that the increase is seeing Allah) indicates that the doctrine of vision (ru'yah) was widespread and well-known among the early Muslims; otherwise, it would not have been possible to divert the apparent meaning of the verse to that interpretation. The force of the argument is that the consensus (or commonality) of the salaf in interpreting this verse as vision provides evidence for the doctrine.

Misconception: Some think 'ziyādah' refers to additional reward in Paradise, but Maturidi argues it is specifically the vision of Allah.
Correction: Maturidi points to transmitted reports that interpret 'ziyādah' as vision, and argues that the acceptance of this interpretation by early scholars shows it was a manifest truth.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the tafsir of 'ziyādah' as vision of Allah to show that the belief was well-known among early Muslims, supporting the doctrine from Quranic proof.

How does that affect your confidence when facing doubts?
?

The Reward Greater than Works is the Vision of Allah
Maturidi argues that the promised reward exceeding works is the vision of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the promised reward better than the best deeds in this world (tawḥīd) must be the vision of Allah, supported by consensus of the Companions.

Core Idea
The best deed is tawḥīd; the promised reward must be even greater—that is seeing Allah.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a logical argument based on Allah's promise to reward believers with something better than their works. Since the best work in the world is tawḥīd (monotheism) and faith, the reward must be qualitatively higher—identified as the vision of Allah (ru'ya). He also appeals to the widespread acceptance of this interpretation among the Companions, forming a consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the line of reasoning: Allah promises better reward → best deed is tawḥīd → reward is vision.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The premise: Allah rewards with better than what they did. The best deed is tawḥīd. Therefore the better reward must be something beyond tawḥīd — the vision of Allah.
3
Understand the proof from consensus
Maturidi notes that the interpretation of 'increase' as vision came in many reports, and if it were not a manifest matter, the apparent meaning would not be turned away. This shows consensus among the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧ - . ـ وأيضا إن الله تعالى وعد أن يجزى أحسن مما عملوا به في الدنيا ، ولا شيء أحسن مما في الدنيا، من التوحيد وأرفع قدرًا من الإيمان به، إذ هو المستَحْسَن بالعقول . والثواب الموعود من جوهر وجاء في غير خبر أن الزيادة هي النظر إلى الله ، وقد يُحتمل غير ذلك مما جاء فيه التفسير، لكنه لولا أن القول بالرؤية كان أمرًا ظاهرًا وإلا لكان لم يحتمل صرف ظاهر لم يجئ فيها إليها، وهذا في الحاصل استدلال بإجماع الصحابة وشيوع القول بالرؤية فيهم.
7 - Also, Allah, the Most High, promised that He will reward them with better than what they did in this world, and there is nothing better than what is in this world, of tawḥīd, and higher in rank than faith in Him, since it is what is deemed good by intellects. And the promised reward is of the essence. And it came in other than one report that the increase is the vision of Allah, and it may admit other than that from what has come in its interpretation, but were it not that the statement of vision was a manifest matter, otherwise the apparent meaning would not have been diverted to which it did not come, and this is in effect an argument by the consensus of the Companions and the prevalence of the statement of vision among them.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a proof (argument 7) based on the Quranic promise that Allah will reward believers with something better than their best deeds in this world. Maturidi argues that the best deed in the world is tawḥīd (monotheism) and faith, which are deemed good by intellect. The promised reward must be even greater; therefore it is the vision of Allah, which is superior to all worldly deeds. He again mentions that reports interpret 'ziyādah' as vision, and that the widespread acceptance of this interpretation among the Companions (sahābah) and early Muslims constitutes a consensus (ijmā') in favor of the doctrine of vision. The argument relies on the fact that the apparent meaning of the verse is not turned away without strong reason, and the prevalence of the vision interpretation shows it was the understood meaning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the 'better than what they did' refers to multiplication of reward (e.g., 10x or 700x).
Correction: Maturidi argues it is qualitatively different—a better kind of reward, not just more of the same—specifically the vision of Allah.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the vision of Allah is the reward promised in the Quran that surpasses even the best deeds of this world, and this is confirmed by the consensus of the Companions.

?

Maturidi's Argument from Implicit Consensus for Ru'ya
Maturidi uses prevalence of ru'ya among Companions as evidence for interpreting the verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the prevalence of the belief in ru'ya among the Companions as a methodological argument for interpreting the Quranic verse about seeing Allah.

Core Idea
The Companions' widespread belief in ru'ya validates its interpretation.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a distinctive argument for the doctrine of ru'ya (vision of Allah in the afterlife). Rather than citing a single verse or hadith, he points to the fact that the interpretation of the relevant Quranic verse as referring to ru'ya was widely accepted among the Companions. This prevalence, he argues, makes it difficult to reinterpret the verse in a different way. The argument relies on the concept of implicit consensus (ijma') of the Companions in matters of doctrine, not just law.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of Maturidi's reasoning.
The passage contains some repetition; this is typical of his style.
2
Identify the main claim
Maturidi is arguing that the Companions' common understanding of the verse as ru'ya carries evidentiary weight.
3
Understand the logical structure
If ru'ya were not prevalent, the apparent meaning could be diverted. But since it is prevalent, the interpretation is protected.
This is a conditional argument.
4
Consider the implication
Maturidi applies the concept of consensus to creedal interpretation, not just legal rulings.
This expands the scope of ijma'.
5
Reflect on 'implicit consensus'
Maturidi uses the prevalence (shuyu') of the view rather than explicit agreement as evidence.
This is a form of implicit ijma'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر إلى الله . وقد يحتمل غير ذلك مما جاء فيه التفسير، لكنه لولا أن القول بالرؤية كان أمرًا ظاهرًا لم يُحتمل صرف ظاهر لم يجئ فيها إليها و [الأمكن أن] يُدفع به الخبر. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وجاء في غير خبر أن الزيادة هي إلى الله، وقد يُحتمل غير ذلك مما جاء فيه التفسير، لكنه لولا أن القول بالرؤية كان أمرًا ظاهرًا وإلا لكان لم يحتمل صرف ظاهر لم يجئ فيها إليها، وهذا في الحاصل استدلال بإجماع الصحابة وشيوع القول بالرؤية فيهم.
Looking at Allah. And it may be possible otherwise than what has come in the interpretation, but were it not for the fact that the statement of ru'ya was an apparent matter, it would not be possible to divert the apparent meaning that did not come in it to it, and [the possible that] the report may be repelled. There is no power except with Allah. And it came in a non-report that the increase is to Allah, and it may be possible otherwise than what has come in the interpretation, but were it not for the fact that the statement of ru'ya was an apparent matter, otherwise it would not have been possible to divert the apparent meaning that did not come in it to it. And this in summary is an inference from the consensus of the Companions and the prevalence of the statement of ru'ya among them.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents a methodological argument for the belief in ru'ya (vision of Allah) in the afterlife. Maturidi notes that the verse referring to 'looking at Allah' (an-nazar) can be interpreted in other ways, but the fact that the interpretation of ru'ya was widespread among the Companions (it was an 'amr zahir') makes it difficult to dismiss that interpretation. He argues that if ru'ya were not a prevalent belief, one could easily divert the apparent meaning to other interpretations. He then references a report that the 'increase' (al-ziyada) refers to looking at Allah. He concludes that this argument is an inference (istidlal) from the consensus (ijma') of the Companions and the prevalence of the belief in ru'ya among them. The argument is not a direct scriptural proof but a methodological one: the Companions' collective understanding and practice validate the interpretation.

Key Terms
رؤية
إجماع الصحابة
ظاهر
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think consensus of Companions is only for legal rulings, but Maturidi applies it to doctrinal interpretation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the Companions' widespread acceptance of a doctrinal interpretation (ru'ya) can be used as evidence, extending the scope of ijma' beyond jurisprudence.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence Maturidi uses to support the interpretation of the verse about 'looking at Allah' as referring to ru'ya?
A) A direct Quranic verse
B) The prevalence of this interpretation among the Companions
C) A rational proof
D) A hadith from the Prophet

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument for ru'ya relies on the implicit consensus of the Companions as a methodological principle, showing that communal understanding can guide interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse of the Quran that seems to contradict a widely held belief of the early community, what is your first reaction? Are you quick to reinterpret it according to your own understanding, or do you seek to understand how the Companions understood it? This lesson shows that Maturidi trusted the manifest practice of the Companions over a possible alternative reading. Where in your own life have you trusted the collective wisdom of a community over your initial interpretation, and what was the outcome? Are you willing to submit your personal reading to the consensus of the salaf even when it seems counterintuitive? That is the challenge of this lesson: to recognize that community testimony can shape our understanding of revelation. Can you think of a belief you hold today that rests on such communal testimony, and how does that affect your certainty in it?
?
The Companions' widespread acceptance of ru'ya was used as evidence for its validity. Reflect on a time when you were part of a community that held a certain belief unanimously. Did that unanimity increase your conviction, or did it raise doubts about conformity? This lesson suggests that collective agreement can be a sign of truth—but only when the community is the righteous early generation. In your current community, do you see a similar harmony between belief and practice? Where is there dissonance?
?
Maturidi uses the phrase 'amr zahir' — a manifest matter. Is your faith manifest in your life so that others can see it as apparent? Or is it hidden and theoretical? The Companions' belief in ru'ya was not just an abstract doctrine; it shaped their hope and expectation. How does the belief that you will see Allah affect your daily actions and priorities? If it were truly manifest in your heart, what would change today?

Hadith of seeing Allah like the moon and the Prophet's response
Maturidi cites hadith and an incident to show ru'ya is not impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Prophet's words and actions to argue that seeing Allah (ru'ya) is not an impossibility.

Core Idea
The Prophet's gentle response to a question about seeing Allah shows ru'ya is not far-fetched.

This lesson examines a key argument from Imam Maturidi. He cites two pieces of evidence: a promise of seeing Allah like the moon, and an incident where the Prophet answered a question about seeing his Lord. The Prophet's tender reply reveals that such a question is permissible and the concept is not inherently problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read Maturidi's Arabic text and the translation. Notice how he presents the hadith and the incident.
2
Understand the hadith evidence
The hadith says believers will see Allah like the moon. This implies ru'ya is possible in the afterlife.
Contrast with those who deny any vision of Allah.
3
Analyze the Prophet's response
When asked if he saw his Lord, the Prophet said 'with my heart.' He did not rebuke the questioner. Maturidi argues this leniency shows ru'ya is not far-fetched.
Consider why rebuking would have been expected if ru'ya were impossible.
4
Reflect on Maturidi's conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the Prophet's behavior indicates ru'ya is not an impossible concept. This counters those who consider such questions as disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً ما جاء عن رسول الله أنه قال: «سترون ربكم يوم القيامة كما ترون القمر لا تُضامون في رؤيته». وسئل: هل رأيت ربك؟ فقال: «بقلبي». قيل: فلم ينكر على السائل السؤال، وقد علم السائل أن رؤية القلب هي العلم وأنه قد علمه، وأنه لم يسأل عن ذلك. وقد حذر الله عز وجل المؤمنين عن السؤال عن أشياء قد كُفّوا عنها، بقوله: (يا أيها الذين آمنوا لا تسألوا عن أشياء)، فكيف يُحتمل أن يكون السؤال عن مثله لحي؟ وذلك كفر في الحقيقة عند قوم. ثم لا ينهاهم عن ذلك، ولا يوبّخهم في ذلك، بل يلين القول في ذلك، ويُرِي أن ذلك ليس ببعيد. والله الموفق.
And also what has come from the Messenger of Allah that he said: 'You will see your Lord on the Day of Resurrection as you see the moon, you will not be harmed in seeing Him.' And he was asked: Did you see your Lord? He said: 'With my heart.' It is said: So why did he not rebuke the questioner for the question, when the questioner knew that vision of the heart is knowledge and that he already knew it, and that he did not ask about that? And Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, warned the believers against asking about things they were refrained from, with His saying: 'O believers, do not ask about things...' So how can it be possible that the question about the like of Him is for a living being? And that is disbelief in reality according to some people. Then he did not forbid them from that, nor rebuke them in that, but softened the speech in that, and showed that it is not far. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents two pieces of evidence: a hadith promising ru'ya in the afterlife, and an incident where the Prophet was asked if he saw his Lord. The Prophet's answer 'with my heart' indicates that ru'ya is not sensory but real. The key point is that the Prophet did not rebuke the questioner, even though questioning can be forbidden. Instead, he responded gently, implying that the concept of ru'ya is not inherently problematic. Maturidi notes that some groups consider such questioning to be disbelief, but the Prophet's lenient response shows otherwise. The argument is that if ru'ya were impossible or heretical, the Prophet would have forbidden it categorically, but he did not.

Key Terms
رؤية
سترون ربكم
بقلبي
السؤال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that asking about seeing God is forbidden and a sign of disbelief.
Correction: The Prophet's lenient response shows that the question is permissible and the concept of ru'ya is not inherently problematic.

Checkpoint
What does the Prophet's response to the question 'Did you see your Lord?' indicate according to Maturidi?
A) He rebuked the questioner
B) He avoided the question
C) He showed that ru'ya is not far from possibility
D) He denied the possibility of ru'ya

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the Prophet's gentle response to show that the question of seeing Allah is permissible and the concept not far-fetched.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you sincerely asked a question about Allah's nature, or did you avoid it out of fear or doubt? What holds you back from seeking deeper understanding?
?
The Prophet softened his speech and showed that the concept of seeing Allah is not far. In your own heart, do you truly feel close to seeing Allah, or is the idea of encountering Him distant and abstract? What step can you take today to move that reality closer?
?
The source shows that asking about such matters is permissible. Is there a question about the divine you have buried within you? Name it now and commit to seeking an answer from reliable sources.

Ru'ya as the Greatest Reward for Tawhid and Iman
Maturidi proves ru'ya is necessary as the highest reward for the best action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that ru'ya (vision of Allah) is the necessary ultimate reward for the highest act of tawhid and iman.

Core Idea
The best deed deserves a reward beyond natural pleasure: direct vision of Allah.

Maturidi presents a logical argument linking the highest good (tawhid) with the highest reward (ru'ya). He distinguishes between intellectual goodness and natural goodness, showing that the reward for intellectual goodness must be of a higher order. This passage establishes the necessity of ru'ya as the ultimate honor for believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Let Maturidi's own words set the stage before you analyze.
Notice the logical connectors like 'lazima' (necessary).
2
Identify the Premises
The argument rests on three premises: (1) Allah rewards with the best of deeds, (2) tawhid is the best deed because it is intellectually good, (3) the promised reward of Paradise has natural goodness, which is inferior to intellectual goodness.
Understand why intellectual goodness is higher than natural goodness.
3
Understand the Conclusion
From the premises, Maturidi concludes that ru'ya is necessary. It turns the unseen worshipped into a witnessed reality, an honor that surpasses any physical pleasure.
This shows that the highest reward corresponds to the highest act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله تعالى وعد أن يجزى أحسن مما عملوا به في الدنيا، ولا شيء أحسن من التوحيد وأرفع قدرًا من الإيمان به، إذ هو المستحسن بالعقول. والثواب الموعود من جوهر الجنة حسنه حسن الطبع، وذلك دون حسن العقل؛ إذ لا يجوز أن يكون شيء حسنًا في العقول لا يستحسنه ذو عقل، وجائز ما استحسنه الطبع أن يكون طبع لا يتلذذ به كطبع الملائكة، ومثله في العقوبة. لذلك لزم القول بالرؤية لتكون كرامة تبلغ في الجلالة ما أكرموا به، وهو أن يصير لهم المعبود بالغيب شهودًا كما صار المطلوب من الثواب حضورًا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also Allah Almighty promised to reward with the best of what they did in this world, and there is nothing better than tawhid and higher in rank than iman in Him, for it is the best in the intellects. And the promised reward from the essence of Paradise: its goodness is goodness of nature, and that is below the goodness of the intellect; for it is not permissible that something be good in the intellect that no possessor of intellect deems good, and it is possible that what nature deems good may be a nature that does not take pleasure in it like the nature of angels, and the like in punishment. Therefore the statement of ru'ya is necessary so that it is an honor reaching in majesty what they were honored with, which is that the worshipped in the unseen becomes witnessed for them, just as the sought reward becomes present. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument for ru'ya. It begins with the premise that Allah promises to reward with the best of deeds. The best deed is tawhid (monotheism) and iman (faith), which are good in the intellect. The promised reward from Paradise has a natural goodness (hasan al-tab'), which is inferior to intellectual goodness. Since intellectual goodness is higher, the reward must be of a higher order. Maturidi then argues that nothing satisfies this except ru'ya—the direct vision of Allah, which turns the unseen worshipped into a witnessed presence. This is the greatest honor for believers, surpassing physical pleasures. Therefore, ru'ya is necessary (lazima) as the ultimate reward.

Key Terms
توحيد
إيمان
حسن العقل
حسن الطبع
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the reward of Paradise is only physical pleasure.
Correction: Maturidi argues the highest reward must be intellectual, namely ru'ya, because tawhid is intellectually good.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is ru'ya necessary as a reward?
A) Because physical pleasures are insufficient
B) Because it is the only thing promised in the Quran
C) Because tawhid, the best action, deserves a reward that surpasses natural goodness
D) Because all believers will see God anyway

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves ru'ya is necessary because tawhid, being intellectually good, deserves a reward that transcends natural pleasures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly desire to see Allah in the next life, or are you content with Paradise without that vision? What does your current pursuit of tawhid reveal about your heart's longing? Ask yourself honestly and change your intention today.
?
If tawhid is the best deed because it is intellectually good, are you cultivating intellectual love for Allah through knowledge, or is your faith merely a means to avoid Hell? How can you increase your study of His names and attributes to make your faith deeper?
?
When you imagine the reward of Paradise, do you primarily think of physical pleasures? This lesson forces you to ask: is your desire for Allah's vision stronger than your desire for any created delight? Make a concrete plan to prioritize spiritual longing over material wishes.
