Ru'ya Yields Direct Knowledge Unlike Inferential Knowledge
Maturidi argues ru'ya is necessary for direct knowledge in the Hereafter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that ru'ya (vision of Allah) in the Hereafter produces direct knowledge (ilm al-ayan) which is distinct from inferential knowledge and free from doubt, and how this is supported by Quranic evidence and logical symmetry.

Core Idea
In the Hereafter, all will have direct knowledge of Allah; ru'ya grants an even greater honor to believers.

Imam Maturidi addresses a deep question: What kind of knowledge will we have of Allah in the Hereafter? He argues that it will be direct and certain, free from any doubt or whispering. This passage contrasts true knowledge (ilm al-ayan) with inferential knowledge (ilm al-istidlal) and establishes the necessity of ru'ya for believers as a special blessing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style and logical flow.
2
Identify the two types of knowledge
The passage presents a distinction between knowledge by direct vision (ayan) and knowledge by inference (istidlal). Find how Maturidi describes them.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Maturidi cites a Quranic verse and the admission of disbelievers in the Hereafter as evidence that everyone will have certain knowledge.
The specific verse is referenced as 'ولو أننا نزلنا إليهم الملائكة' (Quran 6:111).
4
Understand the logical symmetry and final distinction
Follow the logical argument: since direct knowledge cannot become inferential, inferential cannot become direct. Then note the distinction between general certain knowledge shared by all and the special glad tidings of ru'ya for believers.
This highlights the unique honor of ru'ya for believers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن كلاً يجمع على العلم بالله في الآخرة، العلم الذي لا يعتريه الوسواس، وذلك علم العيان لا علم الاستدلال وكثرة الآيات لا تُحقق علم الحق الذي لا يعتريه ذلك. دليله قوله: (ولو أننا نزلنا إليهم الملائكة) الآية، وما ذكر من استعانة الكفرة بالتكذيب في الآخرة وإنكار الرسل، وقولهم: «لم نمكث إلا ساعة من النهار» وغير ذلك. وبعد، فإنه إذ لا يجوز أن يصير علم العيان نحو علم الاستدلال لم يجز أن علم الاستدلال نحو علم العيان، فثبت أن الرؤية توجب ذلك. وبعد، فان في ذلك العلم يستوى الكافر والمؤمن، والبشارة بالرؤية خُص بها المؤمن. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also everyone agrees on knowledge of Allah in the Hereafter, knowledge that is not affected by whispering (waswas). And that is knowledge of direct vision (ilm al-ayan), not knowledge of inference (ilm al-istidlal), and the abundance of signs does not realize the knowledge of truth that is not affected by that. Its proof is His saying: 'And if We had sent down to them the angels' (the verse), and what mentioned of the disbelievers' seeking help from denial in the Hereafter and denial of the messengers, and their saying: 'We did not remain except an hour of the day' and other than that. Then, since it is not permissible that knowledge of direct vision becomes like knowledge of inference, it is not permissible that knowledge of inference becomes like knowledge of direct vision. So it is established that ru'ya necessitates that. Then, in that knowledge the disbeliever and the believer are equal, but the glad tidings of ru'ya are specific to the believer. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage argues that in the Hereafter, all beings will have certain knowledge of Allah (not subject to doubt). This knowledge is direct (ilm al-ayan) rather than inferential (ilm al-istidlal). Maturidi supports this with Quranic evidence that even disbelievers will have undeniable knowledge in the Hereafter (e.g., they will admits their error). He then uses a logical symmetry: since direct knowledge cannot become inferential, inferential cannot become direct. Therefore, ru'ya (which gives direct knowledge) is necessary. He notes that while both believers and disbelievers will share this certain knowledge, the special glad tiding of ru'ya is for believers only—indicating that ru'ya provides an additional honor beyond mere knowledge.

Key Terms
علم العيان
علم الاستدلال
رؤية
وسواس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ru'ya (vision of Allah) is for everyone in the Hereafter, including disbelievers.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that while both believers and disbelievers will have certain knowledge of Allah, the special blessing of ru'ya is exclusive to believers.

Checkpoint
What type of knowledge will all have in the Hereafter according to Maturidi?
A) Inferential knowledge (ilm al-istidlal)
B) Direct vision knowledge (ilm al-ayan)
C) Speculative knowledge
D) Knowledge through signs

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that ru'ya is necessary to obtain direct, certain knowledge of Allah in the Hereafter, and that this vision is a special blessing for believers only.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You will inevitably have certain knowledge of Allah in the Hereafter—knowledge that even disbelievers will possess. But are you living today with a longing for the special honor of ru'ya, or are you content with just the knowledge that you will see Him? What change would it make in your life if you truly yearn for that vision?
?
Maturidi says that the abundance of signs (ayat) does not realize the truth that is free from doubt. How many times have you sought certainty through more knowledge, more arguments, or more proofs, while neglecting the direct connection with Allah? What one step can you take today to seek Him directly, not just through inference?
?
The blessing of ru'ya is exclusively for believers, yet many believers today are distracted by temporary pleasures. If you were guaranteed that you would see Allah in the Hereafter, how would your priorities shift right now? What would you be willing to sacrifice to ensure you are among those who receive that glad tiding?

Distinguishing Idrak from Ru'ya
Maturidi clarifies that negating idrak does not negate ru'ya, and gives two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi holds that the negation of idrak (encompassment) in the Quran does not negate ru'ya (vision) of Allah.

Core Idea
Negating idrak is not negating ru'ya.

Imam Maturidi addresses a common confusion about the Quranic verse 'No vision can grasp Him.' Some assume this denies any vision of Allah. Maturidi clarifies that the verse only negates idrak, a complete encompassing, not ru'ya (seeing). He then gives two proofs: one from another Quranic verse and one from rational reflection on Allah's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: إدراك, رؤية, إحاطة, حد.
Pay attention to the analogy with knowledge.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage presents two arguments: first from Quranic analogy (verse 20:110), second from rational reasoning about limitation (hadd). List them in your own words.
The first is scriptural, the second is rational.
3
Understand the key distinction
Reflect on why idrak is impossible for Allah (encompassment requires a limited object) while ru'ya is possible. Note Maturidi's emphasis on Allah being one in essence (واحدي الذات) and above limitation.
This guards against both anthropomorphism and denial of vision.
4
Check your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to see if you can identify what the verse negates according to Maturidi.
Answer is based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
{ قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : } ولا نقول بالإدراك، لقوله: (ولا تدركه الأبصار)؛ فقد امتدح به بنفي الإدراك لا بنفي الرؤية؛ وهو كقوله: (ولا يحيطون به علما)، كان في ذلك إيجاب العلم ونفي الإحاطة، فمثله في حق الإدراك. وبالله التوفيق. وأيضاً إن الإدراك إنما هو الإحاطة بالمحدود، والله يتعالى عن وصف الحد، إذ هو نهاية وتقصير عما هو أعلى منه على أنه واحدي الذات ـ والحد وصف المتصل الأجزاء حتى ينقضي - مع إحالة القول بالحد أو كان ولا ما يُحد أو به يُحد، فهو على ذلك.
Imam Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And we do not affirm idrak (comprehension), because of His saying: 'No vision can grasp Him' (6:103); for He has praised Himself by negating idrak, not by negating ru'ya. And it is like His saying: 'And they encompass Him not in knowledge' (20:110); in that there is affirmation of knowledge and negation of encompassment; so the like applies to idrak. And success is from Allah. Also, idrak is only encompassment of the limited, and Allah is exalted above being described by limit (hadd), for it is an end and a falling short from what is higher than Him, while He is one in essence. And limit (hadd) is the description of that whose parts are connected until it ends, along with the impossibility of saying that He is limited or that He is limited by something or limited with something, so He is above that.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage provides a crucial clarification: the Quranic negation ('No vision can grasp Him') negates idrak (comprehension/encompassment), not ru'ya (vision). Maturidi draws an analogy with the verse 'They encompass Him not in knowledge'—which affirms knowledge of Allah while negating complete encompassment. Similarly, ru'ya is affirmed while idrak is negated. He then gives a rational proof: idrak means complete encompassment of a limited object. But Allah is not limited (he is one in essence, not composed of parts). Therefore, idrak is impossible for Allah, whereas ru'ya does not imply limitation and is thus possible. This distinguishes the Sunni position from anthropomorphism on one side and denial of ru'ya on the other.

Key Terms
إدراك
رؤية
إحاطة
حد
واحدي الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verse 'No vision can grasp Him' negates the possibility of seeing Allah altogether.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the verse only negates idrak (comprehensive grasping), not ru'ya (vision). Ru'ya is affirmed by other proofs.

Checkpoint
What does the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' (6:103) negate according to Maturidi?
A) Seeing Allah
B) Encompassing Allah
C) Knowing Allah
D) Believing in Allah

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that the Quranic negation of idrak does not negate ru'ya; rather, it affirms ru'ya while denying complete encompassment, consistent with Allah's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that you will see Allah in Paradise, but do you live today with the humble admission that your mind cannot fully grasp Him? In what areas do you try to confine Allah to your own understanding, and how will this distinction between idrak and ru'ya change that?
?
The Quran negates idrak, not ru'ya. Have you ever felt spiritually distant because you couldn't 'encompass' Allah intellectually? How does knowing that you can see Him without grasping Him transform your relationship with Him starting now?
?
If Allah is beyond all limits and boundaries, then how dare you impose your own expectations on how He should act or answer your prayers? What specific limitation have you placed on Allah recently, and what will you do today to remove that limit and submit to His absolute transcendence?

The Need for Ru'ya as an Honor
Maturidi establishes the necessity of ru'ya as an honor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that ru'ya is necessary as an honor for believers, based on the distinction between natural and intellectual beauty.

Core Idea
Ru'ya turns the unseen worshipped into a witnessed presence for believers.

Maturidi begins by contrasting natural beauty with intellectual beauty to establish a hierarchy. He then argues that the vision of Allah (ru'ya) is a necessary honor that transforms the unseen worship into direct witness, similar to how reward becomes present. This passage lays the foundation for understanding ru'ya as a special grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Read from the beginning up to the first 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'. Note the distinction between natural and intellectual beauty.
This sets up a comparative framework.
2
Identify the argument for necessity
Maturidi says 'therefore it is necessary to affirm the vision' (لذلك لزم القول بالرؤية). Understand why this necessity follows from the beauty discussion.
The necessity is tied to honoring believers.
3
Analyze the analogy with reward
Notice how he compares ru'ya to the reward becoming present. This clarifies the nature of honor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجنة حسنه حسن الطبع، وذلك دون حسن العقل؛ إذ لا يجوز أن يكون شيء حسنًا في العقول لا يستحسنه ذو عقل، وجائز ما استحسنه الطبع أن يكون طبع لا يتلذذ به كطبع الملائكة، ومثله في العقوبة. لذلك لزم القول بالرؤية لتكون كرامة تبلغ في الجلالة ما أَكْرِموا به، وهو أن يصير لهم المعبود بالغيب شهودًا كما صار المطلوب من الثواب حضورًا. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضاً إن كلاً يجمع على العلم بالله في الآخرة ، العلم الذي لا يعتريه الوسواس، وذلك علم العيان لا علم الاستدلال وكثرة الآيات لا تُحقق علم الحق الذي لا يعتريه ذلك. دليله قوله : ولو أننا نزلنا إليهم الملائكة الآية ، وما ذكر من استعانة الكفرة بالتكذيب في الآخرة وإنكار الرسل ، وقولهم : لم نمكث إلا ساعة من النهار وغير ذلك. وبعد، فإنه إذ لا يجوز أن يصير علم العيان نحو علم الاستدلال لم يجز أن علم الاستدلال نحو علم العيان، فثبت أن الرؤية توجب ذلك . وبعد، فان في ذلك العلم يستوى الكافر والمؤمن، والبشارة بالرؤية خُص بها المؤمن. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The garden's beauty is the beauty of nature, which is below the beauty of intellect; for it is not permissible that something be good in the intellects that no intelligent person considers good, and it is possible that what nature considers good is a nature that does not delight in it, like the nature of angels, and likewise in punishment. Therefore, it is necessary to affirm the vision (ru'ya) so that it be an honor reaching in majesty what they were honored with, which is that the worshipped in the unseen becomes witnessed for them, just as the desired reward becomes present. And there is no power except with Allah. And also, everyone agrees on the knowledge of Allah in the afterlife, the knowledge that is not affected by doubt, and that is the knowledge of direct vision ('ayan), not the knowledge of inference (istidlal), and the abundance of signs does not verify the knowledge of truth that is not affected by that. Its evidence is His saying: 'And even if We sent down to them the angels' [al-An'am 6:111] and what is mentioned of the disbelievers' resorting to denial in the afterlife and their denial of the messengers, and their saying: 'We stayed not but an hour of the day' [Yunus 10:45] and other than that. And then, since it is not permissible for the knowledge of direct vision to become like the knowledge of inference, it is not permissible for the knowledge of inference to become like the knowledge of direct vision, so it is established that the vision necessitates that. And then, in that knowledge the disbeliever and the believer are equal, but the glad tiding of the vision is specific to the believer. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing that the vision of Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife is a special honor for believers that provides direct, doubt-free knowledge. Maturidi begins by distinguishing between natural beauty and intellectual beauty, stating that natural beauty is inferior. He then argues that the vision (ru'ya) is necessary as an honor for believers, making the unseen worshipped object witnessed, just as reward becomes present. He emphasizes that all people will have knowledge of Allah in the afterlife, but that knowledge is of direct vision ('ayan), not inferential (istidlal). He cites Quranic evidence (6:111 and others) to show that disbelievers will still deny despite signs, indicating that inferential knowledge can be doubted. He then reasons that since direct vision cannot become like inference, inference cannot become like direct vision, thus establishing that ru'ya provides a unique certainty. Finally, he notes that while both believer and disbeliever share the knowledge of Allah in the afterlife, the glad tiding of seeing Him is exclusive to believers.

Key Terms
رؤية
كرامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the vision of Allah necessary?
A) Because paradise's beauty is intellectual.
B) Because it is an honor that makes the unseen worshipped become witnessed.
C) Because natural beauty is superior.
D) Because it is a reward for all people.

Key Takeaway
Ru'ya is necessary as a special honor that transforms the unseen worship into direct witness for believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly desire to see Allah more than any pleasure of this world or paradise, and what are you doing today to cultivate that longing?
?
If the ultimate honor is witnessing Allah, why do your daily actions often seek recognition from people rather than that ultimate honor? What will you change right now to realign your priorities?
?
The vision transforms the unseen worshipped into witnessed reality for you. Is your relationship with Allah based on mere belief or an urgent, present intimacy? What step will you take this week to make your worship more like that witnessing?

Ru'ya Provides Direct Certain Knowledge for Believers
Maturidi distinguishes direct visual knowledge from inferential knowledge and asserts ru'ya's exclusivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that ru'ya provides a direct, doubt-free knowledge of Allah that is exclusive to believers, unlike inferential knowledge.

Core Idea
Direct vision gives certainty; inference can be doubted, and only believers receive this honor.

In this part of the passage, Maturidi shifts to discuss the type of knowledge in the afterlife. He emphasizes that all will have knowledge of Allah, but it will be direct visual knowledge, not inferential. He cites Quranic evidence to show that even with signs, disbelievers still deny. Finally, he highlights that the glad tiding of seeing Allah is exclusive to believers, making it a special honor.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'وأيضاً إن كلاً يجمع' to the end. Notice the distinction between knowledge of direct vision ('ayan) and inferential knowledge (istidlal).
The key Arabic terms are علم العيان and علم الاستدلال.
2
Understand the Quranic evidence
Maturidi references Quran 6:111 and other verses to show that disbelievers still deny despite signs, indicating inferential knowledge can be doubted.
This supports the need for direct vision.
3
Grasp the exclusivity point
The last sentence states that while both believer and disbeliever share the knowledge of Allah in the afterlife, the good news of vision is specific to believers.
This makes ru'ya a 'glad tiding' and a special honor.

?

Are you willing to accept that you may never have absolute certainty in this life, and yet still strive for the faith that leads to that vision? ?

?

If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow, would you be counted among those who are honored with that vision, or among those who only know Him without seeing Him? What in your life today—your habits, your priorities—shows that you truly consider that moment the most important? Start today by making one sincere du'a for that blessed vision.

The Quranic Negation of Idrak and Affirmation of Ru'ya
Maturidi states the doctrine and cites Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Muslim scholars negate idrak (comprehensive perception) of Allah while affirming ru'ya (vision), based on the Quranic evidence provided by Imam al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
Negation of idrak is not negation of ru'ya.

Imam al-Maturidi begins by stating the position of Ahl al-Sunnah: they do not affirm idrak for Allah. He immediately cites the Quranic verse 'Vision comprehends Him not' (6:103) and explains that Allah praises Himself by negating idrak, not ru'ya. He draws a parallel with another verse that affirms knowledge while negating encompassing. This lesson examines this foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the concise style of Maturidi.
2
Identify the Quranic citation and its implication
Find the verse from Surah al-An'am and note how Maturidi uses it to support his position. Understand the word 'idrak' in the verse.
The verse negates idrak, not ru'ya.
3
Understand the parallel with encompassing
Maturidi compares idrak to ihata (encompassing) using Surah Ta Ha 20:110. Reflect on how this parallel strengthens the argument.
Just as knowledge does not require encompassing, vision does not require comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : ولا نقول بالإدراك ، لقوله : ولا تدركه الابصار [الأنعام ، ١٠٣/٦] ؛ فقد امتدح به بنفي الإدراك لا بنفي الرؤية؛ وهو كقوله: ﴿ولا يحيطون به علما﴾ [طه، ۱۱۰/۲۰] ، كان في ذلك إيجاب العلم ونفي الإحاطة، فمثله في حق الإدراك. وبالله التوفيق. وأيضاً إن الإدراك إنما هو الإحاطة بالمحدود، والله يتعالى عن وصف الحد، إذ هو نهاية وتقصير عما هو أعلى منه على أنه واحدي الذات ـ والحد وصف المتصل الأجزاء حتى ينقضي - مع إحالة القول بالحد أو كان ولا ما يُحد أو به يُحد، فهو على ذلك لا يتغير. على أن لكل شيء حدا يُدرك بسبيله، نحو الطعم واللون والذوق والرائحة وغير ذلك من حدود خاصية الأشياء، جعل الله لكل شيء من ذلك وجها يُدرك به ويُحاط به، حتى العقول والأعراض. فأخبر الله أنه ليس بذي حدود وجهات هي طرق إدراكه بالأسباب الموضوعة لتلك الجهات . وعلى ذلك القول بالرؤية والعلم جميعا. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وبعد، فإن القول بالرؤية يقع على وجوه ؛ لا يُعلم حقيقة كل وجه من ذلك إلا بالعلم بذلك الوجه، حتى إذا عُبّر عنه بالرؤية صُرف إلى ذلك، وما لا يُعرف له الوجه - بدون ذكر الرؤية - لزم الوقف في مائيتها على تحقيقها . وأما الإدراك إنما هو معنى الوقوف على حدود الشيء. ألا ترى أن الظل في التحقيق يُرى لكنه لا يدرك إلا بالشمس، وإلا كان مرئيا على ما يُرى لوقت نسخ الشمس؛ ولكن لا يُدرك بالرؤية إلا بما يتبين له الحد. وكذلك ضوء النهار يُرى، لكن حده لا يُعرف بذاته. وكذلك الظلمة لأن طرفها لا يرى فيُدرك ويُحاط به. وبالحدود يُدرك الشيء وإن كان يرى لا بها ؛ ولذلك ضرب المثل بالقمر، أنه لا يُعرف حده ولا سعته ليوقف ويحاط به ويُرى بيقين . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The jurist Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And we do not affirm comprehension (idrak), because of His saying: 'Vision comprehends Him not' [al-An'am 6:103]; for He praised Himself by negating comprehension, not by negating vision; and it is like His saying: 'But they encompass Him not with knowledge' [Ta Ha 20:110]. In that there is an affirmation of knowledge and a negation of encompassing, so it is similar regarding comprehension. And with Allah is success. And also, comprehension is only encompassing of the limited, and Allah is exalted above the description of limit, for it is an end and a deficiency relative to what is higher than it, while He is One in essence. And limit is a description of what is connected in parts until it ends—with the impossibility of affirming limit, for there is nothing that limits Him or by which He is limited, so He is upon that, unchanging. Yet everything has a limit by which it is perceived, such as taste, color, touch, smell, and other limits of the properties of things. Allah has made for each thing a face by which it is perceived and encompassed, even intellects and accidents. So He informed that He is not possessed of limits and directions that are the means of perceiving Him through the causes placed for those directions. And upon that is the affirmation of vision and knowledge together. And there is no power except with Allah. And then, the affirmation of vision occurs in aspects; the reality of each aspect is not known except by knowledge of that aspect, so that when it is expressed by 'vision', it is turned to that. And what is not known for it an aspect—without mention of vision—it is necessary to stop regarding its reality until it is verified. But comprehension is the meaning of stopping at the limits of a thing. Do you not see that the shadow, in reality, is seen but is not comprehended except by the sun; otherwise it would be visible as it is seen at the time of the sun's removal; but it is not comprehended by vision except by what makes its limit apparent. Likewise, daylight is seen, but its limit is not known by itself. And likewise, darkness, because its extremity is not seen, so it is not comprehended and encompassed. And by limits a thing is comprehended, even if it is seen not by them; therefore an example is given of the moon, that its limit is not known nor its breadth, to stop and encompass it, yet it is seen with certainty. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal clarification that Muslim scholars do not affirm 'idrak' (comprehensive perception) for Allah, but do affirm 'ru'ya' (vision). Maturidi begins by stating the position: 'We do not affirm idrak.' He cites Quran 6:103, 'Vision comprehends Him not,' explaining that Allah praises Himself by negating idrak, not ru'ya. He parallels this with Quran 20:110, which affirms knowledge but negates encompassing. Then he defines idrak as encompassing (ihata) of the limited. Since Allah is beyond limit (had)—limit being a description of composite things that end—He cannot be encompassed. Maturidi explains that everything has a limit by which it is perceived (e.g., taste, color), but Allah has no such limits or directions. Therefore, we affirm both ru'ya and knowledge of Him. He then clarifies that 'ru'ya' can refer to different aspects, and one must understand the intended aspect. He contrasts idrak as 'stopping at the limits of a thing' with ru'ya. To illustrate, he gives examples: a shadow is seen but not comprehended except by the sun (which reveals its limit); daylight is seen but its limit unknown; darkness's extremity is not seen; the moon's limit is unknown but it is seen with certainty. These examples show that something can be seen without being fully comprehended, proving that ru'ya of Allah is possible without idrak.

Key Terms
إدراك
رؤية
إحاطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the verse 'Vision comprehends Him not' negates any kind of seeing of Allah.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the verse negates idrak (comprehensive perception), not ru'ya (vision).

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the Quranic verse 'لا تدركه الابصار' (Vision comprehends Him not) negate?
A) Ru'ya (vision)
B) Idrak (comprehension)
C) Both ru'ya and idrak
D) Only the possibility of seeing with physical eyes

Key Takeaway
The Quranic negation of idrak does not negate ru'ya; Allah can be seen without being fully comprehended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'Vision comprehends Him not,' does your heart tremble with awe, or do you secretly feel relieved that you don’t have to seek His face? What does your reaction reveal about your longing for Allah?
?
If you truly believed that you can see Allah without fully comprehending Him, how would that change the way you prepare for the Hereafter? What would you do differently today to yearn for that sight?
?
You affirm that Allah is known but not encompassed. But in practice, do you try to box Him into your limited understanding? Where in your life do you demand full comprehension before submitting? Repent and surrender to His unknowable vastness.

Definition of Idrak as Encompassing and Illustrative Examples
Maturidi defines idrak and provides illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of idrak as encompassing the limited, why Allah cannot be described by limits, and how examples from creation show that something can be seen without being comprehended.

Core Idea
Idrak is encompassing the limited; Allah is unlimited, so He is seen but not comprehended.

After establishing the Quranic basis, Maturidi provides a conceptual definition of idrak: it is the encompassing (ihata) of the limited. Since Allah is beyond any limit or direction, He cannot be encompassed. He then clarifies that 'ru'ya' can refer to different aspects, and gives everyday examples—shadow, daylight, darkness, the moon—to illustrate that something can be seen without being fully comprehended. This lesson unpacks these arguments and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of idrak
Read the part where Maturidi defines idrak as 'encompassing of the limited'. Understand why this definition is crucial.
Idrak is not just any perception; it is comprehensive perception of limits.
2
Understand why Allah cannot have limits
Maturidi argues that Allah is exalted above limit. Reflect on the implications: limit implies composition and deficiency, which are incompatible with divine oneness.
Allah has no cause, no parts, no end.
3
Note the different aspects of ru'ya
Maturidi explains that 'ru'ya' has multiple meanings, and one must know the intended aspect. This prevents confusion.
This shows careful theological method.
4
Examine the illustrative examples
Look at each example: shadow, daylight, darkness, moon. For each, identify how it demonstrates that something can be seen without being comprehended.
These are everyday experiences that make the distinction tangible.
5
Synthesize: vision does not equal comprehension
After going through the examples, conclude that ru'ya is possible without idrak. Affirming ru'ya for Allah is rational.
This is the main takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : ولا نقول بالإدراك ، لقوله : ولا تدركه الابصار [الأنعام ، ١٠٣/٦] ؛ فقد امتدح به بنفي الإدراك لا بنفي الرؤية؛ وهو كقوله: ﴿ولا يحيطون به علما﴾ [طه، ۱۱۰/۲۰] ، كان في ذلك إيجاب العلم ونفي الإحاطة، فمثله في حق الإدراك. وبالله التوفيق. وأيضاً إن الإدراك إنما هو الإحاطة بالمحدود، والله يتعالى عن وصف الحد، إذ هو نهاية وتقصير عما هو أعلى منه على أنه واحدي الذات ـ والحد وصف المتصل الأجزاء حتى ينقضي - مع إحالة القول بالحد أو كان ولا ما يُحد أو به يُحد، فهو على ذلك لا يتغير. على أن لكل شيء حدا يُدرك بسبيله، نحو الطعم واللون والذوق والرائحة وغير ذلك من حدود خاصية الأشياء، جعل الله لكل شيء من ذلك وجها يُدرك به ويُحاط به، حتى العقول والأعراض. فأخبر الله أنه ليس بذي حدود وجهات هي طرق إدراكه بالأسباب الموضوعة لتلك الجهات . وعلى ذلك القول بالرؤية والعلم جميعا. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وبعد، فإن القول بالرؤية يقع على وجوه ؛ لا يُعلم حقيقة كل وجه من ذلك إلا بالعلم بذلك الوجه، حتى إذا عُبّر عنه بالرؤية صُرف إلى ذلك، وما لا يُعرف له الوجه - بدون ذكر الرؤية - لزم الوقف في مائيتها على تحقيقها . وأما الإدراك إنما هو معنى الوقوف على حدود الشيء. ألا ترى أن الظل في التحقيق يُرى لكنه لا يدرك إلا بالشمس، وإلا كان مرئيا على ما يُرى لوقت نسخ الشمس؛ ولكن لا يُدرك بالرؤية إلا بما يتبين له الحد. وكذلك ضوء النهار يُرى، لكن حده لا يُعرف بذاته. وكذلك الظلمة لأن طرفها لا يرى فيُدرك ويُحاط به. وبالحدود يُدرك الشيء وإن كان يرى لا بها ؛ ولذلك ضرب المثل بالقمر، أنه لا يُعرف حده ولا سعته ليوقف ويحاط به ويُرى بيقين . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The jurist Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And we do not affirm comprehension (idrak), because of His saying: 'Vision comprehends Him not' [al-An'am 6:103]; for He praised Himself by negating comprehension, not by negating vision; and it is like His saying: 'But they encompass Him not with knowledge' [Ta Ha 20:110]. In that there is an affirmation of knowledge and a negation of encompassing, so it is similar regarding comprehension. And with Allah is success. And also, comprehension is only encompassing of the limited, and Allah is exalted above the description of limit, for it is an end and a deficiency relative to what is higher than it, while He is One in essence. And limit is a description of what is connected in parts until it ends—with the impossibility of affirming limit, for there is nothing that limits Him or by which He is limited, so He is upon that, unchanging. Yet everything has a limit by which it is perceived, such as taste, color, touch, smell, and other limits of the properties of things. Allah has made for each thing a face by which it is perceived and encompassed, even intellects and accidents. So He informed that He is not possessed of limits and directions that are the means of perceiving Him through the causes placed for those directions. And upon that is the affirmation of vision and knowledge together. And there is no power except with Allah. And then, the affirmation of vision occurs in aspects; the reality of each aspect is not known except by knowledge of that aspect, so that when it is expressed by 'vision', it is turned to that. And what is not known for it an aspect—without mention of vision—it is necessary to stop regarding its reality until it is verified. But comprehension is the meaning of stopping at the limits of a thing. Do you not see that the shadow, in reality, is seen but is not comprehended except by the sun; otherwise it would be visible as it is seen at the time of the sun's removal; but it is not comprehended by vision except by what makes its limit apparent. Likewise, daylight is seen, but its limit is not known by itself. And likewise, darkness, because its extremity is not seen, so it is not comprehended and encompassed. And by limits a thing is comprehended, even if it is seen not by them; therefore an example is given of the moon, that its limit is not known nor its breadth, to stop and encompass it, yet it is seen with certainty. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal clarification that Muslim scholars do not affirm 'idrak' (comprehensive perception) for Allah, but do affirm 'ru'ya' (vision). Maturidi begins by stating the position: 'We do not affirm idrak.' He cites Quran 6:103, 'Vision comprehends Him not,' explaining that Allah praises Himself by negating idrak, not ru'ya. He parallels this with Quran 20:110, which affirms knowledge but negates encompassing. Then he defines idrak as encompassing (ihata) of the limited. Since Allah is beyond limit (had)—limit being a description of composite things that end—He cannot be encompassed. Maturidi explains that everything has a limit by which it is perceived (e.g., taste, color), but Allah has no such limits or directions. Therefore, we affirm both ru'ya and knowledge of Him. He then clarifies that 'ru'ya' can refer to different aspects, and one must understand the intended aspect. He contrasts idrak as 'stopping at the limits of a thing' with ru'ya. To illustrate, he gives examples: a shadow is seen but not comprehended except by the sun (which reveals its limit); daylight is seen but its limit unknown; darkness's extremity is not seen; the moon's limit is unknown but it is seen with certainty. These examples show that something can be seen without being fully comprehended, proving that ru'ya of Allah is possible without idrak.

Key Terms
إدراك
إحاطة
الحد
المحدود
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah is seen, He must be fully comprehended or limited.
Correction: Maturidi shows through examples that vision does not require comprehension; one can see something without knowing its limits.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the examples Maturidi uses to illustrate that something can be seen without being comprehended?
A) Shadow
B) Daylight
C) Fire
D) The moon

Key Takeaway
Idrak is defined as stopping at the limits of a thing; since Allah has no limits, He is seen but not comprehended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that Allah is seen but not comprehended. Is there an area of your life where you demand to fully understand Allah before trusting Him? What would it mean for you to surrender that demand today?
?
The shadow is seen, yet its limit is only known through the sun. In what ways might you be looking only at the 'shadow' of Allah's actions—focusing on what is apparent—without seeking the true Source that reveals the limits? How can you turn your gaze to Him?
?
Maturidi says that the moon is seen with certainty, yet its limits are unknown. When you struggle with uncertainty about Allah's decree, can you still affirm that you see His wisdom with certainty, even when you cannot encompass it? Will you commit to thanking Him for what you do not understand?

The Analogy of Knowledge to Vision of God
Maturidi argues by analogy for the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that vision of God in the afterlife is possible by analogy with how we acquire knowledge of the unseen beyond ordinary physical means.

Core Idea
Knowledge and vision of God both transcend ordinary worldly conditions.

This lesson examines a concise yet profound argument from Imam Maturidi. He points out that in this physical world, our knowledge normally only extends to bodies and their accidents. Yet we have come to know things about the unseen — like God's attributes — through revelation and reason. Maturidi then draws an analogy: just as knowledge of the unseen is possible beyond the ordinary scope, so too is vision of God possible beyond the ordinary conditions of sight. This passage establishes a key principle for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting the key terms: الشاهد, العرض, الجسم, الغائب, الرؤية. Try to grasp the logical flow.
The phrase 'والله أعلم' is a standard conclusion.
2
Understand the analogy
Identify the two parts: (1) In the witnessed world we know only accident and body. (2) Knowledge of the unseen came from outside that. The conclusion: vision of God is similar.
The analogy is between knowledge and vision, not between the objects.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this analogy defend the possibility of seeing God? How does it avoid anthropomorphism?
This argument shows that divine vision is not subject to physical limitations.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you have grasped the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إنه في الشاهد - بكل أسباب العلم - لا يعلم غير العرض والجسم؛ ثم جاء من العلم بالغائب خارجا منه ، فمثله الرؤية . والله أعلم.
And also: in the witnessed realm (al-shāhid), by all means of knowledge, nothing is known except accident (al-ʿaraḍ) and body (al-jism); then knowledge of the unseen (al-ghāʾib) came from outside that [ordinary scope], so the likeness of that is vision (al-ruʾya). And God knows best.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents an analogical argument. The author (Maturidi) states that in the witnessed world, our knowledge is normally limited to accidents and bodies. However, we have come to know things about the unseen (ghāʾib) that are outside this ordinary scope—for example, knowledge of God's attributes. Similarly, the vision of God in the afterlife should not be judged by the ordinary conditions of vision in this world. Just as knowledge of the unseen is possible through revelation and reason, so too is vision of God possible. The phrase 'والله أعلم' (God knows best) concludes the point.

Key Terms

العرض (al-ʿaraḍ)
الجسم (al-jism)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that vision of God must follow the same rules as physical vision (e.g., requiring distance, direction, or light).
Correction: Maturidi argues that just as we accept knowledge of the unseen that comes from outside ordinary physical means, we can accept vision of God that is not subject to physical conditions.

Checkpoint

C) Both are limited to bodies and accidents.
D) Both require special prophetic knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses analogy to show that the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife is as acceptable as our knowledge of the unseen, both transcending ordinary worldly conditions.

You accept the unseen (ghaib) through revelation — but do you live as if it is more real than what you see? When your desires conflict with a command from the unseen, which wins? Be honest: what is one area where your faith in the unseen is weak, and how will you strengthen it starting today?
?
Maturidi's argument shows that we can grasp truths beyond our ordinary experience. Is there a truth from God that you have resisted because it didn't fit your 'ordinary' reasoning? Will you submit to it now, even if your mind struggles? Commit to one such truth and what obedience requires.

Third and Fourth Points: Vision Without Conditions
Maturidi enumerates two arguments showing that vision can occur without normal conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments Maturidi uses to show that vision is not necessarily dependent on the usual physical conditions, and how this supports the possibility of seeing God without those conditions.

Core Idea
Vision can happen without normal conditions; conditions can exist without vision.

Maturidi continues his refutation of al-Ka'bi's claim that vision requires specific conditions like distance and facing. Here he presents two additional arguments: first, we see shadows, darkness, and light without those conditions; second, conditions can be present yet vision absent, and vice versa. These points help establish that Allah can be seen without being limited by physical constraints.

Guided Path
1
Read the third point
Identify the third argument: seeing shadow, darkness, and light without the usual conditions. Notice that these are examples where vision occurs despite missing the conditions al-Ka'bi deemed necessary.
Shadow, darkness, and light are seen directly, not through conditions like distance or facing.
2
Understand the fourth point
The fourth argument is twofold: conditions can be present without vision (due to a veil or substance properties), and vision can occur without conditions. This shows that conditions are neither necessary nor sufficient for vision.
Maturidi uses an analogy with the body attribute to strengthen his point.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy: just as God can be Agent and Knower without being a body, so vision can occur without the usual physical conditions. This parallels the logic of negating necessary attributes.
The analogy helps show that conditions are not essential to the act of vision itself.
4
Connect to divine vision
Relate these arguments to the broader debate about seeing Allah. If vision can occur without physical conditions, then there is no rational barrier to the beatific vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث ما بينا من رؤية الظل والظلمة والنور من غير شيء من تلك الوجوه. والرابع أنه قد يجوز وجود تلك المعاني كلها مع عدم الرؤية، إما بحجب أو بخصائص جوهر؛ فجاز تحقيق الرؤية على نفي تلك المعاني؛ نحو ما أجيب القائل بالجسم عند معارضته بالفاعل والعالم أنه لا كذلك: فيجوز وجود ذلك ولا جسم، فمثله في الرؤية.
The third: what we have explained regarding the seeing of shadow, darkness, and light without any of those aspects [i.e., the usual conditions]. And the fourth: that it is possible that all those meanings [conditions] exist with an absence of vision, either due to a veil or due to the specific properties of a substance; so it is permissible to affirm vision while negating those meanings, just as the one who says [God is] a body was answered when he objected by [saying He is] the Agent (al-fāʿil) and the Knower (al-ʿālim) that it is not so: for it is possible that He exists [as Agent and Knower] and not as a body, and the like applies to vision.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents two points (third and fourth) in a series of arguments. The third point: we see shadows, darkness, and light without the usual conditions of vision (such as distance, facing, etc.). This shows that vision is not always dependent on those conditions. The fourth point: it is possible for all those conditions to be present yet vision still not occur (due to a veil or the specific property of a substance), and conversely, it is possible to have vision even when those conditions are absent. Maturidi draws an analogy: just as when someone argues that God must be a body because He is an agent and knower, the response is that He can be Agent and Knower without being a body; similarly, vision can occur without the usual physical conditions. Therefore, the conditions that al-Ka'bi insisted upon are not necessary for the vision of God.

Key Terms
الظل (al-ẓill)
الظلمة (al-ẓulma)
النور (al-nūr)
الحجاب (al-ḥijāb)
خصائص الجوهر (khaṣāʾiṣ al-jawhar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The usual physical conditions (distance, facing, etc.) are necessary for any act of vision.
Correction: Maturidi shows that vision can occur without those conditions (e.g., seeing shadow, darkness, light) and that conditions can be present without vision (due to veils or substance properties), so conditions are not necessary.

Checkpoint

B) That vision can occur without the usual conditions, and conditions can exist without vision.
C) That only bodies can be seen.
D) That the vision of God is exactly like human vision.

Key Takeaway
The third and fourth arguments together demolish the claim that vision requires specific physical conditions, clearing a rational path for the possibility of seeing Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Maturidi argues that vision can occur without physical conditions. What spiritual conditions might be necessary for you to be among those who see Allah? Are you cultivating those?
?
The source uses the analogy that Allah can be Agent and Knower without being a body. In what ways do you still think of Allah in limited, human-like terms? What steps can you take to purify your understanding of Him?

The Argument from Distance and Smallness
Imam Maturidi refutes the objection that distance or smallness prevents vision, showing that these are relative to the observer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that distance and smallness are not absolute barriers to vision; rather, the veil (al-ḥijāb) is relative, and its removal allows vision even for the same object.

Core Idea
Distance and smallness are relative veils, not absolute barriers to sight.

Imam Maturidi addresses a common objection: how can we see Allah if He is far or subtle? He responds by pointing out that what is hidden from one observer may be visible to another. The key is the veil (al-ḥijāb), and if it is removed, vision becomes possible. This principle has implications for the vision of Allah in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once carefully, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by a conclusion.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'البعد', 'الدقة', and 'الحجاب'. Understand how each functions in the argument.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is important for theology: it defends the possibility of seeing Allah without being bound by physical limitations.
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' shows reliance on God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن البعد الذي يحجبنا والدقة يجوز أن يبلغه بصر غيرنا؛ فصار ارتفاع الرؤية بالحجاب فإذا ارتفع جاز. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
However, the distance that veils us and the smallness [of an object] may be reached by the sight of another; thus the prevention of vision is due to the veil, so if the veil is removed, vision becomes possible. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific objection: that distance and small size prevent vision. Maturidi responds that these are relative. What veils one observer may not veil another. For example, the Angel of Death can see beings all over the earth, which human sight cannot. Therefore, the fact that something is veiled from humans does not mean it is absolutely invisible. The barrier is the veil (al-ḥijāb), and if that veil is removed, vision is possible. The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' expresses reliance on God.

Key Terms
البعد (al-buʿd)
الدقة (al-diqqa)
الحجاب (al-ḥijāb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Distance and smallness are absolute physical barriers that prevent vision for any observer.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that distance and smallness are relative; they may veil one observer but not another. The true barrier is the veil (al-ḥijāb) placed by God, and He can remove it.

Checkpoint

C) The size of the object.
D) The material composition of the object.

Key Takeaway
Distance and smallness are not absolute barriers; the veil is relative, and God can remove it to allow vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Maturidi's Critique of al-Ka'bi and Proof of Bodily Visibility
Maturidi criticizes al-Ka'bi's method and proves bodies are visible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that every body is visible in principle and how he refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that only bodies can be seen.

Core Idea
Visibility is not limited to bodies; the only invisible thing is an accident.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi directly confronts the Mu'tazili scholar al-Ka'bi, who argued that God cannot be seen because He is not a body. Maturidi turns the argument around, showing that every body is visible, and the only thing not visible per se is an accident. This is crucial for the Sunni affirmation of ru'ya (seeing Allah) in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Let Maturidi's concise argument sink in before analyzing.
The passage is dense; pay attention to every phrase.
2
Identify Maturidi's Criticism
Find where Maturidi faults al-Ka'bi's method. Notice that al-Ka'bi based his reasoning only on bodies and did not test his sight on other things.
Maturidi says al-Ka'bi's approach is an 'estimation' (تقدير), not a thorough investigation.
3
Understand the Positive Proof
Maturidi states that every body is seen in principle, even if veiled by smallness or distance. The Angel of Death example shows that these veils can be removed for a different observer. This establishes that veils are not causes of non-visibility but causes of veiling.
The key point: visibility is inherent, veiling is accidental.
4
Grasp the Final Conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the only thing not seen by its own essence is an accident (ʿaraḍ); every entity (ʿayn) is seen. Thus, those who deny vision of God must prove He is not a visible entity, using the burden of proof.
This reinforces the Sunni position that God can be seen in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن الذي يقوله الكعبي تقدير برؤية الأجسام، ولم يمتحن بصره بغير الأجسام والأعراض أن كيف سبيل الرؤية له. وبعد، فإن كل جسم يُرى وإن كان الدقة والبعد يحجبان، فيجوز ارتفاعهما عن بصر غير فيرى؛ على ما يرى ملك الموت من بأطراف الأرض ووسطها مما لو اعتبر ذلك ببصر البشر لما احتمل الإدراك. فثبت أن الذي قدّر به ليس هو سبب تعريف ما يبصر، ولكن سبب تعريف ما يحجب به البصر، فإذا ارتفع رأى مع ما كان المنفي رؤيته لذاته عرضاً، وإلا فكل جسم يُرى. فإن لزم إنكار الرؤية لما ليس بجسم أو لما لا يُرى إلا بما ذكر ليلزم الإقرار به؛ لأن الذي لا يُرى لذاته هو العرض، وإلا فكل عين يُرى. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Afterwards, what al-Kaʿbī says is an estimation based on the vision of bodies, and he did not test his sight with other than bodies and accidents as to how the path of vision is for him. Afterwards, every body is seen, even if smallness and distance veil it, so it is possible that they be removed from the sight of another and it sees, just as the Angel of Death sees those at the ends of the earth and its middle, such that if one were to consider that with human sight, it would not be capable of perception. Thus it is established that what he estimated by is not the cause of defining what is seen, but rather the cause of defining what veils the sight; so if the veil is removed, it sees along with what was negated from being seen in itself as an accident; otherwise, every body is seen. For if the denial of vision for what is not a body or for what is not seen except by what was mentioned is necessary, then it is necessary to affirm it; because what is not seen in itself is the accident, otherwise every entity is seen. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a critique of al-Ka'bī's method and a positive proof. Maturidi first points out that al-Ka'bī's reasoning is based solely on the vision of bodies; he did not consider whether other things (like accidents) can be seen. The author then states that every body is visible in principle, even if smallness or distance may veil it from a particular observer. These veils can be removed for another observer, as seen in the case of the Angel of Death who sees all creatures. Therefore, the factors that al-Ka'bī used to deny vision (distance, smallness) are not the cause of visibility but rather the cause of veiling. Once the veil is lifted, the object is seen, regardless of whether it is a body or not. The only thing not seen inherently is an accident (ʿaraḍ); everything else (every entity/ʿayn) is seen. Thus, the burden is on the one who denies vision to prove that God is not a visible entity, not on the one who affirms it.

Key Terms
الكعبي (al-Kaʿbī)
الجسم (al-jism)
العرض (al-ʿaraḍ)
ملك الموت (Malak al-Mawt)
العين (al-ʿayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ka'bī's argument that God cannot be seen because He is not a body is valid.
Correction: Maturidi shows that visibility is not limited to bodies; the only thing not seen per se is an accident. Therefore, God, not being an accident, can be seen.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the only thing that is not seen by its own essence?
A) A body (jism)
B) A substance (jawhar)
C) An accident (ʿaraḍ)
D) The Angel of Death

Key Takeaway
Every body is visible in principle; the only thing not seen per se is an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that al-Ka'bī relied only on his own limited sight experiment. What area of your life are you basing solely on your own limited experience instead of trusting the broader testimony of revelation and the unseen (like the Angel of Death)? Name one specific belief or practice you will adjust starting tomorrow.
?
If the only thing not seen per se is an accident, and you believe you will see God in the hereafter, how should this certainty change your worship today? What is one thing you can do to live more consciously of that coming vision?
?
The Angel of Death perceives across immense distances that are impossible for humans. How does this truth challenge your comfort with the unseen aspects of your faith? Will you let it increase your awe or your fear? What is one tangible step you can take today to respond to that reality?

Why Vision is Not in This World
Maturidi refutes the objection from this world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the non-occurrence of seeing God in this world is not due to impossibility but because it would negate the purpose of this world as a place of trial.

Core Idea
This world is a place of trial, not of vision.

Al-Ka'bī objected that since God is not seen in this world, He cannot be seen in the afterlife. Maturidi responds by explaining the nature of this world as a realm of trial and burden, where actual vision would undermine that purpose. Let's see his precise words.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that Maturidi first states the objection, then gives his response.
The objection is implicit in the phrase 'عارض بأمر الدنيا'.
2
Identify Maturidi's key claim
He says it is not impossible (لا يحال) but would nullify trial and remove burden. Understand why that is.
3
Reflect on the purpose of this world
Note the phrase 'والدنيا لهما خلقت' — this world was created for trial and burden. How does that relate to the deferral of vision?
This world is a test, so complete unveiling is for the next world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارض بأمر الدنيا. ولا يُحال ذلك، ولكن يُسقط المحنة ويرفع الكلفة، والدنيا لهما خلقت . ثم ذكر في أمر موسى عليه السلام أن ذلك [كان] على علم الإحاطة بالآيات. وقد بينا فساد ذلك. وما ذلك العلم بالذي يَسأل؛ وهو رسول بُعث إلى ما به نجاة الخلق؛
And he [al-Kaʿbī] objected by [the matter of] this world. But that is not impossible, rather it nullifies trial (al-miḥna) and removes burden (al-kulfa), and this world was created for them [trial and burden]. Then he mentioned regarding the matter of Moses, peace be upon him, that that [was] based on comprehensive knowledge of the signs. And we have already shown the corruption of that. And that knowledge is not [the kind] that asks; and he is a messenger sent to that by which the creation is saved.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage addresses two objections. First, al-Ka'bī objected that God is not seen in this world, so why would He be seen in the next? Maturidi responds that the non-occurrence in this world is not due to impossibility but because if vision occurred in this world, it would remove the trial (miḥna) and burden (kulfa) that this world is meant for. This world is created as a place of testing, so the actual vision of God is deferred to the afterlife. Second, the objector brought up the story of Moses asking to see God, suggesting that if vision were possible, Moses would have seen. Maturidi refutes that Moses' request was based on comprehensive knowledge (ʿilm al-iḥāṭa) of the signs, meaning he already knew that vision was possible in principle but asked for a specific kind of manifestation. The author claims to have previously shown the corruption of that interpretation. He then adds that such knowledge is not the kind that asks; Moses was a messenger sent for the salvation of creation, implying that his request had a pedagogical purpose, not that he doubted the possibility.

Key Terms
الدنيا (al-dunyā)
المحنة (al-miḥna)
الكلفة (al-kulfa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is not seen in this world, then He cannot be seen at all.
Correction: Maturidi explains that the absence of vision in this world is not due to impossibility but because it would remove the trial and burden that characterize this world.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is God not seen in this world?
A) Because it is impossible.
B) Because it would nullify the trial and burden of this world.
C) Because only prophets can see God.
D) Because God does not reveal Himself.

Key Takeaway
The absence of divine vision in this world is a matter of divine wisdom, not impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If this world is a place of trial and God does not show Himself to you now, what does that mean for how you should approach the hardships you face today? Will you grumble for not seeing, or will you embrace the test with patience and hope for vision tomorrow?
?
You believe that seeing God in the afterlife is real, but are you living today as if the unseen is more precious than the seen? What would you change in your daily worship and struggles if you truly assigned greater weight to what is deferred?
?
The objection says, 'If I don't see God now, it must be impossible.' Has a hidden version of this objection crept into your heart—making you doubt God's love or presence because you don't perceive Him directly? How will you remind yourself that the very silence of God in this world is a mercy designed to purify your faith?

The Story of Musa and the Request to See God
Maturidi refutes the objection from Moses' request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Moses' request to see God was based on comprehensive knowledge and served a pedagogical purpose, not ignorance.

Core Idea
Moses' request was a sign of comprehensive knowledge, not doubt.

The objector also cites the story of Moses asking to see God, implying that if vision were possible, Moses would have seen. Maturidi clarifies that Moses' request was not born of ignorance but was grounded in comprehensive knowledge (ʿilm al-iḥāṭa) and had a purpose related to his prophetic mission.

Read the passage about Musa

ʿIlm al-iḥāṭa means encompassing knowledge.

It was a pedagogical act, not a sign of doubt.

The Linguistic Principle for Interpreting Verses of Vision
Maturidi states a linguistic principle and applies it to ru'ya verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic principle that determines when a verse about seeing Allah can be taken literally and when it is metaphorical.

Core Idea
Context decides whether 'seeing' means literal vision or something else.

Maturidi begins by laying down a fundamental rule of language: when speech deals with a well-known matter or the intended meaning is clearly indicated by the context, the words are turned from their literal sense. He then applies this rule to the verse about the believers looking at their Lord and to the story of Moses. This lesson focuses on that principle and its application.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening principle
Read the Arabic and translation for the principle: 'If speech is about a familiar matter or the intended meaning is combined, it is diverted from its literal meaning, otherwise not.' Notice the two conditions that allow departure from literal meaning.
2
Examine the Qur'anic examples
Study the examples given from the Qur'an where 'see' (رأيت) is used metaphorically, like 'Do you not see how your Lord spread the shade'. Understand how context shifts meaning.
These examples show that 'seeing' can mean understanding or witnessing an action.
3
Apply to the story of Moses
Read how the principle applies to Musa's request 'Show me, that I may look at You'. Maturidi argues that the context makes it clear this was a request for a specific type of vision, not literal sight.
This refutes the Mu'tazili claim that the verse proves vision is impossible.
4
Connect to the ru'ya verse
Consider how this principle allows the verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة' to be taken literally for the believers in the afterlife, because there is no contextual reason to divert from literal meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا ما قال في قوله: وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: والأصل في الكلام أنه إذا كان على أمر معهود أو يقرن به المقصود إليه صُرف عن حقيقته، وإلا لا. وذلك نحو قوله: ألم تر إلى ربك كيف مد الظل، وألم تر كيف فعل ربك... وأصله أن من قال: رأيت فلانًا أو نظرت إلى فلان لم يحتمل غير ذاته؛ وإذا قال: رأيته يقول كذا ويفعل كذا إنه لا يريد به رؤية ذاته، فمثله أمر قصة موسى عليه السلام وهذه الآية. ثم الأصل أن من تأمل الذي ذكر عرف أنه مشتهي النحلة؛ لأنه لم يذكر المعنى الذي له يجب أن تكون الرؤية بتلك الشرائط، إنما أخبر أنه كذلك وجد، وهو قول المشبهة، أنه وجد كل فاعل في الشاهد جسما، وكذا كل عالم، فيجب مثله في الغائب. ثم ذكر معنى رؤية الجسم ولم يذكر معنى رؤية غير الجسم حتى يكون له دليلاً. وبعد، فإنه نفي الرؤية بالدقة والبعد، وهما زائلان عن الله سبحانه. ثم احتج بامتداح الله بقوله: ولا تدركه الأبصار وهو يدرك الأبصار، وقال: لا يجوز أن يزول. فمثله عليه في قوله: خالق كل شيء، وقوله: وهو على كل شيء قدير، فلا يجوز أن يزول. ثم قد وصف الله بالرؤية على طريق إسقاط ما ذكر، فثبت أن ذلك طريق لا يؤدى عن كنه ما به الرؤية.
And we have explained what he said regarding His statement: 'Faces that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord' (75:22-23). The jurist (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The principle in speech is that if it is about a familiar matter or the intended meaning is combined with it, it is diverted from its literal meaning, otherwise not. That is like His statement: 'Do you not see how your Lord spread the shade' (25:45) and 'Do you not see how your Lord did...' And the principle is that when someone says 'I saw so-and-so' or 'I looked at so-and-so', it does not imply anything other than his essence; but if he says 'I saw him saying such-and-such and doing such-and-such', he does not intend thereby the vision of his essence. The same applies to the story of Moses (peace be upon him) and this verse. Then the principle is that whoever ponders what was mentioned knows that he is a follower of the Bee Sect (Nahla), because he did not mention the meaning for which vision must have those conditions; he only informed that he found it so. And that is the statement of the Anthropomorphists, that he found every agent in the witnessed world to be a body, and likewise every knower, so the like must apply to the unseen. Then he mentioned the meaning of vision of a body and did not mention the meaning of vision of a non-body, so that it would be a proof for him. Afterwards, he negated vision by fineness and distance, and both are removed from Allah, exalted. Then he argued by Allah's praise in His statement: 'Vision perceives Him not, but He perceives all vision' (6:103), and said: It is not permissible that it should cease. The same applies to His statement: 'Creator of everything' (6:102), and His statement: 'And He is over all things competent' (11:4), so it is not permissible that it should cease. Then He described vision for Allah in the manner of dropping what was mentioned, so it is established that that is a path that does not convey the essence of what vision is.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili argument against the vision of Allah. Maturidi begins by recalling the earlier discussion about the verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة'. He then cites the jurist (Abu Bakr al-Sayrafi or similar) who states a linguistic principle: when speech is about a well-known matter or the intended meaning is indicated by context, the literal meaning is set aside. Examples are given from the Qur'an where 'رأيت' is used metaphorically. The same principle applies to the story of Moses: when he said 'أرني أنظر إليك', it was about a specific request, not literal vision. Then Maturidi critiques his opponent (Ka'bi) for being like the anthropomorphists, because he analogizes from the witnessed world to the unseen without considering the difference in kind. Ka'bi's proof is based on observing bodies and applying that to Allah, which is invalid. He also negates vision based on 'fineness and distance' which do not apply to Allah. Finally, Ka'bi argues from the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' that vision is impossible, but Maturidi counters that just as the attributes of being Creator and All-Powerful are eternal, so is the negation of vision eternal—yet the Qur'an also affirms vision in other verses, so the proper understanding is that vision is possible without the conditions of bodily vision.

Key Terms
الرؤية (ru'ya)
الأصل في الكلام (principle in speech)
الحقيقة (literal meaning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any mention of 'seeing' in the Qur'an must be metaphorical because Allah cannot be seen.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the linguistic principle allows literal meaning when context does not indicate otherwise. The verse about the believers has no such context, so it can be literal.

Checkpoint
According to the linguistic principle quoted by Maturidi, when is speech diverted from its literal meaning?
A) Only when there is a clear contradiction with reason.
B) When it is about a familiar matter or the intended meaning is combined with it.
C) Whenever the speech is figurative.
D) Only in poetic contexts.

Key Takeaway
The linguistic principle shows that the ru'ya verse can be literal because no contextual factor requires it to be metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses that describe Allah's attributes in seemingly human terms (e.g., 'coming' or 'sitting'), do you instinctively reinterpret them metaphorically? Where does this habit come from, and how does the principle from this lesson challenge you to pause before reinterpreting?
?
The principle says 'I saw Zayd' refers to his essence, but 'I saw him saying' refers to his action. In your relationship with Allah, do you primarily seek to witness His actions in your life, or do you truly long to see His essence? Which one would change your worship tomorrow?
?
If the highest reward for believers is the vision of Allah (ru'ya), what does that say about your current pursuit? Are you content with second-hand knowledge and signs, or does your heart ache for the sight of Him? What is one concrete step you can take today to cultivate that longing?

Refuting Anthropomorphic Analogies and the Negation of Vision
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazili analogy and the proof from negating vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazili argument that uses analogical reasoning from the witnessed world and the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' to deny vision of Allah.

Core Idea
Comparing Allah to bodies is invalid; the negation of vision does not preclude a different kind of sight.

After establishing the linguistic principle, Maturidi turns to critique his opponent's methodology. He shows that the Mu'tazili reasoning assumes that vision requires a body, which is an anthropomorphic fallacy. He then addresses the key proof from 'Vision perceives Him not', arguing that just as other divine attributes are eternal, this negation is eternal, but it does not rule out a vision that transcends physical limitations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's error
Read the section where Maturidi says the opponent is like the anthropomorphists (المشبهة) because he assumes that everything observed in the world must apply to Allah.
The opponent infers from bodies in the witnessed world to Allah in the unseen.
2
Critique of the analogy
Notice that the opponent only mentions the meaning of vision for a body, not for a non-body. This is a flawed proof.
3
Refute the distance/fineness argument
Understand that the opponent rejects vision due to fineness and distance, but these do not apply to Allah.
4
Respond to 'لا تدركه الأبصار'
Learn how Maturidi counters the argument from Surah al-An'am: just as Allah is eternally the Creator and All-Powerful, so also the negation of vision is eternal, but this does not conflict with the possibility of a vision that Allah Himself creates in the believers.
Maturidi points out that the same verse that negates vision also affirms Allah's encompassing perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
الغائب (unseen)
الشاهد (witnessed world)
المشبهة (Anthropomorphists)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' absolutely and forever negates any possibility of seeing Allah.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the negation is eternal just like other attributes, but it does not rule out a vision that is not by physical eyes. The same verse also says 'وهو يدرك الأبصار', indicating that Allah's perception is different.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi compare the Mu'tazili opponent to the anthropomorphists (المشبهة)?
A) Because the opponent believes Allah has a body.
B) Because the opponent uses analogy from the witnessed world to the unseen without distinguishing between the two.
C) Because the opponent denies all divine attributes.
D) Because the opponent claims that Allah can be seen with physical eyes.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazili use of the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' by showing it does not negate a vision that transcends physical limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine seeing Allah in the afterlife, do you unconsciously picture it like seeing a physical object? How does this lesson challenge that hidden assumption—and what would it mean for you to let go of that mental image?
?
This passage reveals that even learned theologians can build elaborate arguments on flawed analogies from their own experience. Where in your own understanding of Allah's attributes might you be relying on worldly comparisons without realizing it? Will you actively search for those hidden analogies today?
?
If the vision of Allah in the Hereafter is not physical, then what kind of 'seeing' is it? Are you truly content to trust Allah's promise of a vision that transcends everything you know—or does your heart secretly demand a vision that fits your current understanding? What step will you take to surrender that demand to divine reality?

Seeing Allah Without How: The Bila Kayf Principle
Maturidi answers the question of how Allah is seen with 'bila kayf'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theologians affirm the vision of Allah while denying any modality ('bila kayf') and how this preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah is seen, but without 'how' — because 'how-ness' belongs only to bodies.

After establishing that Allah can be seen in the afterlife, the question naturally arises: 'How is He seen?' The classical answer is 'bila kayf' — without modality. This lesson unpacks that answer, showing that it is not a refusal to answer but a precise theological commitment to Allah's transcendence above all creaturely attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Question and Answer
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the direct Q&A structure: 'If it is said: How is He seen? It is said: Without how.'
The formula 'bila kayf' is a hallmark of Sunni theology.
2
Understand the Reason for Bila Kayf
The passage explains that 'how-ness' (al-kayfiyya) belongs only to beings that have form (sura). Since Allah is not a body, He cannot be described by a 'how'.
This safeguards tanzih (transcendence).
3
Identify the Negated Attributes
The passage lists many pairs of creaturely attributes that do not apply to Allah's vision: standing/sitting, light/darkness, inside/outside, etc. Read through them and consider why each is denied.
These negations reinforce that the vision is unlike any physical sight.
4
Reflect on the Conclusion
The passage ends by affirming that Allah is beyond anything imagination or reason can grasp. This is the ultimate point: the vision is real but incomprehensible.
This is not agnosticism but humble acknowledgment of divine majesty.
5
Challenge the Misconception
Some think 'bila kayf' is a cop-out. But it is a precise theological method: affirm the attribute (vision) while negating modality. This prevents anthropomorphism without denying the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: كيف يُرى؟ قيل: بلا كيف؛ إذ الكيفية تكون لذى صورة؛ بل يُرى بلا وصف قيام وقعود، واتكاء وتعلق واتصال وانفصال، ومقابلة ومدابرة، وقصير وطويل، ونور وظلمة، وساكن ومتحرك، ومماس ومباين، وخارج وداخل، ولا معنى يأخذه الوهم أو يقدره العقل، لتعاليه عن ذلك.
If it is said: How is He seen? It is said: Without how; for 'how-ness' belongs to that which has form; rather, He is seen without description of standing or sitting, leaning or clinging, connection or separation, facing or turning away, short or tall, light or darkness, stationary or moving, touching or being separate, outside or inside, and no meaning that imagination grasps or reason estimates, because of His transcendence above that.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer unit on the modality of seeing Allah. After affirming that Allah can be seen, the question arises: 'How is He seen?' The answer is 'Bila kayf' (without how). The reason given is that 'how-ness' (al-kayfiyya) belongs only to beings that have form (sura). Allah is not a body and has no form, so He is seen without any modality. The passage then enumerates a series of creaturely attributes that do not apply to the vision of Allah: standing, sitting, leaning, clinging, connection, separation, facing, turning away, short, tall, light, darkness, stationary, moving, touching, being separate, outside, inside. Finally, it states that Allah is beyond anything that imagination can grasp or reason can estimate. This is a classic Ash'ari/Maturidi formulation: affirmation of vision without modality or resemblance.

Key Terms
بلا كيف (bila kayf)
الكيفية (how-ness)
الصورة (form)
التعالي (transcendence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'bila kayf' is a cop-out or an admission of ignorance.
Correction: Bila kayf is a precise theological method: it affirms the reality of the attribute (vision) while denying any modality that would imply anthropomorphism. It is not ignorance but a positive assertion of transcendence.

Checkpoint
Why is the vision of Allah described as 'bila kayf'?
A) Because 'how-ness' belongs only to beings with form, and Allah is not a body.
B) Because we cannot know anything about Allah.
C) Because the vision is metaphorical.
D) Because Allah is not visible.

Key Takeaway
The vision of Allah is real but without modality—affirmed by faith, not comprehended by reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of seeing Allah, do you unconsciously picture a form? How does the 'bila kayf' principle challenge your instinct to visualize the Unseen? Write down one specific mental image you must now reject.
?
The passage says Allah's vision is beyond 'what imagination grasps or reason estimates'. Are you willing to accept a reality that your mind cannot fully comprehend? What does that require of your faith today?
?
If you cannot describe 'how' Allah is seen, does that make the vision less real to you? Examine your heart: do you demand intellectual satisfaction before belief? Would you affirm the vision without understanding the modality, submitting to divine transcendence?
