The Problem of Distinguishing Divine Creation in Mu'tazila Thought
Maturidi critiques Mu'tazila for lacking a divine distinguishing mark.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazila view fails to provide a distinguishing mark for God's creation, thereby collapsing into a position similar to dualism and naturalism.

Core Idea
Without a divine mark, Mu'tazila cannot prove God's exclusive creation.

This passage continues Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazila. He argues that on their view, God did not provide any evidence to distinguish His creation from that of others, leading to an absurdity where the universe could be the work of multiple agents. Maturidi then invokes a Qur'anic verse to highlight the flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the density of theological terminology.
2
Identify the two missing elements
Maturidi points out two things God did not do according to the Mu'tazila: provide a proof of His bringing into existence (ihdath) and a proof of His pre-eternity (qidam). Also, He did not distinguish the accidents He creates from those created by others.
This lack of distinction is the core of the critique.
3
Understand the analogy with dualists
Maturidi shows that without a distinguishing mark, the Mu'tazila position is similar to the dualists and naturalists who claim things come to be by themselves without a maker's proof. This is labeled a sign of incapacity ('ajz).
The analogy exposes the problem of attributing effects to multiple causes.
4
Analyze the Qur'anic argument
Maturidi quotes Qur'an 23:91, which argues that if multiple gods existed, each would take away what he created. He then shows that on the Mu'tazila view, God also did not 'go away' with His creation because He placed no mark on it, rendering the verse ineffective for proving monotheism on their premises.
The verse is used as a reductio against the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن الله جل ثناؤه لم يقم على قولهم حجة إحداثه سوى العالم، ولا حجة قدمه. ثم لم يفصل بين الأعراض التي هي صنع غيره وبين التي هي صنعه؛ لأن الاجتماع والزوال ونحو ذلك قد يوجد في العيان ولا يُرى له الجامع المحرك ؛ ويُحتمل أن يكون ذلك لغيره لا له وإن لم يعاين، إذ هو نظير ما يعاين منه. فصار ذلك كقول الثنوية وأصحاب الطبائع : تكون الأشياء بها من غير أن أقام كل منها دليل صنعه بما يُفصل به من صنع غيره؛ وذلك آية العجز . والمعنى الذي احتج الله به على كون العالم بكليته به أن قال : إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق [المؤمنون، ۹۱/۲۳]؛ فعلى قولهم فالله تعالى أيضاً لم يذهب بما خلق، لأنه لم يجعل عليه علما.
And furthermore, God, exalted be His praise, did not establish according to their saying a proof of His bringing into existence other than the world, nor a proof of His pre-eternity. Then He did not distinguish between the accidents that are the making of other than Him and those that are His making; because combination and movement and the like may exist in observable reality and no combiner or mover is seen for it. And it is possible that that is from other than Him, not from Him, even if not witnessed, since it is analogous to what is witnessed from Him. So that became like the saying of the dualists and the naturalists: that things come to be by themselves without each of them having established a proof of its making by which it is distinguished from the making of another; and that is a sign of incapacity. And the meaning by which God argued for the world in its entirety being from Him is that He said: 'If there were therein gods besides Allah, then verily each would have gone away with that which he had created' [23:91]; so according to their saying, God also did not go away with what He created, because He did not place a mark upon it.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the Mu'tazila. Maturidi argues that God did not provide a separate proof for His bringing about the world (ihdath) or His pre-eternity (qidam) on their view. Moreover, God did not distinguish between accidents that are His making and those of others. For example, combination and movement are observed in reality without a visible combiner or mover, so it is possible that such effects come from other than God, even if not directly seen, since they are analogous to observed cases. This leads to a position similar to dualists and naturalists, who claim things come to be by themselves without evidence of a specific maker, which is a sign of incapacity ('ajz). Maturidi then invokes the Qur'anic verse 23:91, which argues that if there were multiple gods, each would have taken away what he created. On the Mu'tazila view, God also did not 'go away' with what He created, because He placed no distinguishing mark on His creation. Thus, the verse does not prove monotheism on their premises.

Key Terms
حجة
إحداث
قدم
أعراض
الجامع المحرك
آية العجز
ذهب بما خلق
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is denying that God creates the world.
Correction: He is not denying creation; he is showing that the Mu'tazila view fails to provide evidence that God alone creates, leading to an unacceptable equivalence with polytheism.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi label as a sign of incapacity ('ajz) in the Mu'tazila view?
A) That God did not create the world
B) That things come to be without a distinguishing mark of their maker
C) That God is not eternal
D) That the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
Without a divine distinguishing mark, the Mu'tazila view collapses into a position indistinguishable from dualism and naturalism, and even the Qur'anic argument fails to prove monotheism on their premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the events of your daily life—your health, your provisions, your successes and failures—do you genuinely see each as a direct act of God, or do you unconsciously attribute them to natural causes or your own effort? What would change if you trained yourself to look for God's 'mark' in every circumstance, even those that seem ordinary? This lesson exposes the danger of a worldview that loses God's distinct handiwork. Where in your life have you blurred that distinction, and how will you restore it starting today?
?
The verse (23:91) says that if there were multiple gods, each would take away what he created. This means that what belongs to God should be unmistakably His. Yet you may have areas of your life—your career, relationships, or even your worship—that you treat as belonging partly to yourself or to others. Is there a part of your heart that still claims ownership over something that should be wholly surrendered to God? What is one concrete step you can take this week to let God fully 'take' that creation back from you?

God's Power and Human Action: Maturidi's Refutation
Maturidi dismisses an objection about divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the flawed logic in arguing that human action can override God's decree, and Maturidi's dismissal of such reasoning.

Core Idea
Attributing greater power to a servant than to Allah is absurd.

Maturidi begins by presenting an objection: if God appoints terms for His servants, yet a servant can kill another before his time, it seems God cannot prevent the action, implying the servant's power is greater. Maturidi immediately declares God exalted above this description. This lesson examines the objection and Maturidi's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first line of the Arabic passage where the objection is described. Notice the claim that the servant's power seems to exceed God's.
The objection uses a hypothetical scenario.
2
Identify Maturidi's response
Find the phrase 'جل ربنا وتعالى عن هذا الوصف' – here Maturidi dismisses the objection by glorifying God. Understand that he does not engage in detailed rebuttal but rather states the absurdity.
This reflects the principle of tanzih.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why is this objection fallacious? Consider that God's decree is not thwarted by human actions; rather, human actions are within God's power.
This connects to the broader theological debate on free will and predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضرب لعبيده مددا و [ضرب] لهم أرزاقاً قدرها لهم ثم يجيئ عبد فيقتله قبل استيفائه مدته وإنجازه وعدته على بقاء قدرته والله تعالى لا يقدر على منع العبد مما هو فعله يريد الوفاء به على غير منع القدرة عنه فصارت قدرة العبد أعظم ومشيئته أنفذ جل ربنا وتعالى عن هذا الوصف. والثنوية تزعم أن النور وقع في حبس الظلمة ووثاقها من الوجه الذي همّ به الصلاح ودفع شرها عن جوهره وكذلك الظلمة على قول من يجعل ابتداء القدح منها فأخطئاً جميعا لما خرج فعلهما على خلاف ما أرادا وصار كل واحد منهما في جهد الخلاص من يد الآخر فلزمهم القول في النور بالخطأ وبالجهل والعجز أما الخطأ لما لم يكن عاقبته على ما أراد والجهل لما لم يكن علم أنه يبقى في وثاق عدوه وأما العجز هو أنه في جهد الخلاص وتدبيره فلم يتهيأ له. وكذلك قول المعتزلة إن الله لم يقو كافرا ولا أحدا إلا ليطيعه ولا ملك أحدا شيءا إلا ليشكر ولا خلق أحدا إلا ليخضع له وذلك يريد وله فعل ما فعل ولو كان منه غير هذا كان يكون سفيها ظالما ثم لم يكن بجميع ما أعطى أعداءه ما أراد وله أخطاء وذلك الخطأ المعروف أن لا يخرج الأمر على ما يريده ثم القدرة على ما لو فعل لكان خارجا عن علمه ثم ما يثبت من الفعل فيما بعد هو فعل الله في الحقيقة في عدم المنع منه فأوجب قولهم مضاهاة من ذكرت بكل وجوه المذمة.
He appointed for His servants terms and [appointed] for them provisions He decreed for them, then a servant comes and kills him before the completion of his term and the fulfillment of his promise, while his power remains, and God Almighty is not able to prevent the servant from what is his action, meaning to fulfill it, without preventing power from him, so the servant's power became greater and his will more effective. Glory be to our Lord and exalted is He above this description. And the Dualists claim that the Light fell into the prison of Darkness and its bonds in the way that it intended goodness and repel its evil from its essence, and likewise Darkness according to the one who makes the beginning of the defect from it. So both erred, because their action turned out contrary to what they wanted, and each of them became in the effort of deliverance from the hand of the other; so it becomes necessary for them to attribute to the Light error, ignorance, and incapacity. As for error, because its consequence was not as it intended; ignorance, because it did not know that it would remain in the bondage of its enemy; and as for incapacity, it is that it is in the effort of deliverance and its planning, but it could not achieve it. And likewise the saying of the Mu'tazila: God did not empower any disbeliever or anyone except to obey Him, nor did He give anyone any ownership except to be grateful, nor did He create anyone except to submit to Him; and that is what He intends, and He has the action He did; and if it were otherwise from Him, He would be foolish and unjust. Then He did not achieve with all that He gave to His enemies what He intended; so He erred. And that error is well-known: that the matter does not turn out as He intends; then the power [is something] that if He had done it would be outside His knowledge; then what is established of action afterwards—it is God's action [in reality]—in the [absence of] prevention from it. So their saying necessitated resemblance to those I have mentioned in all aspects of blame.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two groups: the Dualists (Thunawiyya) and the Mu'tazilites. Maturidi begins by describing an objection: God sets terms and provisions for His servants, yet a servant can kill another before the appointed time, seemingly frustrating God's decree. The objector claims God cannot prevent the servant, implying the servant's power is greater. Maturidi immediately declares God exalted above this. Next, he examines the Dualists, who believe in two eternal principles of Light and Darkness. They claim Light entered Darkness to bring good and repel evil, but ended up trapped. Maturidi shows this entails attributing error, ignorance, and incapacity to the Light (God). Then he addresses the Mu'tazilites, who assert that God only empowers for obedience, gives ownership for gratitude, and creates for submission; otherwise God would be foolish and unjust. They must admit God failed to achieve His intention with His enemies, so He erred. Maturidi concludes their saying makes God resemble the flawed beings mentioned earlier. The entire argument defends God's absolute power and knowledge against any suggestion of deficiency.

Key Terms
قدرة
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human actions can frustrate God's decree and make God powerless.
Correction: Maturidi shows this is a false conclusion; God's power is not limited by human action.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say after presenting the objection about the servant killing before the term?
A) He argues that the servant's power is limited.
B) He glorifies God and declares Him exalted above such description.
C) He concedes that God cannot prevent the action.
D) He says the servant's power is a gift from God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi asserts that attributing greater power to a servant than to Allah is a theological error; God's power is absolute and beyond comparison.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your choices somehow challenge God's control over your life? How does Maturidi's exclamation 'Glory be to our Lord and exalted is He above this description' confront that feeling? What do you need to surrender today?
?
When you consider your own independence in action, do you recognize that all power ultimately belongs to God? What would change if you truly internalized that?
?
Are there areas of your life where you unconsciously assume your will supersedes God's decree? How can you realign your understanding right now, starting with a specific thought or habit?

Refutation of Dualists: The Deficiencies of Light
Maturidi refutes Dualists via necessary defects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi demonstrates that Dualist cosmology logically attributes error, ignorance, and incapacity to the Light principle.

Core Idea
Dualism leads to a deficient deity who errs and is trapped.

The Dualists believed in two eternal principles: Light (good) and Darkness (evil). They claimed Light entered Darkness to bring good but ended up trapped. Maturidi shows this story forces one to attribute three defects to Light: error, ignorance, and incapacity. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Dualist claim
Read the beginning of the passage: 'والثنوية تزعم أن النور وقع في حبس الظلمة ووثاقها من الوجه الذي همّ به الصلاح ودفع شرها عن جوهره' – The Light entered Darkness intending goodness but is stuck.
This is a mythological narrative.
2
Identify the three defects
Maturidi points out that since the Light's action did not achieve its intended goal, it committed error; it did not foresee the outcome, so it is ignorant; it cannot escape, so it is incapable. Find the Arabic terms: خطأ، جهل، عجز.
These are the logical consequences of the Dualist story.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the Light is a divine principle, attributing these defects makes it unworthy of worship. Maturidi uses this to refute Dualism entirely.
This argument is by necessary entailment (luzum).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضرب لعبيده مددا و [ضرب] لهم أرزاقاً قدرها لهم ثم يجيئ عبد فيقتله قبل استيفائه مدته وإنجازه وعدته على بقاء قدرته والله تعالى لا يقدر على منع العبد مما هو فعله يريد الوفاء به على غير منع القدرة عنه فصارت قدرة العبد أعظم ومشيئته أنفذ جل ربنا وتعالى عن هذا الوصف. والثنوية تزعم أن النور وقع في حبس الظلمة ووثاقها من الوجه الذي همّ به الصلاح ودفع شرها عن جوهره وكذلك الظلمة على قول من يجعل ابتداء القدح منها فأخطئاً جميعا لما خرج فعلهما على خلاف ما أرادا وصار كل واحد منهما في جهد الخلاص من يد الآخر فلزمهم القول في النور بالخطأ وبالجهل والعجز أما الخطأ لما لم يكن عاقبته على ما أراد والجهل لما لم يكن علم أنه يبقى في وثاق عدوه وأما العجز هو أنه في جهد الخلاص وتدبيره فلم يتهيأ له. وكذلك قول المعتزلة إن الله لم يقو كافرا ولا أحدا إلا ليطيعه ولا ملك أحدا شيءا إلا ليشكر ولا خلق أحدا إلا ليخضع له وذلك يريد وله فعل ما فعل ولو كان منه غير هذا كان يكون سفيها ظالما ثم لم يكن بجميع ما أعطى أعداءه ما أراد وله أخطاء وذلك الخطأ المعروف أن لا يخرج الأمر على ما يريده ثم القدرة على ما لو فعل لكان خارجا عن علمه ثم ما يثبت من الفعل فيما بعد هو فعل الله في الحقيقة في عدم المنع منه فأوجب قولهم مضاهاة من ذكرت بكل وجوه المذمة.
He appointed for His servants terms and [appointed] for them provisions He decreed for them, then a servant comes and kills him before the completion of his term and the fulfillment of his promise, while his power remains, and God Almighty is not able to prevent the servant from what is his action, meaning to fulfill it, without preventing power from him, so the servant's power became greater and his will more effective. Glory be to our Lord and exalted is He above this description. And the Dualists claim that the Light fell into the prison of Darkness and its bonds in the way that it intended goodness and repel its evil from its essence, and likewise Darkness according to the one who makes the beginning of the defect from it. So both erred, because their action turned out contrary to what they wanted, and each of them became in the effort of deliverance from the hand of the other; so it becomes necessary for them to attribute to the Light error, ignorance, and incapacity. As for error, because its consequence was not as it intended; ignorance, because it did not know that it would remain in the bondage of its enemy; and as for incapacity, it is that it is in the effort of deliverance and its planning, but it could not achieve it. And likewise the saying of the Mu'tazila: God did not empower any disbeliever or anyone except to obey Him, nor did He give anyone any ownership except to be grateful, nor did He create anyone except to submit to Him; and that is what He intends, and He has the action He did; and if it were otherwise from Him, He would be foolish and unjust. Then He did not achieve with all that He gave to His enemies what He intended; so He erred. And that error is well-known: that the matter does not turn out as He intends; then the power [is something] that if He had done it would be outside His knowledge; then what is established of action afterwards—it is God's action [in reality]—in the [absence of] prevention from it. So their saying necessitated resemblance to those I have mentioned in all aspects of blame.

The entire argument defends God's absolute power and knowledge against any suggestion of deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi believes in two eternal principles like the Dualists.
Correction: Maturidi uses their own narrative to show its logical flaws, not to affirm it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what three defects does the Dualist narrative necessarily attribute to the Light?
A) خطأ وجهل وعجز
B) كذب وظلم وجهل
C) عجز وفقر وهلاك
D) شر وضلال وفساد

Key Takeaway
Dualism fails because its own narrative implies God is ignorant, erroneous, and powerless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believed that God could make a mistake or be powerless, how would that affect your trust in His promises? Is there any area of your life where you act as if God is limited or unaware of your situation?
?
Maturidi shows that a supposed deity trapped in its own creation is a contradiction. Do you ever treat God as if He is confined by your circumstances or by the evil in the world? How does this lesson call you to a higher view of God's sovereignty?
?
The Dualists' god intended good but failed. Reflect: When you fail to see good outcomes from your efforts, do you subtly doubt God's wisdom or power? What would it mean for you to truly believe that God's will is never thwarted?

Refutation of Mu'tazilites: God's Will and Human Actions
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazilites on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazilite position on God's will leads to attributing error to God.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite theology forces God to be a failure by their own logic.

The Mu'tazilites claimed that God only empowers people to obey, gives ownership to thank, and creates to submit; otherwise God would be foolish and unjust. Maturidi shows that since God's enemies do not obey, this implies God's will was not fulfilled, hence error. This lesson traces his argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the passage: 'إن الله لم يقو كافرا ولا أحدا إلا ليطيعه...' – God only empowers for obedience, etc.
This is a theological premise.
2
Identify the implication
Maturidi says that if God only wills obedience, but disbelievers do not obey, then God's intention was not achieved. This leads to attributing error (خطأ) to God, because His will did not manifest as intended.
Error here means failure to achieve purpose.
3
See the comparison to Dualists
Maturidi concludes that the Mu'tazilite saying makes God resemble the flawed beings mentioned earlier (the Light). This shows how their theology undermines God's perfection.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضرب لعبيده مددا و [ضرب] لهم أرزاقاً قدرها لهم ثم يجيئ عبد فيقتله قبل استيفائه مدته وإنجازه وعدته على بقاء قدرته والله تعالى لا يقدر على منع العبد مما هو فعله يريد الوفاء به على غير منع القدرة عنه فصارت قدرة العبد أعظم ومشيئته أنفذ جل ربنا وتعالى عن هذا الوصف. والثنوية تزعم أن النور وقع في حبس الظلمة ووثاقها من الوجه الذي همّ به الصلاح ودفع شرها عن جوهره وكذلك الظلمة على قول من يجعل ابتداء القدح منها فأخطئاً جميعا لما خرج فعلهما على خلاف ما أرادا وصار كل واحد منهما في جهد الخلاص من يد الآخر فلزمهم القول في النور بالخطأ وبالجهل والعجز أما الخطأ لما لم يكن عاقبته على ما أراد والجهل لما لم يكن علم أنه يبقى في وثاق عدوه وأما العجز هو أنه في جهد الخلاص وتدبيره فلم يتهيأ له. وكذلك قول المعتزلة إن الله لم يقو كافرا ولا أحدا إلا ليطيعه ولا ملك أحدا شيءا إلا ليشكر ولا خلق أحدا إلا ليخضع له وذلك يريد وله فعل ما فعل ولو كان منه غير هذا كان يكون سفيها ظالما ثم لم يكن بجميع ما أعطى أعداءه ما أراد وله أخطاء وذلك الخطأ المعروف أن لا يخرج الأمر على ما يريده ثم القدرة على ما لو فعل لكان خارجا عن علمه ثم ما يثبت من الفعل فيما بعد هو فعل الله في الحقيقة في عدم المنع منه فأوجب قولهم مضاهاة من ذكرت بكل وجوه المذمة.
He appointed for His servants terms and [appointed] for them provisions He decreed for them, then a servant comes and kills him before the completion of his term and the fulfillment of his promise, while his power remains, and God Almighty is not able to prevent the servant from what is his action, meaning to fulfill it, without preventing power from him, so the servant's power became greater and his will more effective. Glory be to our Lord and exalted is He above this description. And the Dualists claim that the Light fell into the prison of Darkness and its bonds in the way that it intended goodness and repel its evil from its essence, and likewise Darkness according to the one who makes the beginning of the defect from it. So both erred, because their action turned out contrary to what they wanted, and each of them became in the effort of deliverance from the hand of the other; so it becomes necessary for them to attribute to the Light error, ignorance, and incapacity. As for error, because its consequence was not as it intended; ignorance, because it did not know that it would remain in the bondage of its enemy; and as for incapacity, it is that it is in the effort of deliverance and its planning, but it could not achieve it. And likewise the saying of the Mu'tazila: God did not empower any disbeliever or anyone except to obey Him, nor did He give anyone any ownership except to be grateful, nor did He create anyone except to submit to Him; and that is what He intends, and He has the action He did; and if it were otherwise from Him, He would be foolish and unjust. Then He did not achieve with all that He gave to His enemies what He intended; so He erred. And that error is well-known: that the matter does not turn out as He intends; then the power [is something] that if He had done it would be outside His knowledge; then what is established of action afterwards—it is God's action [in reality]—in the [absence of] prevention from it. So their saying necessitated resemblance to those I have mentioned in all aspects of blame.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two groups: the Dualists (Thunawiyya) and the Mu'tazilites. Maturidi begins by describing an objection: God sets terms and provisions for His servants, yet a servant can kill another before the appointed time, seemingly frustrating God's decree. The objector claims God cannot prevent the servant, implying the servant's power is greater. Maturidi immediately declares God exalted above this. Next, he examines the Dualists, who believe in two eternal principles of Light and Darkness. They claim Light entered Darkness to bring good and repel evil, but ended up trapped. Maturidi shows this entails attributing error, ignorance, and incapacity to the Light (God). Then he addresses the Mu'tazilites, who assert that God only empowers for obedience, gives ownership for gratitude, and creates for submission; otherwise God would be foolish and unjust. They must admit God failed to achieve His intention with His enemies, so He erred. Maturidi concludes their saying makes God resemble the flawed beings mentioned earlier. The entire argument defends God's absolute power and knowledge against any suggestion of deficiency.

Key Terms
معتزلة
قدرة
مشيئة
خطأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi agrees with the Mu'tazilites that God only wills obedience.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this leads to attributing error to God, so he rejects the premise.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say follows from the Mu'tazilite claim that God only empowers for obedience?
A) God's power is limited.
B) God commits error because His enemies do not obey.
C) God is wise.
D) God's will is always fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazilite theology inadvertently makes God appear as a failed agent, undermining His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own life, do you ever find yourself limiting God's will to only what seems 'good' to you, and then feeling disappointed when things don't go as you expect? How does this lesson call you to trust in God's perfect wisdom even when His decree leads to apparent evil? Will you actively surrender your own expectations to His will starting today?
?
Consider the Mu'tazilite impulse to defend God's justice by denying that He could will evil. Have you ever tried to protect God's reputation by explaining away His decree in a way that actually diminishes His sovereignty? Repent of that well-meaning but misguided attempt, and resolve instead to affirm both God's justice and His comprehensive will, even when you cannot reconcile them rationally.

The Fear of Anthropomorphism and Denial of Divine Attributes
Maturidi presents the opponents' argument against essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning of those who deny God's essential attributes and names due to fear of anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Overzealous transcendence leads to denial of God's attributes.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi introduces a controversial stance: some people, out of extreme caution to preserve God's transcendence, end up denying that God has any essential attributes or names. They argue that if God has a name like 'knower', which creatures also have, that implies similarity (tashbih). Understanding their reasoning is key to seeing why Maturidi refutes them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the opponents' main claim and their supporting arguments.
2
Identify the opponents' reasoning
They argue that shared names imply similarity, so they reject essential attributes and names. They also reject calling God 'thing', 'knower', 'powerful'.
3
Reflect on the example of place
They extend their argument to spatial descriptions: if God were in a place, it would lead to likeness and limitation. Consider why they see this as problematic.
The passage ends abruptly; the refutation comes later.
4
Draw the core insight
Understand that the opponents are not denying God but are overly cautious. This sets up Maturidi's response that true tawhid requires affirming attributes without similarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الوصف الله والتسمية لا يوجبان التشابه قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : أنكر قوم أن يكون صفة الله ذاتية يوصف بها أو اسم ذاتي يعرف به وظنوا أن ذلك يوجب التشابه إذ له اسم كما كان لغيره وقالوا وإذ لم يجز أن يكون له موافقة في شيء من جملة الخلق على الإشارة إليه كان أدلة تحقيق الموافقة بالاسم الذي لكل شيء أحرى ولهذا أنكروا القول بالشيء والعالم والقادر وضربوا له المثل بأن القول له وفيه بالمكان إذ يوجب التشبيه والحد فهو بكل مكان كذلك إذ الأمكنة
Question: Description of God and naming do not necessitate similarity. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Some people denied that God has an essential attribute by which He is described, or an essential name by which He is known, and they thought that this necessitates similarity, since He has a name just as other than Him has a name. And they said: Since it is not permissible that He have any agreement in anything from the totality of creation in terms of pointing to Him, then the evidence of the reality of agreement by the name which everything has is more appropriate. And for this reason they denied the statement that He is a thing, a knower, and powerful, and they drew an example for this: that the statement about Him and in Him by place, since it necessitates likeness and limitation, so He is in every place likewise, since places [have limit].
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage introduces a new question (مسألة) on whether describing and naming God implies anthropomorphism (تشابه). Maturidi presents the argument of those who deny God's essential attributes and names. Their reasoning: if God has a name like other beings, that similarity implies anthropomorphism. They argue that since God cannot be pointed to like any creature, affirming shared names actually strengthens the case for similarity. Therefore they reject calling God 'thing', 'knower', 'powerful', and they similarly reject any spatial description of Him, arguing that if one affirms place, it leads to likeness and limitation, and thus God would be in every place equally. The passage cuts off before Maturidi's rebuttal (likely continuing on the next page). This lesson presents the opponents' rationale for denying attributes and names out of excessive caution against tashbih.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that those who deny God's attributes are atheists or reject God entirely.
Correction: Actually, these opponents are motivated by a strong desire to avoid anthropomorphism, not by disbelief.

Checkpoint

A) They think God is beyond all description
B) They think it leads to resemblance (تشابه) with creatures
C) They follow the Mu'tazila
D) They deny God's existence

Key Takeaway
Excessive fear of anthropomorphism can lead to denying essential attributes and names of God, which itself is a deviation from proper tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The opponents in this passage denied God being 'a thing' (شيء) out of extreme caution. Have you ever avoided affirming something about God—even something true—because you worried it might lead to a wrong belief? What is one true thing about God that you need to affirm more boldly, and what will you do to practice that affirmation this week?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

Divine Names Do Not Imply Similarity
Al-Maturidi refutes the objection that divine names imply similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Sunna respond to the objection that divine names imply similarity, and how they use the principle of negation (tanzih) alongside affirmation.

Core Idea
Divine names do not imply similarity; they are used by necessity and accompanied by negation.

This passage from Imam al-Maturidi addresses a key objection raised by those who deny that God can have names or attributes, fearing it would imply similarity to creation. Al-Maturidi explains that while names are drawn from the witnessed world, they are not intended to indicate likeness but are used with the understanding that God is utterly transcendent, as affirmed by the verse 'There is nothing like Him'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Some people deny essential names and attributes because they think it implies similarity. They argue that if God cannot be compared to anything in reference, then sharing a name would prove similarity even more.
They illustrate their point with an analogy: saying God is in a place implies limitation, so similar reasoning applies to names.
2
Al-Maturidi's affirmation
He states that God does have essential names (like 'Allah', 'al-Rahman') and attributes (like knowledge and power). However, our description is limited by our capacity and arises from necessity, drawing from the witnessed world.
This does not mean God is like the witnessed; the names are only approximations.
3
Negation coupled with naming
To avoid implying similarity, the names are accompanied by negations, such as the verse 'There is nothing like Him'. Thus tawhid is an affirmation of essence within a negation, and a negation within an affirmation.
The names are not God's real names but expressions to bring meanings close to understanding.
4
Proof from messengers
The prophets used these names; if they implied similarity, they would contradict tawhid. Their use is permissible because the names are accompanied by the negation of likeness.
The verse 'There is nothing like Him' negates any thingness from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ الوصف الله والتسمية لا يوجبان التشابه ] قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : أنكر قوم أن يكون صفة الله ذاتية يوصف بها، أو اسم ذاتي يعرف به، وظنوا أن ذلك يوجب التشابه؛ إذ له اسم كما كان لغيره. وقالوا: وإذ لم يجز أن يكون له موافقة في شيء من جملة الخلق على الإشارة إليه كان أدلة تحقيق الموافقة بالاسم الذي لكل شيء أحرى. ولهذا أنكروا القول بالشيء والعالم والقادر، وضربوا له المثل بأن القول له وفيه بالمكان إذ يوجب التشبيه والحد فهو بكل مكان كذلك؛ إذ الأمكنة لا نهاية، فالوصف بها وبالواحد منها واحد فمثله الأول. وبالله التوفيق. وأما الأصل عندنا أن الله أسماء ذاتية يُسمّى بها نحو قوله الله الرحمن، وصفات ذاتية بها يوصف نحو العلم بالأشياء والقدرة عليها. لكن الوصف له منا والاسم إنما هو بما يحتمله وسعنا وتبلغه عبارتنا بالضرورة. إذ سبيل ذلك إنما هو عن المعروف في الشاهد، وذلك يوجب التشابه في القول، إذ عن معروف به في الشاهد قُدّر. ولكن الضرورة أطلقت لنا على نفي المفهوم من الشاهد ليُنفى به الشبه. ونسميه بالذي ذكرت ضرورة، ولو احتمل وسعنا التسمية بما لا يسمّى به غير كنا نسميه. لكنه إذ كان الشاهد هو دليله وبه يجب معرفته فمنه قدر اسمه على ما يقرب إلى الفهم بما يريد به، وإن كان الله يتعالى عن أن يكون له مثال أو شبه. ألا ترى أن العبارة التي بها نُسمّيه عالما قادرًا، في الألسن مختلفة، من غير أن كان ثم اختلاف؛ فيدلك أن الأسماء التي نسميه بها عبارات عما يقرب المعاني إلى الأفهام، لا أنها في الحقيقة أسماؤه. ولما تأخذ القلوب منها معان يتعالى عنها قرن بالتسمية حرف نفي، فجعل التوحيد إثبات ذات في ضمن نفي، ونفيًا في ضمن إثبات على ما فُسترت. وبالله التوفيق. ثم الدليل على ما قلنا مجيئ الرسل والكتب السماوية بها، ولو كان في التسمية بما جاءت به الرسل تشبيه لكانوا سبب نقض التوحيد، وهم جميعا دعوا إلى عبادة الواحد وإلى معرفة وحدانية الباري؛ لم يجز أن يكون ذلك مما يحقق العدد ويثبت الموافقة للخلق. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ولكن لما احتملت تلك الأسماء خروج المسمى بها على المعروفين من المسمين بها جاز مجيئهم بها مع قوله : ليس كمثله شيء [الشورى، 11/42]، ليُنفى به شيئية الأشياء من.
Issue: Describing God and naming Him do not necessitate similarity. Abu Mansur said: Some people denied that God has an essential attribute by which He is described, or an essential name by which He is known, and they thought that would necessitate similarity, since He has a name just as others have. They said: Since it is not permissible for Him to have agreement with anything among creation in terms of reference to Him, then the proof of agreement by the name which belongs to everything is even more appropriate. For this reason they denied the saying that He is a thing, the world, and the powerful, and they drew an analogy for Him with the saying that He is in a place, since it necessitates similitude and limitation; so He is in every place likewise, since places have no end, so the description by them and by one of them is one, and His like is the first. And with God is success. As for the principle with us: God has essential names by which He is named, such as His saying 'Allah, al-Rahman', and essential attributes by which He is described, such as knowledge of things and power over them. However, our description of Him and naming is only according to what our capacity can bear and our expressions reach out of necessity. For the way of that is from what is known in the witnessed world, and that necessitates similarity in expression, since it is estimated from what is known in the witnessed world. But necessity has released for us the negation of the concept from the witnessed world in order to negate similarity. And we name Him with what we mentioned out of necessity, and if our capacity could bear naming Him with what none other is named, we would name Him. But since the witnessed world is His evidence and by it He must be known, from it His name is determined in a way that approximates understanding with what is intended, even though God is exalted above having any likeness or similitude. Do you not see that the expressions by which we name Him 'knowing' and 'powerful' differ in languages, without there being any difference? That indicates that the names by which we name Him are expressions that bring meanings close to understandings, not that they are His names in reality. And because the hearts take from them meanings that are exalted above [their ordinary senses], a particle of negation is coupled with the naming, so that tawhid (declaration of God's oneness) is made an affirmation of essence within a negation, and a negation within an affirmation, as has been covered. And with God is success. Then the proof of what we say is the coming of the messengers and the heavenly books with them. And if there were in the naming with which the messengers came a similarity, they would have been the cause of violating tawhid, and they all called to the worship of the One and to knowledge of the oneness of the Creator; it would not be permissible that that should affirm number and establish agreement with creation. And there is no power except with God. But because those names admitted the possibility that the named one transcends the well-known meanings of those who are named by them, it was permissible for them to come with them along with His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' [al-Shura 42:11] to negate by it the thingness of things from.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that giving God names and attributes implies similarity with creatures. Al-Maturidi begins by stating the position of those who deny that God has essential attributes or names, because they think it would necessitate similarity since creatures also have names. They argue that if God cannot be compared to anything in reference, then sharing a name would prove similarity even more. Consequently, they deny calling God a 'thing', 'knower', 'powerful', and compare it to saying God is in a place, which implies limitation. Al-Maturidi then presents the correct position of Ahl al-Sunna: God does have essential names like 'Allah' and 'al-Rahman', and attributes like knowledge and power. However, our descriptions are based on our capacity and necessity, drawing from the witnessed world. This necessity forces us to use terms from the witnessed world, which would imply similarity in wording, but we couple them with negation to negate similarity. He explains that the names are not God's real names but expressions to approximate meanings for understanding, as evidenced by different languages. Tawhid is framed as affirmation with negation. Finally, he provides proof: the messengers brought these names, but if they implied similarity, they would contradict tawhid. The names are permissible because they are accompanied by the verse 'There is nothing like Him', negating any resemblance.

اسم ذاتي
تشابه
الشاهد
الضرورة
نفي
إثبات
توحيد
ليس كمثله شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that if God is called by names also used for creatures, then God must be similar to creatures.
Correction: Al-Maturidi explains that these names are used out of necessity and are accompanied by negations (like 'There is nothing like Him') to remove any implication of similarity.

Checkpoint
What was the reason given by some people for denying that God has essential names and attributes?
A) They thought names are unnecessary
B) They thought it would necessitate similarity between God and creation
C) They had no proof
D) They followed the messengers

Key Takeaway
Divine names are used out of necessity and do not imply similarity; they are affirmed with negation, as demonstrated by the verse 'There is nothing like Him'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call God 'All-Knowing' or 'All-Powerful', do you truly believe that His knowledge and power are nothing like any knowledge or power you have ever witnessed? How does this shape your awe of Him? Commit to pausing before each attribute and mentally adding 'nothing like Him'.
?
This lesson teaches that we use human language out of necessity, not because it captures God's reality. In your prayer or worship, are you sometimes guilty of imagining God in human terms? What specific step will you take today to purify your mental image of Him?
?
Al-Maturidi says the names come with negation to protect tawhid. Where in your life do you need to apply the same principle — affirming something about God while immediately negating any creaturely comparison? Write down one false imagination you will actively reject today.

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
The Nature of Divine Names and Attributes
Maturidi explains divine names analogically from the witnessed world with negation of likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that divine names and attributes are understood analogically from the witnessed world while negating any similarity.

Core Idea
Divine names are human approximations, not God's actual names.

Maturidi begins by stating the foundational principle for understanding divine names and attributes. He explains that we can only describe Allah using concepts derived from our world, but we must always negate any notion of similarity. This passage lays out how tawhid balances affirmation and negation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of Maturidi's argument.
The passage is dense and compact.
2
Identify essential names and attributes
Notice that Maturidi distinguishes between essential names (like Allah, al-Rahman) and essential attributes (like knowledge and power).
This is a key distinction in kalam.
3
Understand the role of the witnessed world (al-shāhid)
Observe that Maturidi says we derive names from the shāhid by necessity, but we negate the concept to avoid tashbīh.
This move prevents anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the linguistic example
Maturidi gives the example of different languages using different words for the same meaning, showing that names are expressions, not God's actual names.
This supports the idea that names are for human understanding.
5
Grasp the conclusion about tawhid
The passage ends by stating that tawhid is an affirmation within negation and vice versa.
This summarizes the method of balancing affirmation and negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأصل عندنا أن الله أسماء ذاتية يُسمّى بها نحو قوله الله الرحمن ، وصفات ذاتية بها يوصف نحو العلم بالأشياء والقدرة عليها . لكن الوصف له منا والاسم إنما هو بما يحتمله وسعنا وتبلغه عبارتنا بالضرورة. إذ سبيل ذلك إنما هو عن المعروف في الشاهد، وذلك يو. التشابه في القول، إذ عن معروف به في الشاهد قُدّر. ولكن الضرورة أطلقت لنا على نفي المفهوم من الشاهد ليُنفى به الشبه. ونسميه بالذي ذكرت ضرورة ، ولو احتمل وسعنا التسمية بما لا يسمّى به غير كنا نسميه . لكنه إذ كان الشاهد هو دليله وبه يجب معرفته فمنه قدر اسمه على ما يقرب إلى الفهم بما يريد به، وإن كان الله يتعالى عن أن يكون له مثال أو شبه. ألا ترى أن العبارة التي بها نُسمّيه عالما قادرًا، في الألسن مختلفة، من غير أن كان ثمة اختلاف؛ فيدلك أن الأسماء التي نسميه بها عبارات عما يقرب [المعاني] إلى الأفهام، لا أنها في الحقيقة أسماؤه. ولِما تأخذ القلوب منها معان يتعالى عنها قرن بالتسمية حرف نفي، فجعل التوحيد إثبات ذات في ضمن نفي، ونفيًا في ضمن إثبات على ما فُسترت . وبالله التوفيق.
And as for the principle with us: that Allah has essential names (asmā' dhātiyya) by which He is named, such as His saying 'Allah', 'al-Rahman', and essential attributes (ṣifāt dhātiyya) by which He is described, such as knowledge of things and power over them. But description of Him from us and the name is only according to what our capacity can bear and what our expressions reach by necessity. For the way of that is only from what is known in the witnessed world (al-shāhid), and that is [based on] similarity in speech, since it is estimated from what is known in the witnessed world. However, necessity has given us license to negate the concept from the witnessed world in order to negate likeness (al-shabah). And we name Him by what I mentioned out of necessity, and if it were possible we would name Him with what none other is named, but we do not name Him [with that]. But since the witnessed world is His evidence and by it He must be known, from it His name is estimated in a way that brings [meaning] close to understanding by what He intends, even though Allah is exalted above having any likeness or similitude. Do you not see that the expression by which we name Him 'Knowing', 'Powerful' differs in tongues without there being any difference [in meaning]? That indicates that the names by which we name Him are expressions that bring meanings close to understandings, not that they are in reality His names. And because hearts take from them meanings that He is exalted above, a letter of negation is attached to the naming, so tawhid is made an affirmation of essence within negation, and a negation within affirmation, as has been stated. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the nature of divine names and attributes according to Maturidi. He begins by stating the principle: Allah has essential names (like 'Allah' and 'al-Rahman') and essential attributes (like knowledge and power). However, the way we describe Him is limited by our capacity and necessity. The basis for understanding these names is the witnessed world (al-shāhid), but we must negate any concept of similarity (tashbīh) from that understanding. Maturidi explains that we name Allah out of necessity, drawing from the shāhid, but we know that His reality transcends any example or likeness. He gives an example: the expressions for 'Knowing' and 'Powerful' vary across languages, yet the meanings are the same for us, showing that these names are approximations for human understanding, not His actual names. Because the meanings we conceive are below His reality, a negation ('nothing like Him') is attached to make tawhid an affirmation within negation.

الشاهد
التشبيه
التوحيد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine names are exactly the same as human names, meaning God is similar to creation.
Correction: Maturidi explains that names are expressions from the witnessed world with negation of likeness, so they are not identical in reality.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why must we negate the concept from the witnessed world when describing Allah?
A) Because the witnessed world is not reliable.
B) To negate any likeness (tashbīh) between Allah and creation.
C) Because we cannot know anything from the witnessed world.
D) To affirm that Allah has no attributes at all.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that divine names are human expressions derived from the created world but purified by negation of any similarity to God.

?
If every name we use for Allah is only an approximation, how does that affect the way you approach His names in supplication? Are you content with this limitation, or does it create a longing in you to know Him as He truly is?
?
Maturidi says we name Allah out of necessity—what does that necessity reveal about your own relationship with God? Do you rely on these names as a genuine bridge to connect with Him, or have they become empty words you repeat without awareness of their inadequacy?

The Impossibility of a Derived Divine Name
Maturidi refutes the Batiniyya view of a derived divine name via infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if God's name is derived from another (e.g., His action), it leads to infinite regress, thus proving that His name must be essential and self-subsistent.

Core Idea
God’s name cannot come from another; otherwise, an infinite chain of origination would be required.

Maturidi here refutes the Batiniyya claim that God has no essential name but only a name derived from His creation or attribute. He shows that such a view leads to a logical contradiction and an infinite regress of causes. The only consistent position is that God has an eternal, essential name.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Batiniyya claim
Read the passage and locate where Maturidi presents the Batiniyya view that God has no essential name and that His name is derived from another (e.g., the Intellect or Soul).
2
Follow the logical challenge
Examine Maturidi's questioning: if the name is derived from another, what is the source? Is it from God Himself or from something else?
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
Trace the argument that if the name comes from an origination, that origination itself would require another origination, leading to an infinite chain. The only way to avoid this is that the origination (and thus the name) is by God's essence.
This shows that God must have an essential, not derived, name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع على ما يقال: «الله» اسمه أو هو اسم غيره؟ فيرجع في الحقيقة إلى أنه اسم العقل، و«الرحمن» اسم النفس ; وعلى هذا مذهبهم. وأبوا الاسم كراهة التشبيه، ثم جعلوا المعبود باسم الإله والرحمن والرحيم أغيارًا لا يُحصى عددهم، وأجزاء يصعب إحصاؤهم. فكأن الرسل جاءوا عندهم بعبادة العدد لا بالتوحيد والله المستعان . ثم يقال لهم عند قولهم ليس له اسم ما تعنون بقولكم : ليس له اسم ذاتي ولا صفة ذاتية ؟ فلا يجدون السبيل إلى أن يعبّروا عن أنفسهم بما قالوا: «ليس له اسم»; و يُبطل جملتهم الذي قالوا: «الله الذي ليس له اسم ذاتي ثم زعموا أن له اسما من غيره نحو المبدع بإبداع هو علّة لمبدع هو العال لا المعلول ولا العلة ; لأن كل معلول يجوز أن يصير علة بحال . [٤٦ و] فيقال / له إذ جعل اسمه عن غيره : أكان ما حقق له [ من] غيره ذلك الاسم أو سَمّى [ هو ] ؟ فإن قال : لا ، له أن يسمّيه ما شاء من الأغيار والعلة والمعلول; إذ بغيره استحق، لأنه كذلك يقول : كان ولا علة ولا معلول; فإذا هو قول كان بحق المجاز لا بالحقيقة بالضرورة، فأوجب هذا الاسم له غيره من غير أن كان منه ما استوجب. فإن قال : كان منه الإبداع ، قيل : كان منه الإبداع بعد أن لم يكن، حتى حقق له الاسم بأن كان به من أي وجه، حتى أوجب له الاسم، فيلزم جعله بإبداع [آخر ] إلى ما لا نهاية له، وذلك محال، ولا يقول به. فيجب أن يكون الإبداع بذاته، فيكون لم يزل مبدعا، وفي ذلك كله وجوب الاسم الذاتي له بالضرورة. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
With on what is said: Is 'Allah' His name or is it a name of another? So it returns in reality to it being the name of the Intellect, and 'ar-Rahman' is the name of the Soul; and this is their doctrine. And they refused the name due to dislike of anthropomorphism, then they made the worshipped being under the names 'al-Ilah', 'ar-Rahman', and 'ar-Rahim' as others whose number cannot be counted, and parts whose enumeration is difficult. As if the messengers came to them for the worship of number, not for monotheism; and God is the aid. Then it is said to them when they say 'He has no name': what do you mean by your saying 'He has no essential name and no essential attribute'? So they cannot find a way to express themselves by what they said: 'He has no name'; and their statement is invalidated by what they said: 'Allah who has no essential name', then they claimed that He has a name from another, such as the originate by origination that is a cause for an originate that is the Intellect, not the effect nor the cause; because every effect may become a cause in some state. So it is said to him when he makes His name from another: Was it that what was realized for Him from another is that name, or did He name it? If he says: No, He can name it whatever He wills of the others, the cause and the effect; since by another He deserved it, because he says: He was with no cause and no effect; then it is a statement that was by metaphor necessarily, not by reality. So this name was obligated for Him by another without there being from Him what made it deserved. If he says: From Him was origination, it is said: Origination was from Him after it was not, until the name was realized for Him by being with it in some way, until the name was obligated for Him. Then it necessitates making it by another origination ad infinitum, and that is impossible, and he does not say it. So origination must be by His essence, so He has never ceased to be an originator, and in all that is the necessity of the essential name for Him necessarily. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the Batiniyya's position on divine names. The argument addresses the question: Is 'Allah' God's name or the name of something else? The Batiniyya reduce it to the Intellect and the Soul. They rejected names to avoid anthropomorphism, but then ended up with multiple objects of worship. The argument then reveals a contradiction: they say 'He has no essential name' yet they speak of 'Allah' and deny essential names. If they claim His name comes from another (e.g., the first originate from the Intellect), the question is: was that name something God achieved from another, or did He Himself name it? If He did not, then the name is arbitrary. If they respond that origination came from Him, then that origination itself must have been caused by another origination, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, the only consistent conclusion is that the origination (and thus the name) is by His essence, meaning He eternally originates, and He has an essential name necessarily.

الإبداع

Misconception: The argument is about the linguistic derivation of the name 'Allah' from a root.
Correction: The argument is ontological: whether God's name depends on something else for its existence, not how it is linguistically formed.

B) That the concept of an essential name is impossible
C) That the name must be self-subsistent
D) That names are meaningless

Key Takeaway
God's name must be essential and self-subsistent; any attempt to derive it from another leads to an impossible infinite chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider the Batiniyya's motivation: they rejected divine names to protect God from anthropomorphism. In what area of your faith are you tempted to 'protect' God by denying or reinterpreting what He has revealed about Himself? What would it cost you to accept the essential reality of His names?

The Contradiction of Denying an Essential Name
Maturidi identifies the inconsistency in the opponents' claim and poses the initial question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God has no essential name leads to logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Denying an essential name while using names is self-defeating.

Imam Maturidi begins by exposing the self-contradiction at the heart of those who say God has no essential name. They claim He has no name, yet they call Him 'Allah' and even say He has a name derived from His actions. The teacher then asks a simple but powerful question: if the name comes from something else, did that something else reveal the name or merely invent it?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense, logical style of Maturidi.
2
Identify the contradiction
The opponents say 'He has no name' yet they call Him 'Allah' and claim He has a name from something else. That is the first inconsistency.
3
Examine the question about the origin of the name
Maturidi asks: does the something else actually reveal the name, or does God Himself name? If the latter, it is metaphorical, not literal.
4
Understand the conclusion of the first part
The name is said to come from other than God, and God did nothing to deserve it, so it is a metaphorical attribution.
This sets up the need for a deeper argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذاتية ؟ فلا يجدون السبيل إلى أن يعبّروا عن أنفسهم بما قالوا: «ليس له اسم»؛ و يُبطل جملتهم الذي قالوا: «الله الذي ليس له اسم ذاتي ثم زعموا أن له اسما من غيره " نحو المبدع بإبداع هو علّة لمبدع هو العال لا المعلول ولا العلة ؛ لأن كل معلول يجوز أن يصير علة بحال . فيقال له إذ جعل اسمه عن غيره : أكان ما حقق له من غيره ذلك الاسم أو سَمّى هو ؟ فإن قال : لا ، له أن يسمّيه ما شاء من الأغيار والعلة والمعلول؛ إذ بغيره استحق، لأنه كذلك يقول : كان ولا علة ولا معلول؛ فإذا هو قول كان بحق المجاز لا بالحقيقة بالضرورة، فأوجب هذا الاسم له غيره من غير أن كان منه ما استوجب. فإن قال : كان منه الإبداع ، قيل : كان منه الإبداع بعد أن لم يكن، حتى حقق له الاسم بأن كان به من أي وجه، حتى أوجب له الاسم، فيلزم جعله بإبداع آخر إلى ما لا نهاية له، وذلك محال، ولا يقول به. فيجب أن يكون الإبداع بذاته، فيكون لم يزل مبدعا، وفي ذلك كله وجوب الاسم الذاتي له بالضرورة. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Essential? So they find no way to express themselves by what they said: 'He has no name'; and their statement is invalidated: they said: 'God who has no essential name' and then they claimed that He has a name from something else, such as al-Mubdi (the Originator) by an origination (ibda) which is a cause for an originator (mubdi) which is the cause-effect? not the effect and not the cause; because every effect may become a cause in some state. So it is said to him: when you make His name from something else: was what actualized for Him from that something the name, or did He name? If he says: 'No', then He may name Him whatever He wills from other things and the cause and the effect; because by other than Him He deserves it, because thus he says: He was and there was no cause and effect; so it is a statement that is metaphorical, not literal, necessarily. So this name for Him was caused by other than Him without Him having done anything that deserved it. If he says: 'The origination was from Him', it is said: The origination was from Him after it was not, until the name was actualized for Him by being through it in some way, until it necessitated for Him the name. Then it would necessitate making it by another origination ad infinitum, and that is impossible, and he does not say it. So origination must be by His essence, thus He is eternally originating, and in all that is the necessity of the essential name for Him necessarily. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim that God has no essential name (ism dhati) or that His name is derived from something other than His essence. The author first points out the contradiction: these people say 'He has no name' yet they call Him 'Allah' and then claim He has a name from something else, like 'al-Mubdi' from His act of origination. The argument proceeds by examining the logical consequences: if the name comes from something else, one must ask whether that something else actually reveals the name or merely coins it. If they answer that God did not have that name originally (i.e., it is merely attributed), then it is a metaphorical name, not real. If they say the act of origination (ibda) is the source, then that act itself would have to be justified by another origination, leading to an infinite chain. Therefore, origination must be by God's essence, meaning He is eternally Mubdi, and so He necessarily has an essential name. The conclusion is that the essential name is necessary.

Correction: Maturidi shows that this denial leads to self-contradiction and logical impossibility because it still uses names derived from something else, which cannot be literal attributions.

Key Takeaway
Denying an essential name for God while still naming Him leads to logical contradiction and forces the admission that any name derived from something else is metaphorical, not real.

?
This argument reveals a logical contradiction in denying essential names. But more personally, have you ever been content with a superficial understanding of God's names, not allowing them to transform your character? What would it mean for you to truly believe that His names are real and essential?

The Infinite Regress Argument for an Essential Name
Maturidi shows that deriving God's name from His act leads to infinite regress, so the act must be by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the argument of infinite regress to prove that God must have an essential name.

Core Idea
If God's name comes from His act, that act itself would require another act, leading to infinite regress.

Having exposed the initial contradiction, Maturidi now takes the opponents' position to its logical conclusion. If they argue that God's name comes from His act of origination (ibda), then that act itself must be explained. The teacher shows this leads to an infinite chain of origination, which is impossible. Therefore, origination must be by God's essence, establishing that He has an essential name.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the argument starting from 'If he says: The origination was from Him'.
Notice the logical flow: supposition → objection → contradiction → conclusion.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
If the name depends on a temporal act of origination, then to justify that act, another origination would be needed, and so on ad infinitum. That is impossible.
Infinite regress is a classical argument against derived names.
3
See why origination by essence is necessary
To avoid regress, the origination must be by God's essence, meaning He is eternally originating (lam yazal mubdi'an).
This makes the name essential, not derived.
4
Conclude the necessity of an essential name
The passage ends with the affirmation that the essential name is necessarily required.
Note the phrase 'bi-d-darura' (necessarily).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

إبداع (origination)
اسم ذاتي (essential name)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason infinite regress is impossible?
A) God cannot create infinite things.
B) It would require an endless chain of causes, which is logically impossible.
C) The Qur'an forbids it.
D) The opponents themselves do not accept it.

Key Takeaway
If God's name were derived from a temporal act, it would lead to an infinite regress; hence the act must be eternal by His essence, necessitating an essential name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself thinking of God in terms of what He does for you rather than who He is? How does the logical necessity of essential names challenge you to seek knowledge of His eternal attributes beyond your personal experiences? Take a moment to repent if you have reduced your understanding of God to momentary favors.
?
The argument shows that denying essential names leads to an absurd infinite regress. But have you ever, in your heart, subconsciously thought that God's attributes could change based on His actions? How does this truth that His names are essential and unchangeable give you security in your worship? Commit to affirming one essential name of God daily this week.
?
If God's names were derived from actions, then His identity would be dependent on creation. How does that thought make you feel? Do you truly trust a God whose essence is independent and perfect in His names? Examine your heart: Have you ever been more comforted by a blessing from God than by the name of the Giver Himself? Resolve to learn one essential name thoroughly until it transforms your devotion.

The Invalidity of the Question: Why Did God Create?
Maturidi presents the first group's argument that the question 'why' is invalid based on God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a group of scholars regarded the question 'Why did God create creation?' as invalid, based on God's attributes of wisdom, knowledge, and self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
God's actions are inherently wise; the 'why' question is invalid for the Creator.

Imam Maturidi begins by presenting a group who argued that the question 'Why did God create creation?' is itself invalid. Their reasoning draws on God's eternal attributes: He is ever-wise, all-knowing, and utterly self-sufficient. Because these attributes are essential to Him, His actions cannot deviate from wisdom. This lesson explores that argument and the Quranic support for it.

Guided Path
1
Read the first group's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation for the first answer. Note how Maturidi introduces the group and their core claim that the question is invalid.
Pay attention to the attributes used: hakim, 'alim, ghani.
2
Analyze the reasoning
Identify the logical steps: God's wisdom prevents deviation; deviation arises only from ignorance or need; God is neither ignorant nor needy; therefore His action cannot lack wisdom.
The argument is a syllogism based on God's attributes.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Look at the verse cited: 'We did not create the heaven and the earth as players... He is not questioned about what He does.' Understand how this verse supports the claim.
The verse negates play (lahw) and establishes God's unquestionable authority.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why the question 'why' is problematic: it implies a purpose external to God, which contradicts His self-sufficiency and eternal wisdom.
This connects to the larger theme of God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم . [ لِمَ خلق الله الخلق ؟ ]

الحمد لله الذي لا غاية لما يستحق من الشكر والمحامد على ما لدينا من جزيل المنن وعظيم العوائد، وإياه نسأل التوفيق لأهْدَى سُبل المراشد.

قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } اختلف الناس في جواب سؤال السائل: «لم خلق الله الخلق»؟

أـ قال قوم: السؤال فاسد لا يُسأل عن ذلك؛ إذ الله سبحانه حكيم لم يزل، عليم غنى؛ ففعله لا يحتمل الخروج عن الحكمة، إذ يخرج الفعل عنها لجهل بها، أو لما يُخاف فوت نفع لو حفظ طريق الحكمة. فإذا كان الله سبحانه عليمًا لا يجهل، غنيّاً لا يمسته حاجة ينتفع بدفعها بطل أن يخرج فعله عن جهة الحكمة وسؤال «لِمَ» ليست فيه الحكمة؛ ولذلك نفي وجل توهم اللعب عن فعله فقال : وما خلقنا السماء والأرض وما بينهما لاعبين إلى قوله : ولا يسئل عما يفعل وهم يسئلون ، وألحق الويل بمن يظن به الحاجة أو في فعله السفة. ولا قوة إلا بالله .

بــ وقال قوم من المعتزلة : رأى الأصلح كذلك ففعل، ولا يُسأل عن فعله الأصلح. {قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } وهذا كلام لا يخلو من أن يراد بالأصلح الحكمة، فهو الأول.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. [Why did God create creation?]

Praise be to Allah, whose deserving of thanks and praise has no limit, for the abundant favors and great benefits we have. And from Him we seek success to the most guided paths of right guidance.

Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: People differed regarding the answer to the questioner's question: 'Why did God create creation?'

a) A group said: The question is invalid; it should not be asked about that. For Allah, exalted, is ever-wise, knowing, self-sufficient. So His action does not admit deviation from wisdom, for an action deviates from it due to ignorance of it, or out of fear of losing a benefit if the path of wisdom were preserved. Since Allah is knowing, not ignorant, self-sufficient, not touched by need from which He would benefit by repelling it, it is impossible that His action depart from the way of wisdom. And the question 'Why' does not contain wisdom. Therefore He negated the suspicion of play from His action, saying: 'And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them as players' up to His saying: 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned.' And He attached woe to those who think of need in Him or foolishness in His action. And there is no power except with Allah.

b) And a group from the Mu'tazila said: He saw the most beneficial (al-aslah) as such, and so He did, and He is not asked about what is most beneficial. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This statement is not free from [the possibility that] by 'most beneficial' wisdom is intended, so it is the first [i.e., the same as the first group's view].
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents two views on the question of why God created creation. The first view, attributed to a group (likely the Ahl al-Sunnah), declares the question itself invalid. Since God is eternally wise, knowing, and self-sufficient, His actions cannot deviate from wisdom. The question 'why' would imply a purpose external to God, which is not applicable because God is not ignorant or needy. The author quotes Quranic verses that negate play in creation and assert that God is not questioned. The second view is from the Mu'tazila, who say that God acted according to what is most beneficial (al-aslah) and therefore is not asked about that. Imam Maturidi comments that if by 'most beneficial' they mean wisdom, then it is the same as the first view. Thus, the correct stance is that God's actions are inherently wise and not subject to human interrogation.

Key Terms
الحكمة (wisdom)
اللعب (play)
السؤال (question)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that asking 'Why did God create?' is a valid theological question that expects a purpose-like answer.
Correction: The passage argues that the question is invalid because God's attributes (wisdom, knowledge, self-sufficiency) make His actions inherently wise; questioning them implies deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the first group, why is the question 'Why did God create creation?' invalid?
A) Because creation is an illusion.
B) Because God is wise, knowing, and self-sufficient, so His actions cannot lack wisdom.
C) Because the question was never asked in the Quran.
D) Because humans are not allowed to ask questions.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal wisdom and self-sufficiency make the question 'Why did He create?' invalid, as His actions are inherently wise and not subject to human interrogation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt the urge to question God's wisdom in your life—perhaps in a trial, loss, or unanswered prayer? How does this lesson's assertion that His actions cannot deviate from wisdom challenge that impulse? What would it cost you to stop asking 'why' and start trusting?
?
The verse says, 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned.' When you find yourself mentally interrogating God's decree, are you placing yourself in the position of the one who is questioned—rather than the one who submits? Write down one specific situation where you need to repent of this attitude and embrace silence before His wisdom.
?
If God's self-sufficiency means He created you not because He needed you but out of pure wisdom and grace, how should that reshape your sense of purpose? Do you live as if you owe Him everything, or as if He owes you an explanation? Commit to one change today that reflects the reality that He owes you nothing yet gives you everything.

The Mu'tazilite View on Al-Aslah and Maturidi's Critique
Maturidi presents the Mu'tazilite answer based on al-aslah and comments that it may be equivalent to the first group's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite answer that God created for the most beneficial (al-aslah) and Imam Maturidi's critique that it may reduce to the first group's view.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite 'most beneficial' parallels wisdom; Maturidi questions its distinctness.

After presenting the first group's rejection of the question, Imam Maturidi turns to the Mu'tazilite response. They held that God acts according to what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for creation, and therefore cannot be questioned about that. Maturidi offers a brief but incisive comment: if by 'most beneficial' they mean wisdom, then their view is essentially the same as the first group's. This lesson examines that response and Maturidi's assimilation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite position
Read the Arabic and translation for the second answer. Note how Maturidi presents it as a group from the Mu'tazila.
The term al-aslah is key.
2
Understand Maturidi's critique
Read Maturidi's comment: 'This statement is not free from [the possibility] that by 'most beneficial' wisdom is intended, so it is the first.' Reflect on what he means.
Maturidi does not outright reject the Mu'tazilite view but suggests it is not fundamentally new.
3
Compare the two views
Consider the overlap: both views ultimately appeal to wisdom. The first group explicitly says the question is invalid; the Mu'tazila say God is not asked about al-aslah. If al-aslah equals wisdom, both say God's actions are wise and unquestionable.
This shows Maturidi's method of reconciling views.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Understand why this assimilation matters: it prevents a false dichotomy and maintains that God's actions are inherently good and wise, not subject to human evaluation.

Source Text

Negation of God's Name and the Avoidance of Anthropomorphism
Imam Maturidi argues that negating God's name avoids tashbih, supported by ibdaʿ and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the negation of God's name from all created things serves to avoid anthropomorphism (tashbih), and why the nature of God's creative act (ibdaʿ) and accidents supports this theological safeguard.

Core Idea
Denying God a name is not a denial of attributes but a shield against anthropomorphism.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi continues his discussion on God's name and the danger of tashbih. He shows that even those who affirm a name for God agree with those who deny it on one crucial point: the name is negated from all created things. He then deepens the argument by examining God's act of creation (ibdaʿ) and the limitations of accidents, reinforcing that attributes like knowledge and power cannot be ascribed to creation in the same way they apply to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow and key terms such as 'ibdaʿ' and 'aʿrāḍ'.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two interconnected arguments: first, the common ground between affirmers and deniers of God's name in negating the name from all things; second, the evidence from ibdaʿ and accidents that God is not described by creation.
The first argument establishes agreement; the second provides deeper proof.
3
Trace the conclusion
The final sentence ties both arguments: if affirming a name led to tashbih, then negating it also prevents tashbih. Reflect on why this conclusion is important for Islamic theology.
The argument shows that negation is not a denial of attributes but a safeguard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول والثاني، ولكان بين من زعموا أن له اسما وبين غيره موافقة في نفي الاسم من جميع الأشياء. على أنه يجد في القول بواحد من الخلق نفي التشبيه، وإن كان من حيث اسم الآحاد اجتماع. وبعد، فإن الإبداع عنده علة، ولا يوصف بالشيء، لما كان به الأشياء. والأعراض كلها لا توصف بعالم ولا قادر ولا نحو ذلك . فلو كان في إثبات الاسم تشابه لكان في نفي ذلك كذلك من الوجه الذي ذكرت ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The first and the second, and there would be between those who claim that He has a name and others agreement in negating the name from all things. That is, one finds in the statement that [He is] one of creation negation of tashbih, even if from the aspect of the name of individuals there is aggregation. Afterwards, innovation (ibdaʿ) is a cause with Him, and He is not described by the thing, because by it things exist. All accidents are not described as knowing, powerful, or the like. So if in affirming the name there were tashbih, then in negating it there would be likewise from the aspect I mentioned. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a proof concerning the negation of God's name from all created things to avoid anthropomorphism (tashbih). It begins by stating that both those who claim God has a name and those who deny it agree that the name is negated from all things. This leads to the point that considering God as 'one of creation' negates tashbih, even though names among individuals may seem to indicate similarity. The author then explains that God's act of creation (ibdaʿ) is a cause in His knowledge, but He is not described by the created thing. Accidents (aʿrāḍ) are not described with attributes like knowledge or power. The conclusion is that if affirming the name led to tashbih, then negating it would also prevent tashbih in the same way.

Key Terms
اسم (name)
تشبيه (anthropomorphism)
إبداع (innovation/creation)
أعراض (accidents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that God has a name implies that God has no attributes.
Correction: This passage shows that negating the name is a safeguard against anthropomorphism, not a denial of attributes. God's essence remains distinct and attributes are affirmed without similarity.

Checkpoint
