A) The existence of God
B) Avoiding tashbih through negation of name
C) The nature of accidents
D) The meaning of ibdaʿ

Key Takeaway
Negating God's name from creation is a fundamental safeguard against tashbih, upheld by both affirmers and deniers of God's name, and supported by the nature of God's creative act and the limitations of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage argues that if affirming a name could lead to tashbih, then negating it also prevents it. Where in your own worship do you need to be more deliberate about guarding against anthropomorphism, even in your most intimate supplications?

Creation as Necessitated by Generosity and Power
A group argues that creation is necessitated by God's generosity and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's attributes of generosity and power necessitate creation, and why this does not imply compulsion.

Core Idea
God's generosity and power naturally entail creation without compulsion.

Imam al-Maturidi records an argument from a group of theologians who claim that God's attributes of generosity (jūd) and power (qudra) logically require creation. This passage presents two interconnected reasons: generosity must have a recipient, and unused power is futile. As you read, consider how these reasons aim to show that creation is a necessary outflow of God's perfect attributes, not an arbitrary act.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that the group gives two reasons: one from generosity, one from power.
The phrase 'wa huwa qādir' begins the second reason.
2
Analyze the first reason: generosity
Identify why generosity necessitates creation: a generous bestower must have someone to receive the generosity.
The term 'ifāḍat al-jūd' means pouring forth generosity.
3
Analyze the second reason: power
Examine the argument that an unused capability is wasted (ḍā'i'a). Why does this imply creation?
This reason assumes that God's power must be actualized to be meaningful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جـــ وقال قوم: إذ هو جواد كريم قادر لزم الوصف بإفاضة الجود، فلا بد من خلق يكون بخلقه واهبا مفيضا جوده عليه؛ وهو قادر، وقدرة لا تحقق الفعل ألبتة ضائعة؛ فلذلك خلق. وبالله التوفيق.
And a group said: Since He is Generous, Benevolent, and Capable, the description of pouring forth generosity is necessary, so there must be a creation by whose creation He is a bestower, pouring His generosity upon it; and He is Capable, and a capability that never actualizes an act is wasted; therefore He created. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from a group (likely Mu'tazilites or a theological school) that God's attributes of generosity, benevolence, and capability necessitate creation. They argue that if God is described as generously giving (ifāḍat al-jūd), then there must be a recipient of that generosity, i.e., creation. Moreover, if God is capable (qādir) and this capability never results in an act, it would be wasted (ḍā'i'a). Hence, creation is a necessary consequence of God's attributes. The author records this view and ends with 'With Allah is success', indicating a neutral or approving tone.

Key Terms
جواد
كريم
قادر
إفاضة الجود
قدرة ضائعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this argument implies God is compelled to create by an external force.
Correction: The argument presents God's own attributes as the internal reason for creation, not external compulsion. God's nature necessitates creation, but that necessity is from His own essence.

Checkpoint
According to the group's argument, why did God create?
A) Because He was bored.
B) Because He is generous and capable, and not creating would waste His ability.
C) Because He needed creation to be known.
D) Because creation is an accident of His essence.

Key Takeaway
Creation is a necessary consequence of God's attributes of generosity and power, not an arbitrary act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's generosity necessarily entails your existence, you are not a random accident but an object of His essential love. Do you live as if you are a necessary recipient of His goodness, or do you take His gifts for granted? This week, identify one blessing you usually overlook and thank God for it as a necessary expression of His generosity.
?
God's power cannot be wasted—your very being is a demonstration of His capability. Are you using your own capacity to act in ways that fulfill His purpose, or are you wasting your potential in disobedience? Commit to one concrete action today that aligns with His will as a response to His power.
?
The author ends with 'And with Allah is success'—a reminder of humility. What is one theological belief you hold so strongly that you might be closing yourself to deeper truth? Spend time in du'a asking Allah to keep your heart open to correction while maintaining conviction.

No Alternatives Implies Injustice and Lack of Choice
The jurist lists consequences of denying alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the jurist argues that if God had no alternative in His actions, then His actions would not be preferable, He would possess the attribute of injustice, and He would lack true choice.

Core Idea
Denying alternatives leads to injustice and negates divine choice.

This lesson introduces the jurist's critique of a deterministic view of God's actions. He begins by pointing out that if God could not have done otherwise, then nothing He does can be considered preferable. Moreover, He would be stuck with the attribute of injustice. The passage also argues that God would not be truly choosing His actions, as any alternative would be corrupting. This sets the stage for a deeper critique of determinism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation aloud to get a sense of the argument.
2
Identify the two consequences
Notice that the jurist says two things: (1) nothing of His action is preferable, and (2) He possesses the attribute of injustice. Also, He is not choosing.
3
Understand the logic of choice
The jurist argues that if God had no alternative, then anything else would be corrupting, implying He is incapable of reform. That is blameworthy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله: وجملة هذا الفصل أنه على قولهم إذ لم يكن له غير الذي فعل لم يكن شيء من فعله مفضلا؛ إذ هو أبقى بكل فعله صفة الجور؛ ولا كان لما يفعله مختارا له، إذ لو كان منه غير ذلك كان مفسدا، وكان عن جعل الإصلاح في غيره عاجزا. وذلك هو النهاية صفة الذم. والله الموفق. ولو كان لا يجوز له غير الذي فعل لكان بفعله منتفعا، ويصير هو إليه محتاجا ليحمد به ويثني عليه؛ إذ من لا يستحق حمدا ولا مدحا إلا بغيره فهو إليه محتاج في أن يحق له الثناء، وبه منتفع. إذ من قولهم: إن فعله غيره، ولم يكن له تركه ولا غير الذي فعله؛ إذ غيره يحط رتبته ويسفهه. فثبت بما فعل النفع، وهو غيره عندهم. وهذه صفة الحاجة في عرف العقول. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The jurist, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The summary of this chapter is that according to their statement, since there is for Him no alternative to what He did, nothing of His action is preferable; for He would remain with every action of His possessing the attribute of injustice; nor would He be choosing what He does, for if something else came from Him, it would be corrupting, and He would be incapable of placing reform in something else. And that is the ultimate attribute of blame. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And if it were not permissible for Him to do other than what He did, then He would be benefiting by His action, and He would become in need of it to be praised and thanked; for whoever does not deserve praise and commendation except by something else, he is in need of it for praise to be due to him, and benefited by it. For it is part of their statement that His action is other than Him, and He had no abandonment of it nor an alternative to what He did; for something else would lower His rank and deem Him foolish. So it is established that He benefited by what He did, and it is other than Him according to them. And this is the attribute of need in the custom of intellects. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by a jurist (al-Faqih) summarizing and critiquing a previous view. The view under critique is that God had no alternative in His actions—i.e., He necessarily did what He did. The jurist argues that if God had no alternative, then nothing He did could be considered preferable (afdal), since He would be stuck with the same action; thus He would have the attribute of injustice (jawr). Moreover, He would not be choosing His actions, because if He did something else, it would be corrupt (mufsid), implying He is incapable of reform. This is blameworthy. Additionally, if God could not have done otherwise, then He would be benefiting from His own action and would need it to receive praise and thanks—a sign of need (haja). The jurist points out that according to the proponents, God's action is other than Him, and He had no ability to abandon it or do otherwise, because any alternative would lower His rank and deem Him foolish. Thus, they imply that God benefits from His action, which is other than Him, indicating need. The passage concludes that this is the attribute of need in the understanding of rational minds, and ends with 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms
مفضل (preferable)
جور (injustice)
مختار (choosing)
مفسد (corrupting)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying God alternatives demonstrates His absolute control.
Correction: It actually leads to attributing injustice and negating His free choice.

Checkpoint
According to the jurist, if God had no alternative to what He did, what would be the case?
A) He would be praised.
B) He would be unjust and not truly choosing.
C) He would be all-powerful.
D) He would be independent.

Key Takeaway
Denying God alternatives in action leads to attributing injustice to Him and denying His free choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused your own wrongdoing by thinking 'I had no choice'? How does this lesson show that such thinking actually implies injustice on your part, and what will you do the next time that excuse comes to mind?
?
If God's freedom to choose among alternatives is the foundation of His justice, then your belief that God is forced by circumstances (like your prayers or your sins) is a subtle form of attributing injustice to Him. Do you hold any hidden assumptions that God must act in a certain way? How can you replace them with trust in His free choice?
?
The jurist says that without alternatives, God would not be choosing. When you reflect on your own life, do you live as if God is stuck with your situation? Or do you truly believe He freely chooses what is best for you—even when you don't understand? What would change if you genuinely embraced that today?

No Alternatives Implies Need for Praise
The jurist argues that no alternatives imply God benefits and is in need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the jurist argues that if God could not have done otherwise, He would be in need of His actions for praise, which contradicts His perfection.

Core Idea
No alternatives implies God needs His actions for praise – a sign of need.

This lesson continues the jurist's critique. He now examines the implication for God's self-sufficiency. If God had no alternative, then He would be benefiting from His own action and would need it to receive praise. This, the jurist argues, is an attribute of need, which is incompatible with divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the jurist's argument.
2
Identify the conditional
Notice the 'if' statement: if it were not permissible for God to do otherwise, then He would be benefiting and in need.
3
Understand the logic of need
The jurist explains that whoever deserves praise only through something else is in need of that thing. Since God's actions are other than Him, He would need them for praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refutation of the Claim That God Cannot Do Otherwise
Maturidi refutes the deterministic claim using reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God cannot act otherwise implies injustice and need, and why this is impossible for God.

Core Idea
God's freedom of choice is essential; denying it leads to absurdity.

Maturidi now takes on a deterministic view that limits God's power. He shows that if God could only do what He did, His actions would lose their virtue and He would be dependent on His creation for praise. This short but dense argument uses logical consequences to defend divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the dense logical structure and the two main consequences Maturidi draws.
The argument moves from the opponents' premise to impossible conclusions.
2
Identify the two consequences
First, if God cannot do otherwise, His actions cannot be virtuous (since virtue implies choice) but bear the attribute of injustice. Second, God would be in need of His actions for praise, implying dependency.
These are drawn from the text: 'صفة الجور' and 'صفة الحاجة'.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why these consequences are impossible for the perfect, self-sufficient God. This shows the opponents' view is false.
Maturidi concludes that their view reduces God to a being of blame.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وجملة هذا الفصل أنه على قولهم إذ لم يكن له غير الذي فعل لم يكن شيء من فعله مفضلا؛ إذ هو أبقى بكل فعله صفة الجور؛ ولا كان لما يفعله مختارا له، إذ لو كان منه غير ذلك كان مُفسدا، وكان عن جعل الإصلاح في غيره عاجزا. وذلك هو النهاية صفة الذمّ. والله الموفق. ولو كان لا يجوز له غير الذي فعل لكان بفعله منتفعا، ويصير هو إليه محتاجا ليُحمد به ويثني عليه ; إذ من لا يستحق حمدا ولا مدحا إلا بغيره فهو إليه محتاج في أن يحق له الثناء، وبه منتفع . إذ من قولهم : إن فعله غيره، ولم يكن له تركه ولا غيرُ الذي فعله؛ إذ غيره يحط رتبته ويسفّهه . فثبت بما فعل النفع، وهو غيره عندهم. وهذه صفة الحاجة في عرف العقول. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said: The summary of this chapter is that according to their claim, since He has no other than what He did, nothing of His action is virtuous; for He [thereby] maintains the attribute of injustice in all His actions; and He would not be free in what He does, for if something else were from Him, it would be corrupting, and He would be incapable of making reform in other than Him. That is the ultimate attribute of blame. And God is the grantor of success. And if it were not permissible for Him other than what He did, then He would be benefiting from His action, and He would become in need of it to be praised and extolled; for one who deserves no praise or commendation except through another is in need of that other for praise to be due to him, and benefits from it. This is from their statement: that another did His action, and He had neither the power to leave it nor to do other than what He did; for another lowers His rank and declares Him foolish. So benefit is established through what He did, and it is other than Him according to them. And this is the attribute of need in the understanding of intellects. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the deterministic claim that God cannot do other than what He actually does. Maturidi begins by summarizing the argument of his opponents: if God has no alternative to what He does, then nothing He does can be considered virtuous or excellent, because virtue implies choice. Instead, all His actions would bear the characteristic of injustice (الجور), since He could not do otherwise. Moreover, He would not be free (مختار) in His actions, because if He were to do something else it would be corrupting, implying He is incapable of reform outside of His actual act. This is the utmost blame. Then Maturidi presents a further consequence: if God could not do otherwise, He would be benefiting from His own act (since the act brings Him praise) and thus would be in need of that act to be praised. But a being that needs something else for praise is dependent, which is impossible for God. The opponents' own statement implies that another (i.e., the act itself) is the real benefactor, lowering God's rank. Thus their view leads to ascribing need to God, which is absurd. The argument is closed with the formula 'There is no power except through God.'

Key Terms
الجور
الحاجة
صفة الذم
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is compelled to act in a certain way and cannot do otherwise.
Correction: The text shows this leads to attributes of injustice and need, which are impossible for God. God has perfect free choice and is self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about the view that God cannot do other than what He does?
A) It is a proof of God's power.
B) It implies God is unjust and in need.
C) It is the position of the Salaf.
D) It is consistent with divine wisdom.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God cannot do otherwise reduces Him to a being of injustice and need, contradicting His perfection and self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God could not have done other than what He did, would you still feel gratitude for His blessings? How does this lesson expose the necessity of divine choice for genuine gratitude?
?
Have you ever complained about your situation, implying that God's decree is wrong? How does Maturidi's argument against determinism correct this attitude and call you to trust God's wisdom and freedom?
?
Consider how often you praise God. If your praise were to add something to Him, would He need it? How does this lesson purify your intention in praise, making it purely for His sake and not for any benefit to Him?

The Necessity of Promise and Threat for Command
Maturidi establishes the necessity of reward and punishment for the meaning of command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine reward and punishment are essential for the very meaning of command and prohibition.

Core Idea
Without reward and punishment, commands and prohibitions lose their purpose.

Maturidi begins by grounding the necessity of promise and threat in the logic of command itself. He argues that if obedience brought no benefit and disobedience no harm, the very concepts of command and prohibition would be nullified. This establishes that reward and punishment are not arbitrary but intrinsic to divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'ثم' to 'في الحكمة' and its translation. Notice how Maturidi links promise/threat to inducement and deterrence.
Focus on the logical chain: absence of benefit/harm -> nullification of command.
2
Identify the key consequence
What would disappear if there were no promise and threat? The benefit of obedience and harm of disobedience. This would make command and prohibition meaningless.
Maturidi says 'يبطل معنى الأمر والنهي'.
3
Understand the wisdom
Maturidi concludes that promise and threat are necessary in wisdom. Ask: why is it not for the benefit of the commander? Because God does not benefit from our actions; the benefit is for us.
This refutes the misconception that reward/punishment are for God's benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الوعد والوعيد للترغيب والترهيب؛ إذ لولا ذلك يذهب نفع الائتمار وضرر العصيان، ولم يكن لمن خلق في فعلهم نفع . فإذا لم يكن للمؤتمر نفع ولا للعاصي ضرر يبطل معنى الأمر والنهي، إذ ليس لنفع الآمر والناهي ؛ فلذلك لزم الوعد والوعيد في الحكمة . مع ما في الأمر والنهي مجاهدة النفس وحملها على ما يكرهه الطبع، والذي يكرهه تنفر عنه النفس فلا يجد الممتحن على قهرها وصرفها إلى ما يريده ويؤمر به سبيلاً إلا بإحضار الوعد والوعيد، حتى إذا رأى ذلك سهل عليه ترك الملاذ، وهان عليه تحمل المؤَنِ العظام. وبعد، فإن البشر خلق خلقا قبح عليه فعل الذي لا يقصد به نفع العواقب، أو لا يتقى به ضرر العواقب، فلا بد أن يجعل لأعماله ذلك، وذلك حق الوعد والوعيد. ولولا ذلك لكان يستوى عواقب العدو والولي، وعلى ما تفاوتا هما بحيث الاختيار والإيثار، يجب تفاوت عواقبهما. وبالله التوفيق.
Then promise and threat are for inducement and deterrence; for without that, the benefit of obedience and the harm of disobedience would disappear, and there would be no benefit for the one created in their actions. So if there is no benefit for the obedient nor harm for the disobedient, the meaning of command and prohibition is nullified, for it is not for the benefit of the commander or prohibitor. Therefore, promise and threat are necessary in wisdom. Along with the fact that in command and prohibition there is striving against the soul and forcing it to do what it dislikes by nature, and what it dislikes the soul shuns. So the tested person has no way to overcome it and divert it to what he intends and is commanded except by presenting promise and threat. When he sees that, leaving pleasures becomes easy for him, and bearing great burdens becomes light. Furthermore, human beings were created with a nature that considers it ugly to do something from which no benefit of consequences is intended, or from which no harm of consequences is feared. So it is necessary to assign that to their actions, and that is the right of promise and threat. Without that, the consequences of the enemy and the friend would be equal, and since they differ in terms of choice and preference, their consequences must differ. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of divine reward and punishment (al-wa'd wa al-wa'id) as motivational forces for obedience and deterrence from disobedience. Maturidi explains that without promise and threat, the benefit (naf') of following commands and the harm (darar) of disobedience would be lost, and there would be no benefit for created beings in their actions. If there were no benefit for the obedient nor harm for the disobedient, the very meaning of command and prohibition would be nullified because commands and prohibitions are not for the benefit of the commander (God) but for the benefit of the commanded. Therefore, reward and punishment are necessary according to wisdom. Additionally, commands and prohibitions require struggling against the soul (mujahadat al-nafs), which naturally dislikes restrictions. People need the incentive of promise and threat to overcome their natural inclinations, making it easy to leave pleasures and bear hardships. Finally, human nature deems it ugly to act without considering beneficial or harmful consequences, so actions must have consequences. Without reward and punishment, the righteous and the wicked would have the same fate, which is unjust given their different choices. Thus, reward and punishment are obligatory in divine wisdom.

Key Terms
وعد
وعيد
ترغيب
ترهيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reward and punishment are for God's benefit or are arbitrary.
Correction: The passage states that command and prohibition are not for the benefit of the Commander; rather, they are for the benefit of the commanded. Reward and punishment are necessary in wisdom to make commands meaningful.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if there were no promise of reward and threat of punishment?
A) People would still obey out of natural inclination.
B) The benefit of obedience and harm of disobedience would disappear, nullifying the meaning of command and prohibition.
C) God would benefit from our obedience.
D) Command and prohibition would become optional.

Key Takeaway
Divine reward and punishment are logically necessary to give meaning to God's commands and prohibitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey God, is it because you desire His reward or fear His punishment? Does this lesson challenge you to purify your intention? What will you change today to obey out of love and wisdom?
?
If there were no reward or punishment, would you still obey God? This lesson says commands would be meaningless. Does your obedience depend on reward and punishment? If so, does that make your obedience less sincere? What do you need to work on?
?
Does the logical necessity of reward and punishment make you take God's commands more seriously? How does knowing that God's wisdom requires justice affect your daily choices?

Overcoming the Soul's Resistance through Promise and Threat
Maturidi explains the psychological role of reward and punishment in motivating obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how promise and threat help humans overcome the soul's natural aversion to obedience.

Core Idea
Commands require struggling against the soul; promise and threat make that struggle bearable.

Having established the logical necessity of reward and punishment, Maturidi now looks at the human psychological condition. Obeying divine commands often goes against our natural desires, and the soul instinctively shuns what it dislikes. Without the incentive of reward and the fear of punishment, we would have no effective means to overcome this resistance. Promise and threat ease the burden of obedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on mujahadah
Read the Arabic from 'مع ما في الأمر' to 'العظام' and its translation. Notice the emphasis on striving against the soul.
The term 'مجاهدة النفس' is key.
2
Understand the soul's aversion
The soul naturally shuns what it dislikes. Obedience often requires doing what is difficult or unpleasant. Without motivation, it's hard to persist.
Maturidi says 'تنفر عنه النفس'.
3
See how promise and threat help
The tested person has no way to overcome the soul except by presenting promise and threat. When they see the reward and punishment, leaving pleasures becomes easy and bearing burdens becomes light.
Focus on 'سهل عليه ترك الملاذ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الوعد والوعيد للترغيب والترهيب؛ إذ لولا ذلك يذهب نفع الائتمار وضرر العصيان، ولم يكن لمن خلق في فعلهم نفع . فإذا لم يكن للمؤتمر نفع ولا للعاصي ضرر يبطل معنى الأمر والنهي، إذ ليس لنفع الآمر والناهي ؛ فلذلك لزم الوعد والوعيد في الحكمة . مع ما في الأمر والنهي مجاهدة النفس وحملها على ما يكرهه الطبع، والذي يكرهه تنفر عنه النفس فلا يجد الممتحن على قهرها وصرفها إلى ما يريده ويؤمر به سبيلاً إلا بإحضار الوعد والوعيد، حتى إذا رأى ذلك سهل عليه ترك الملاذ، وهان عليه تحمل المؤَنِ العظام. وبعد، فإن البشر خلق خلقا قبح عليه فعل الذي لا يقصد به نفع العواقب، أو لا يتقى به ضرر العواقب، فلا بد أن يجعل لأعماله ذلك، وذلك حق الوعد والوعيد. ولولا ذلك لكان يستوى عواقب العدو والولي، وعلى ما تفاوتا هما بحيث الاختيار والإيثار، يجب تفاوت عواقبهما. وبالله التوفيق.
Then promise and threat are for inducement and deterrence; for without that, the benefit of obedience and the harm of disobedience would disappear, and there would be no benefit for the one created in their actions. So if there is no benefit for the obedient nor harm for the disobedient, the meaning of command and prohibition is nullified, for it is not for the benefit of the commander or prohibitor. Therefore, promise and threat are necessary in wisdom. Along with the fact that in command and prohibition there is striving against the soul and forcing it to do what it dislikes by nature, and what it dislikes the soul shuns. So the tested person has no way to overcome it and divert it to what he intends and is commanded except by presenting promise and threat. When he sees that, leaving pleasures becomes easy for him, and bearing great burdens becomes light. Furthermore, human beings were created with a nature that considers it ugly to do something from which no benefit of consequences is intended, or from which no harm of consequences is feared. So it is necessary to assign that to their actions, and that is the right of promise and threat. Without that, the consequences of the enemy and the friend would be equal, and since they differ in terms of choice and preference, their consequences must differ. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of divine reward and punishment (al-wa'd wa al-wa'id) as motivational forces for obedience and deterrence from disobedience. Maturidi explains that without promise and threat, the benefit (naf') of following commands and the harm (darar) of disobedience would be lost, and there would be no benefit for created beings in their actions. If there were no benefit for the obedient nor harm for the disobedient, the very meaning of command and prohibition would be nullified because commands and prohibitions are not for the benefit of the commander (God) but for the benefit of the commanded. Therefore, reward and punishment are necessary according to wisdom. Additionally, commands and prohibitions require struggling against the soul (mujahadat al-nafs), which naturally dislikes restrictions. People need the incentive of promise and threat to overcome their natural inclinations, making it easy to leave pleasures and bear hardships. Finally, human nature deems it ugly to act without considering beneficial or harmful consequences, so actions must have consequences. Without reward and punishment, the righteous and the wicked would have the same fate, which is unjust given their different choices. Thus, reward and punishment are obligatory in divine wisdom.

Key Terms
مجاهدة النفس
الطبع
النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People can obey God without any external incentive, purely out of love or rationality.
Correction: Maturidi argues that human nature requires incentive; the soul naturally avoids discomfort, so promise and threat are necessary to motivate obedience.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what makes leaving pleasures and bearing burdens easy for a person?
A) Natural human strength
B) The presence of promise and threat
C) The absence of desires
D) Divine compulsion

Key Takeaway
Promise and threat provide essential psychological motivation that enables humans to overcome the soul's resistance and obey divine commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What specific command of Allah do you find hardest to obey because your soul dislikes it? In light of this lesson, how will you bring the promise of reward and threat of punishment to mind the next time you face that command?
?
When you sin, do you often forget about the threat of punishment because you focus only on the immediate pleasure? What concrete step will you take to remind yourself of the warning before you act?
?
The passage says that seeing promise and threat makes leaving pleasures 'easy' – but you may still find it hard. Is it because you don't truly believe in the promise and threat? What would need to change in your heart for them to have the effect Maturidi describes?

Human Nature Requires Consequential Actions
Maturidi argues that human nature necessitates differing consequences for actions, justifying reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human nature demands that actions have different consequences, making reward and punishment necessary.

Core Idea
Human nature deems it ugly to act without considering outcomes; reward and punishment align with this.

Maturidi's final point grounds the necessity of reward and punishment in the very disposition of human beings. We naturally find it unreasonable and ugly to perform actions without any beneficial or harmful outcome. This inherent sense of justice requires that the righteous and the wicked have different fates, otherwise their choices would be treated equally, which is unjust.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding argument
Read the Arabic from 'وبعد' to the end and its translation. Notice the reference to human nature ('خلق') and the ugliness of action without consequences.
Maturidi uses 'قبح عليه' meaning something deemed ugly by nature.
2
Understand the necessity of consequences
Human beings have a natural disposition that considers it ugly to do something without intending beneficial consequences or avoiding harmful ones. Therefore, actions must have consequences assigned to them.
This is the 'right of promise and threat'.
3
Contrast the fates of the righteous and wicked
Without differing consequences, the enemy and the friend would have the same outcome. But since they differ in choice and preference, their consequences must also differ.
Justice demands differentiation.

Reward as Grace and Justice in Punishment
Maturidi explains that reward is grace due to prior blessings, while punishment is just.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reward is considered divine grace (fadl) while punishment is a matter of justice (adl) in Maturidi's theology.

Core Idea
Reward is grace, not entitlement; punishment is justice, not cruelty.

Maturidi tackles a profound question: does God owe us reward for good deeds? He argues that because Allah has already given us countless blessings that obligate gratitude, any additional reward is pure grace. Conversely, punishment for evil is exactly what justice requires. In this lesson, we'll read his argument and the Quranic proof he offers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice Maturidi's main claim: all reward is grace because of prior blessings.
Pay attention to the Quranic verse he cites.
2
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Identify the verse (6:160) and how Maturidi uses it to contrast reward multiplication with equal punishment.
The verse shows that punishment is exactly matched, while reward is multiplied.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Think about why this distinction matters: it preserves God's justice while highlighting His generosity. Humans cannot demand reward as a right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أمكن أن يجعل الثواب كله فضلاً، إذ قد سبق من الله من النعم ما استحق الشكر عليه بغاية ما احتمل الوسع، فيكون الثواب فضلاً من الله . ثم كذلك المضاعفة في ذلك، كقوله تعالى : من جاء بالحسنة فله عشر أمثالها ومن جاء بالسيئة فلا يجزى إلا مثلها [الأنعام، ٦ / ١٦٠]، فذكر في السيئة ما توجبه الحكمة من الجزاء، وضاعف في الثواب على ما يحتمله الإفضال، إذ ذلك أصله . ولا قوة إلا بالله . فهذا فيما احتمله عقولنا مما يُلزم الأمر والنهي.
And it is possible that all reward is made grace, for there has preceded from Allah blessings that necessitated gratitude to the utmost of capacity, so reward would be grace from Allah. Then likewise the multiplication in that, as His saying, the Most High: 'Whoever comes with a good deed shall have ten times its like, and whoever comes with an evil deed shall be recompensed only with its like' (al-An'am 6:160). So He mentioned in the evil deed what wisdom requires of recompense, and multiplied the reward according to what generosity allows, for that is its principle. And there is no power except with Allah. This is what our intellects comprehend regarding what necessitates command and prohibition.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage addresses the nature of reward and punishment, emphasizing that reward is ultimately a grace (fadl) from Allah, not a strict entitlement. Maturidi argues that Allah has already given countless blessings that obligate gratitude from humans to the utmost of their capacity, so any additional reward for good deeds is pure grace. He supports this with the Qur'anic verse (6:160) which promises tenfold reward for good deeds but only equal recompense for evil. The verse demonstrates that punishment for evil is exactly what wisdom requires (justice), while the multiplication of reward is due to divine generosity (ifdal). This is the principle. The passage concludes by noting that human intellects can grasp this much regarding the necessity of commands and prohibitions.

Key Terms
فضل
إفضال
ثواب
جزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That humans can demand reward from God as a right for their good deeds.
Correction: Reward is not earned but granted by God's grace; humans cannot demand it as a right because prior blessings already obligate gratitude.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is reward considered grace (fadl)?
A) Because humans deserve it by their actions.
B) Because Allah's prior blessings already obligated gratitude.
C) Because God is forced to reward.
D) Because punishment is also grace.

Key Takeaway
Reward is a manifestation of God's grace, not a necessary recompense, while punishment is exactly what justice demands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform good deeds, do you secretly feel that Allah owes you reward? How does the fact that even your deeds are only a return for His prior blessings shake that assumption?
?
If punishment is exactly just, and reward is entirely grace, does this change how you view your sins and your hopes for Paradise? What would it mean to live with the constant awareness that every good thing you receive is unearned?
?
The passage says that Allah's blessings require gratitude to the utmost capacity. Are you truly giving gratitude as much as you can? What keeps you from doing so, and what does that say about your sense of entitlement?

Reward as Grace, Not Obligation
Maturidi argues that reward is entirely God's grace, not obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that divine reward is entirely a matter of God's grace, not an obligation.

Core Idea
Reward is a gift, not a paycheck.

Maturidi addresses a critical theological question: does God owe humans anything? He argues that all reward is a favor because God's prior blessings already obligate gratitude. This passage establishes the foundation for understanding divine justice and generosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
2
Identify Maturidi's claim about reward and punishment
Notice that Maturidi states reward can be entirely grace (fadl), while punishment is based on wisdom. Find where he cites the Quranic verse 6:160.
3
Understand the role of prior blessings
Maturidi argues that God has already given blessings that obligate gratitude. Thus any further reward is a favor. Why does this make reward grace?
4
Examine the intellect and messengers
Maturidi links the argument to the intellect and the proofs brought by messengers. Even if we cannot grasp full wisdom, the messengers provide sufficient evidence.
5
Reflect on the analogy of limbs
The intellect is compared to other limbs that must be used for benefit. How does this analogy support the obligation to use reason?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أمكن أن يجعل الثواب كله فضلاً، إذ قد سبق من الله من النعم ما استحق الشكر عليه بغاية ما احتمل الوسع، فيكون الثواب فضلاً من الله . ثم كذلك المضاعفة في ذلك، كقوله تعالى : من جاء بالحسنة فله عشر أمثالها ومن جاء بالسيئة فلا يجزى إلا مثلها [الأنعام، ٦/١٦٠]، فذكر في السيئة ما توجبه الحكمة من الجزاء، وضاعف في الثواب على ما يحتمله الإفضال، إذ ذلك أصله . ولا قوة إلا بالله . فهذا فيما احتمله عقولنا مما يُلزم الأمر والنهي. ومع ما كان فيما جاء بهما الرسل عن دليل كاف يُلزم القول بعظم الحكمة فيهما لو قصرت عقولنا عن الوقوف على ذلك. مع ما في العقل إباء ترك استعماله كسائر الجوارح التي لم يُحتمل تعطيلها عن المنافع التي فمثله العقل . مع ما كان الذي ذكرت في سائر الجوارح هو حق الفعل أيضا وإشارته. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
It is possible to make all reward as grace, since there have already preceded from God blessings that obligate gratitude to the utmost capacity, so reward becomes grace from God. Likewise doubling in that, as His saying: 'Whoever comes with a good deed shall have ten times its like, and whoever comes with an evil deed shall be recompensed only with its like' [al-An'am 6:160]; so He mentioned for the evil deed what wisdom requires of recompense, and He multiplied the reward according to what generosity allows, for that is its origin. There is no power except with God. So this is within what our minds can comprehend of what obligates command and prohibition. And with what the messengers brought of sufficient proof that obligates saying the greatness of wisdom in them, even if our minds fall short of grasping that. Along with what is in the intellect of aversion to leaving its use like the rest of limbs that cannot be abandoned from the benefits that—so the intellect is similar. Along with what I mentioned regarding the rest of limbs is indeed the right of action and its indication. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of divine reward. Maturidi begins by stating that it is possible for God to make all reward entirely a matter of grace (fadl), not obligation. The reason is that God has already bestowed so many blessings that fully obligate gratitude; thus any additional reward is a favor. He then addresses the doubling of rewards, citing Quran 6:160 which promises tenfold for good deeds but only equivalent for evil. Maturidi observes that for evil deeds, the recompense is exactly what wisdom requires, but for good deeds, multiplication is according to divine generosity. This establishes that reward is based on grace, while punishment is based on justice. He then links this to the intellect's role: our minds can grasp the obligation of commands and prohibitions. Even if we cannot fully comprehend the wisdom behind them, the messengers provide sufficient proof. He concludes by analogizing the intellect to other faculties that must be used for benefit—likewise, the intellect cannot be neglected. The passage ends with a repeated formula of reliance on God.

ثواب
فضل
حكمة
إفضال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think reward is owed to humans as a right.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that reward is a gift of grace because God's prior blessings already obligate gratitude; additional reward is not owed.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is reward considered grace (fadl) rather than obligation?
A) Because God's prior blessings already obligate gratitude, so further reward is a favor.
B) Because humans deserve reward due to their efforts.
C) Because punishment is also a form of grace.
D) Because the intellect cannot grasp obligation.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that all divine reward is a gift of grace, not an earned wage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you fall into sin, do you minimize it because you assume God's mercy will balance the scales? Maturidi says evil is repaid exactly—does that reality make you fear the justice of God? Or does it drive you to seek His grace more earnestly?
?
You have received countless blessings before any act of obedience—your existence, health, faith. How does this truth affect your expectation of reward? Are you willing to serve God without any promise of reward, simply because He is worthy? What would that look like in your daily life?

God's Identity as Existence
Maturidi defines identity as a metaphor for existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in the witnessed world, 'identity' is a metaphor for existence, meaning the negation of non-existence, and how this applies to Allah's eternal existence.

Core Idea
God's identity is His existence—eternal and without beginning.

Maturidi begins by defining 'identity' (al-huwiyya). In our observable world, the word 'identity' is used to refer to existence itself; it implies that something is not non-existent. For Allah, this means He has always existed and will always exist, never subject to non-existence. This lesson unpacks this foundational concept before moving on to God's unchanging nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition of identity
The passage says: 'ثم الهوية في الشاهد كناية عن الوجود، وتأويله نفي العدم عنه'. Read this carefully.
Notice that 'identity' is not a physical form but a reference to existence.
2
Understand the metaphor
Identity is a metaphor for existence, and its interpretation is negation of non-existence. This means when we say something has identity, we affirm its existence and deny its non-existence.
3
Apply to Allah
Allah's identity is His existence; He is not subject to non-existence. This is the basis for understanding His eternity and self-sufficiency.
This idea is central to tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الهوية في الشاهد كناية عن الوجود، وتأويله نفي العدم عنه. والله تعالى لم يزل ولا يزال بلا تغيّر ولا زوال ولا انتقال من حال إلى حال ولا تحرك ولا قرار. إذ هو وصف اختلاف تختلف الأحوال عليه فهو غير مفارق لها؛ ومن لا يفارق الأحوال، وهُنَّ أحداث، يجب بها الوصف بالأحداث. وفي ذلك سقوط الوحدانية ثم القدم، ثم جرى الأحوال، ومن
Then identity [al-huwiyya] in the witnessed is a metaphor for existence, and its interpretation is negation of non-existence from it. And Allah, exalted, has not ceased and will not cease, without change, passing away, transition from one state to another, movement, or rest. For it is a description of difference; states differ upon Him, so He is not inseparable from them. And whoever does not separate from states, and they are temporal occurrences, it necessitates that he is described by temporal occurrences. And in that is the loss of oneness, then pre-eternity, then the course of states, and from
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage discusses the term 'identity' (الهوية) and God's transcendence. In witnessed things, 'identity' is a metaphor for existence, meaning negation of non-existence. God is eternal, without beginning or end, without change, passing away, transition, movement, or rest. Maturidi then gives a logical argument: If God did not separate from states (i.e., change with them), then since states are temporal, He would be described as temporal. That would negate His oneness and pre-eternity. The text ends abruptly, but the argument is clear: God must be unchanging and beyond all temporal attributes.

Key Terms
هوية
وجود
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'identity' refers to a physical form or essence distinct from existence.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that identity is a metaphor for existence itself, not a separate attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does 'identity' (huwiyya) mean in the witnessed world?
A) A physical form
B) A metaphor for existence
C) A name for the soul
D) A property of matter

Key Takeaway
Identity is not a form but a reference to existence, and for Allah, it means He is eternally existent, never subject to non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own identity, do you anchor it in your existence as a creation or in the One whose very identity is existence? How often do you affirm Allah's necessary existence in your daily thoughts?
?
If Allah's identity is simply that He exists without beginning, how does that confront your tendency to think of Allah in human terms? What would change if you truly internalized that He is not like anything you can imagine?
?
The passage says identity negates non-existence. Do you live with the awareness that your own existence is dependent and could cease at any moment, while His existence is necessary? How does that difference affect your reliance on Him?

God's Transcendence of Change
Maturidi affirms God's immutability and argues that change implies temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah is unchanging and that any change would imply temporality, which negates His oneness and pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Allah does not change because change belongs to temporal beings.

After establishing that God's identity is His eternal existence, Maturidi now explains what that means: Allah has no change, no transition, no movement, no rest. He then presents a powerful argument: if states of change happened to Allah, He would be inseparable from temporal occurrences, and that would make Him temporal. This would destroy tawhid and the attribute of pre-eternity. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of God's changelessness
The passage says 'لم يزل ولا يزال بلا تغيّر ولا زوال ولا انتقال من حال إلى حال ولا تحرك ولا قرار'. Read this and reflect on each negation.
This affirms that God is beyond all categories of change.
2
Understand the argument about states
Maturidi says: If God did not separate from states (i.e., if they affected Him), then since states are temporal events, He would be described as temporal. This would negate His oneness and pre-eternity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Consider the conclusion
The text ends abruptly but the conclusion is clear: God must be unchanging and beyond temporal attributes.
The incomplete sentence suggests Maturidi was about to elaborate further.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الهوية في الشاهد كناية عن الوجود، وتأويله نفي العدم عنه. والله تعالى لم يزل ولا يزال بلا تغيّر ولا زوال ولا انتقال من حال إلى حال ولا تحرك ولا قرار. إذ هو وصف اختلاف تختلف الأحوال عليه فهو غير مفارق لها؛ ومن لا يفارق الأحوال، وهُنَّ أحداث، يجب بها الوصف بالأحداث. وفي ذلك سقوط الوحدانية ثم القدم، ثم جرى الأحوال، ومن
Then identity [al-huwiyya] in the witnessed is a metaphor for existence, and its interpretation is negation of non-existence from it. And Allah, exalted, has not ceased and will not cease, without change, passing away, transition from one state to another, movement, or rest. For it is a description of difference; states differ upon Him, so He is not inseparable from them. And whoever does not separate from states, and they are temporal occurrences, it necessitates that he is described by temporal occurrences. And in that is the loss of oneness, then pre-eternity, then the course of states, and from
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage discusses the term 'identity' (الهوية) and God's transcendence. In witnessed things, 'identity' is a metaphor for existence, meaning negation of non-existence. God is eternal, without beginning or end, without change, passing away, transition, movement, or rest. Maturidi then gives a logical argument: If God did not separate from states (i.e., change with them), then since states are temporal, He would be described as temporal. That would negate His oneness and pre-eternity. The text ends abruptly, but the argument is clear: God must be unchanging and beyond all temporal attributes.

Why 'Body' Cannot Be Attributed to Allah
Maturidi refutes the attribution of 'body' to Allah by showing it implies limitation and direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that attributing 'body' (jism) to Allah is impossible due to its implications of limitation and direction.

Core Idea
Body implies limits and directions; Allah is beyond both.

Imam Maturidi tackles a subtle theological error: thinking that because the world is finite and points to a Creator, the Creator must also be limited in some way. Here he clarifies that while we can affirm the world's existence as a sign of Allah, the world's finitude actually proves the opposite about Allah—that He is not limited. This passage carefully distinguishes between affirming Allah's existence (which is necessary) and imposing human categories like 'body' on Him (which is false).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English. Notice how Maturidi defines 'body' before refuting it.
The definition is key: 'body is a name for everything limited.'
2
Understand the 'thing' vs 'body' distinction
Maturidi says 'thing' (shay') is mere affirmation of existence. The world's finitude proves existence, not that the Creator is limited. Distinguish between affirming that Allah exists (thing) and describing Him as a limited body.
This distinction prevents a common misunderstanding.
3
Analyze the argument from direction
Body in the witnessed world implies directions (jiha), which are relative. If Allah were a body, He would be subject to spatial relations, impossible for the Creator.
Directions like above, below, etc., are created and cannot encompass Allah.
4
Reflect on the exception
Maturidi notes that if by 'limit' one means oneness and lordship, it's acceptable, but the term normally implies physical boundaries. Understand why this nuance matters.
This shows Maturidi's precision—he doesn't reject all uses of 'limit,' only those implying finitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجسم فهو اسم لكل محدود والشيء إثبات لا غير. وفي وجود العالم على ما عليه دليل الإثبات؛ لذلك قيل بالشيء، وفيه ـ إذ هو متناه لا من حيث الشيئية [بل من حيث هو] تحت الحد ـ دليل نفي الحد عن الله جل ثناؤه إلا أن يُراد بالحد الوحدانية والربوبية، فهو كذلك، وحرف الحد ساقط؛ لأنه يغلب في الدلالة على نهاية الشيء من طريق العرض ونحو ذلك مما يتعالى عن ذلك، وذلك معنى الجسم في الشاهد. وفيه أيضاً إيجاب الجهات المحتمل كل جهة أن يكون أطول منها وأعرض وأقصر. فلذلك بطل القول بذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And as for body, it is a name for everything limited, and 'thing' is mere affirmation. And in the existence of the world as it is, there is evidence for affirmation; therefore it is said by 'thing', and in it — since it is finite not from the aspect of thingness but from the aspect of being under limit — there is evidence for negation of limit from Allah, glorified be His praise, unless by limit is meant oneness and lordship, then it is so, and the letter of limit is dropped, because it prevails in indicating the end of a thing via accident and the like of what is transcendent above that, and that is the meaning of body in the witnessed world. And in it also is the necessity of directions, each direction being possibly longer, wider, and shorter than another. Therefore the statement [that Allah is a body] is nullified. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of attributing 'body' (jism) to Allah. Maturidi first defines body as everything limited (mahdud). The world, being finite, points to a Creator who is not limited. The world's finitude is evidence not only of the Creator's existence (as per the 'thing' argument) but also of His being beyond limit. If by 'limit' one means oneness and lordship, that is acceptable semantically, but the term 'limit' usually implies boundaries, which is impossible for Allah. Moreover, in the witnessed world, body implies direction (jiha), and directions are relative and varying in size; thus body would subject Allah to spatial relations, which is impossible. Hence the attribution of body to Allah is invalid.

Misconception: Some think that because the world is finite, its Creator must also be finite or limited in some way.
Correction: Maturidi argues the opposite: the world's finitude proves that the Creator is beyond all limits; it is evidence of His transcendence, not His limitation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Attributing 'body' to Allah is impossible because it implies limitation and direction, both of which contradict divine transcendence.

Maturidi warns that even the word 'limit' can mislead. In your daily speech about Allah, do you use words that carry hidden assumptions of finitude or bodily likeness? Pause and examine one phrase you often use—does it reflect tanzih or tashbih?

The Multiple Meanings of 'Ma Huwa' About God
Maturidi classifies five meanings of 'ma huwa' about God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the question 'ma huwa' (what is He?) about God has multiple valid interpretations, each requiring a different answer, and that it cannot imply composition or materiality.

Core Idea
Not all 'what is?' questions about God ask about His essence.

When someone asks 'What is God?,' the answer depends on what they really mean. Imam Maturidi unpacks the ambiguity of this question by showing that it can refer to God's attributes, His actions, or even serve as a request for an example from creation. This lesson introduces his classification and the proper responses for each type.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the passage heading: 'The relation of quiddity, modality, and nearness to God.' Then notice how Maturidi uses Quranic examples to show that 'ma huwa' can be a metaphor for God's name.
The Quranic examples set the stage for the classification.
2
First meaning: asking about His name or attribute
Identify the first usage: answering Pharaoh's question with 'Lord, Creator, Maker, Knower.' This shows that 'ma huwa' can ask about God's attributes.
3
Second meaning: asking about His attribute (siffa)
Find where Maturidi says 'ma huwa' may mean 'what is His attribute?' The answer is 'Hearing, Seeing.'
This is closely related to the first but distinguished as asking specifically about a single attribute.
4
Third and fourth meanings: example and action
Learn the meaning where 'ma huwa' asks for an example from creation (answering with 'staff'), and the meaning where it asks about His action (answering with 'creating and placing with wisdom').
These meanings show that 'ma huwa' is flexible.
5
Fifth meaning: origin (rejected)
Understand the final meaning: 'from what is He?' Maturidi rejects this completely, stating God is exalted above being from something.
This guards against materialistic conceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: نسبة الماهية والكيفية والقرب إلى الله تعالى. وقول الرجل قد يُكنى به عن اسمه، كقول فرعون: {وما رب العالمين؟} قال: {رب السموات والأرض} الآية، وقول الله لموسى: {وما تلك بيمينك يا موسى؟} قال: {عصاي} الآية. فجواب الأول أن يقال: رب خالق بارئ عليم. وقد يكون «ما هو»: ما صفته؟ فجوابه: سميع بصير. و«ما هو»: أي عما يعرف له مائية في الخلق، فهو المثال. و«ما هو» يحتمل: ما فعله؟ فجوابه: خلق الخلق ووضع كل شيء موضعه، وذلك حكمته. وقد يحتمل «ما هو»: أي ممن هو؟ فهو يتعالى عن أن يكون من شيء، بل هو مكوّن الأشياء. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Question: The relation of quiddity (mahiyya), modality (kayfiyya), and nearness (qurb) to God the Exalted. And the statement of a man may be used as a metaphor for His name, like the saying of Pharaoh: 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' He said: 'Lord of the heavens and the earth' the verse, and the saying of God to Moses: 'And what is that in your right hand, O Moses?' He said: 'My staff' the verse. So the answer to the first is to say: Lord, Creator, Maker, Knower. And 'ma huwa' may [mean]: what is His attribute? So its answer: Hearing, Seeing. And 'ma huwa': i.e., about what is known to have a quiddity (mahiyya) in creation, so it is [asking for] an example. And 'ma huwa' may carry [the meaning]: what is His action? So its answer: creating the creation and placing everything in its proper place, and that is His wisdom. And 'ma huwa' may carry [the meaning]: i.e., from what is He? So He is exalted above being from something; rather, He is the maker of things. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the different meanings of the question 'ma huwa' (what is He?) when asked about God. Maturidi begins with a heading: 'The relation of quiddity (mahiyya), modality (kayfiyya), and nearness (qurb) to God.' He then notes that sometimes a person's statement can be a metaphor for His name, citing Quranic examples: Pharaoh asked 'What is the Lord of the worlds?' and Moses answered 'Lord of the heavens and the earth.' Similarly, God asked Moses 'What is that in your right hand?' and Moses answered 'My staff.' Maturidi then explains that the answer to the first question (about the Lord) is to say: 'Lord, Creator, Maker, Knower' — i.e., describing Him by His attributes. Then he analyzes the phrase 'ma huwa' into four possible meanings: 1) Asking about His attribute (siffa): the answer is 'Hearing, Seeing.' 2) Asking about something that has a quiddity (mahiyya) in creation: then it is asking for an example, so the answer is a created thing like the staff. 3) Asking about His action (fi'l): the answer is creating creation and placing everything in its place, which is His wisdom. 4) Asking about His origin (min huwa): then He is exalted above being from something; rather, He is the Maker (mukawwin) of things. The passage shows that 'ma huwa' is ambiguous and must be interpreted according to the context; when applied to God, it cannot imply composition or materiality.

Key Terms
ماهية (mahiyya)
كيفية (kayfiyya)
ما هو (ma huwa)
صفة (siffa)
فعل (fi'l)
مثال (mithal)
حكمة (hikma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The question 'ma huwa' (what is He?) always asks about God's essence or quiddity (mahiyya).
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'ma huwa' can ask about God's attributes, actions, or for an example from creation, and explicitly rejects the interpretation that asks about God's origin or material composition.

Checkpoint
Which interpretation of 'ma huwa' does Maturidi explicitly reject as impossible for God?
A) Asking about His attribute (siffa)
B) Asking about His action (fi'l)
C) Asking for an example (mithal) from creation
D) Asking about His origin (min huwa)

Key Takeaway
The question 'what is God?' must be answered according to its intended meaning: it can refer to His names, attributes, actions, or require an example from creation, but never implies composition or origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Multiple Meanings of 'What is He?' About God
Explains four possible meanings of 'ma huwa' about God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the question 'What is He?' (ma huwa) when asked about God has multiple valid interpretations depending on context, and each interpretation requires a different answer.

Core Idea
The question 'What is He?' about God has four distinct meanings.

When we ask 'What is He?' about God, the answer depends on what we are really asking. Imam al-Maturidi explains that this question can refer to God's attributes, His essence in relation to creation, His actions, or His origin. Each interpretation yields a different answer, and understanding this prevents confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for its structure. Notice the repetition of 'ما هو' and the different responses.
The passage is only a few lines but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the four interpretations
The passage lists four distinct meanings of 'ma huwa'. Find each one and understand the answer given. They are: attribute, likeness, action, origin.
Use the translation to help.
3
Notice the negation 'يتعالى'
For the first and last interpretations, the answer includes 'He is exalted above' (يتعالى). This shows that certain questions are inappropriate for God.
This teaches adab (proper etiquette) in asking about God.
4
Reflect on the wisdom (حكمة)
The third interpretation relates to God's actions and His wisdom in creation. Note how the answer combines creation and placing things in their proper places.
5
Connect to the common misunderstanding
Many assume 'ma huwa' always asks about essence. This passage shows it can be about attributes, actions, or origin. Understand why that assumption is too narrow.
This is the key takeaway.

Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage explains the different possible meanings of the question 'ma huwa' (What is He?) when asked about God. The author first notes that if the question is about God's attributes, the answer is that He is Hearing and Seeing. If the question is about His essence or 'whatness' that exists in creation, then it is asking for a likeness — but God is exalted above having any likeness. If the question is about His actions, the answer is that He created creation and placed everything in its proper place, which is His wisdom. Finally, if the question is about His origin ('from what is He?'), the answer is that He is not from anything; rather, He is the Maker of all things. Each interpretation is addressed according to its proper context.

Why God Cannot Be Described by His Creation
Refutes describing God by attributes realized through His creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is invalid to describe God by the effects of His actions in creation, and how the argument unfolds using rational principles.

Core Idea
God cannot be described by what He creates—that would make Him dependent on His creation.

Imam Maturidi addresses a subtle theological error: thinking that because God creates something (like sickness), He can be described by that thing (like being called 'sick'). This passage shows why such a description is rationally impossible, using arguments from self-sufficiency, need, and the impossibility of change in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the literal English translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before interpreting.
Pay attention to the phrase 'القول بفعله' which is the key term.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage rejects describing God by attributes realized through creation. Find the sentence that states the general principle and the specific case.
The general principle is in the first sentence; the specific case starts with 'ثم القول بفعله'.
3
Follow the reasoning
Notice the reasons given: (1) it would make God benefited by creation, (2) this is not known in the witnessed world, (3) it implies need and change, (4) it would open the door to describing God by any creature.
Each reason starts with 'لما' or 'ولما' – mark them.
4
Reflect on the core principle
God is self-sufficient (ghaniyy), so He cannot be described by anything that implies dependence or benefit from creation.
Connect this to the concept of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجوه التي هي حقيقة تلك الصفات والتي يحققها غيره في الله - لا أنه بذاته يوصف بها - فإنه فاسد؛ لأنه لا يخلو من أن يكون له مدح وتمجيد وتعظيم، فيكون له ذلك بغيره، فيصير بخلقه الخلق ممدوحا منتفعا، وهو الغني بنفسه يتعالى عن أن يكون له بأحد مدح أو نفع. فلذلك لا يوصف بذلك، جل جلاله. ثم القول بفعله، فإنه لا يجوز أن يكون مفعوله، لما لا يُعرف ذلك في الشاهد، ولما يوصف به ولا يوصف بغيره، ولما بينا أن الوصف بغيره يوجب الحاجة إليه ويوصف به في الأزل لما بينا من إحالة التغيّر والزوال، ولما لو جاز الوصف بما هو حال في غيره لجاز الوصف بكل شيء من خلقه، وذلك ممتنع. وقد بيّنا هذا فيما تقدم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
As for the aspects that are the reality of those attributes and which another [creature] realizes in God—not that He is described by them in His essence—that is invalid; because it is unavoidable that He would have praise, glorification, and exaltation through something else, and thus He would become praised and benefited through His creating creation, while He is self-sufficient, exalted above having any praise or benefit from anyone. Therefore He is not described by that, exalted is His majesty. Then [concerning] the statement about His action: it is not permissible that it be His effect (maf'ūl), because that is not known in the witnessed world, and because He is described by it and not described by something else, and because we have explained that description by something else necessitates need for it, and [He would be] described by it in pre-eternity, given the impossibility of change and passing, and because if description by what is inherent in another were permissible, then description by everything in His creation would be permissible, which is impossible. And we have explained this previously. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage argues against describing God by attributes that are realized in Him through others (i.e., by the effects of His actions in creation). The author says that if God were described by something that comes from His creation, He would be praised and benefited by that creation, which is impossible because He is self-sufficient. Then it turns to the specific case of describing God by His action (القول بفعله): God cannot be described by the result of His action (like being called 'sick' because He created sickness). The reasons given: (1) this is not known in the witnessed world, (2) He is described by the action itself (e.g., He creates sickness) not by the effect, (3) describing by something else implies need and would require change in His essence, and (4) if it were allowed, anything in creation could become a description of God, which is absurd.

Key Terms
القول بفعله
مفعوله
حاجة
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because God creates sickness, He can be described as 'sick'.
Correction: The author argues that this is invalid because it implies need, benefit, change, and opens the door to describing God by any creature.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say describing God by the effect of His action (like sickness) is invalid?
A) Because God is self-sufficient and cannot be praised by creation
B) Because it would imply God changes
C) Both A and B
D) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
It is rationally impossible to describe God by the effects of His actions because it would compromise His self-sufficiency and imply change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's mercy, do you base it on the mercy He creates in people? How does this lesson expose a hidden neediness in that description, and will you instead affirm His mercy as essential, independent of creation?
?
If God is truly self-sufficient, why do you sometimes act as if your worship adds something to Him? How will you train your heart to worship without expecting God to 'need' your praise?
?
Reflect on a time you described God by something He did for you (e.g., 'He gave me this, so He is kind'). According to this passage, what is wrong with that? Will you commit to correcting your speech and thinking to describe God only by what He is in Himself?

Turning the Dualist Argument Back
Maturidi refutes the Dualist excuse by turning the argument back.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi turns the Dualist claim that defects cause misperception back against them.

Core Idea
If darkness clouds the Dualist's vision, their objection itself may be flawed.

The Dualists argue that observed contradictions are due to admixture of darkness in light, causing misperception. Imam Maturidi responds by pointing out that if this is true, the same defect could affect the Dualist's own judgment, making their argument self-defeating. This lesson focuses on that initial rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Dualist claim
Read the opening lines where the Dualist asserts that defects cause misperception of things. Note their reasoning.
They claim that in the witnessed world, darkness mixed with light distorts perception.
2
Identify the author's counter
Find where Imam Maturidi turns the argument: if the Dualist's perception is clouded, then their judgment that the author's statements are not wisdom could also be due to that defect.
This is a classic ad hominem, but here it's logical: the same principle undermines their own position.
3
Reflect on the power of the rebuttal
Consider why this rebuttal is effective. It does not require external proof, only consistency with the Dualist's own premise.
The author thus defends the consistency of his own position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن زعمت الثنوية في جميع ما عارضنا من اختلاف الأحوال وتضادّها : أن ذلك كذلك في الشاهد لشوائب الآفات من الظلمة في جوهر النور فيرى الشيء بغير صورته، وبها يقع التواتر للعلم بالأشياء. قيل : فما يبعد أن يكون قولك كذا ليس بحكمة ولا رحمة ؟ بل هو سفه وقسوة، إنما كان منك لما شابك من آفات الظلمة فمنعك أن ترى كل شيء بجوهره وصورته. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم الله سبحانه إذ هو القادر بذاته لا يعجزه شيء، الغني بنفسه لا يُحوجه شيء، العليم بذاته لا يجوز أن يجهل شيئًا، الحكيم بذاته لا يجوز الخطأ منه في الفعل، بطل أن يكون في خلقه تفاوت تتناقض لديه الشهادة ويتضادّ فيه التدبير؛ ولزم القول بكل ما لا تبلغه عقولنا بدرك الحكمة ـ بعد أن ثبت أنه مُنشؤه ومحدثه - أن نعلم أن فيه حكمة بليغة لم تبلغها . . على ما لا يعلم أن كل حاسة من حواسنا جعلت لدرك ما تقع هي عليه، وإن كانت تقصر ربما عن الإحاطة به ، وتجيئ حاسة أخرى فتحيط به. فمثله العقل، إذ هو مخلوق محدود لا يجاوز الحد الذي جعل له؛ مع ما كان موجودًا فيه قبح كل شيء يظهر حسنه وفساد شيء يظهر صلاحه. فثبت أنه ربما يعتريه ما يمنعه عن فهم كنه ما يقع عليه من الحكمة والسفه. وبعد، فأنّى تقدير جهة الحكمة ممن هو محتاج فقير يُحبَّب إليه حاجته ويزيَّن في عينه فقره [و] حسن أشياء قبيحة، بالعادة والإلف، وكذلك أضدادها؟ فإن من هذا وصفه من الإحاطة بحكمة الربوبية، ولتلك الآفات أيضا، عاجز عن إنشاء فعل لا عن شيء؛ إذ هو يتقلب بالجوارح ويستعمل الآلات فأنى يكون لِمَن ذلك مَحلّه في فعله ـ بعد علمه أنه يعمل بقوة أحدثت وعلم أفيد - التحكم بالعجز والجهل على من هو بذاته قادر، عالم بالعجز عن مثله والجهل. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
If the Dualists claim in all that we have objected to of the variation and opposition of states that this is so in the witnessed world due to the admixture of defects from darkness in the essence of light, so that one sees a thing other than its true form, and through that occurs the transmission of knowledge of things. It is said: What prevents your statement from being not wisdom nor mercy? Rather it is folly and harshness, it is only from you because of the admixture of defects of darkness that prevented you from seeing everything in its essence and form. And there is no power except with Allah. Then Allah, glorified is He, since He is the Powerful by His essence, nothing incapacitates Him, the Self-Sufficient by Himself, nothing makes Him need, the Knowing by His essence, it is impossible for Him to be ignorant of anything, the Wise by His essence, error in action is impossible from Him, it is invalid that there be in His creation disparity such that testimony contradicts and planning conflicts; and it is necessary to say regarding everything that our intellects do not attain to the perception of wisdom—after it is established that He is its Originator and Creator—that we know that in it there is profound wisdom that we have not attained. Similarly, the intellect, since it is created and limited, does not exceed the limit set for it; with what exists in it of the ugliness of everything that appears good and the corruption of something that appears sound. So it is established that perhaps something prevents it from understanding the true nature of what it encounters of wisdom and folly. Moreover, how can one who is needy and poor, to whom his need is made beloved and his poverty is adorned in his eyes, and who considers ugly things beautiful by custom and familiarity, and likewise their opposites—how can such a person gauge the aspect of wisdom? Indeed, one of this description—because of those defects as well—is incapable of initiating an action not from something, since he moves with limbs and uses instruments. So how can one in that state in his action—after knowing that he acts with a power that is created and knowledge that is acquired—arrogate incapacity and ignorance over Him who is by His essence Powerful, knowing the incapacity for His like and ignorance? And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage contains the Dualist objection and the author's response. The Dualists argue that in the witnessed world, the admixture of darkness in light causes misperception of things, so observed contradictions do not reflect the true nature of the principles. The author turns the argument back: if the Dualist's own perception is clouded by defects, then his claim that the author's statements are not wisdom could also be due to that admixture. Then the author proceeds to establish the attributes of God: He is powerful by essence, self-sufficient, all-knowing, and perfectly wise. Therefore, there cannot be any disparity or contradiction in His creation. If our intellects fail to perceive the wisdom in some things, it is because they are limited. The author compares the intellect to the senses: each sense has a domain, and may be limited in grasping certain objects. Similarly, the intellect may be prevented from understanding the true wisdom behind things. Then he argues that a human, who is needy and poor, whose judgment is influenced by custom and desire, cannot judge the wisdom of the Lord. Moreover, humans act through limbs and instruments, so they cannot claim incapacity or ignorance over God, who is Powerful and Knowing by essence. This refutes the Dualist claim that observed evil indicates a separate principle.

Key Terms
التواتر (transmission of knowledge)
الحواس (senses)
الآفات (defects)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Imam Maturidi's response to the Dualist claim?
A) He denies that defects exist.
B) He argues that the Dualist's perception may also be affected by defects, so their objection is invalid.
C) He accepts the Dualist premise but says it only applies to them.
D) He changes the subject to God's attributes.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi refutes the Dualist by turning their own argument about defective perception back against them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs you hold that you have never examined because you assume your perception is free of defects? What would it mean if your own judgment about Allah’s attributes were clouded by a similar 'darkness'?
?
The Dualist accused Maturidi of folly, but the accusation was turned back. When you criticize others, do you consider that your own reasoning might suffer from the same flaw you point out? How will you test your own mind today?
?
This lesson shows that even a flawed premise can be used against an opponent. Are you more concerned with winning an argument or with arriving at the truth, even if it means your own position is proven faulty?
