God's Attributes and the Impossibility of Imperfection
Maturidi establishes God's attributes to argue for the perfection of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's essential attributes (power, self-sufficiency, knowledge, wisdom) preclude any real imperfection or contradiction in His creation.

Core Idea
Because God is Perfect by essence, His creation cannot contain true disparity.

After the initial rebuttal, Imam Maturidi moves to a positive argument: God's attributes necessitate that creation is free from real contradiction. Because God is Powerful, Self-Sufficient, Knowing, and Wise by His essence, there can be no actual deficiency in what He creates. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the attributes passage
Read the passage where the author lists God's attributes: Powerful by essence, Self-Sufficient, Knowing, Wise. Notice that each attribute is described as impossible to violate.
The phrase 'by His essence' indicates these are intrinsic, not acquired.
2
Connect attributes to creation
See how the author concludes: since God has these perfect attributes, there cannot be any disparity or contradiction in His creation.
The attributes guarantee that creation is coherent and wise.
3
Understand the implication for human knowledge
The author then adds that if our intellects fail to perceive the wisdom in something, we must affirm that it has profound wisdom beyond our reach.
This is a key point: limited human understanding does not imply lack of wisdom in God's work.

?

?

?
You have just read that God is Powerful, Self-Sufficient, Knowing, and Wise by His essence. Which of these attributes do you most need to internalize in your current circumstances? Commit to a specific action this week that reflects that attribute in your relationship with God.

Limits of Intellect and Refutation of Dualist Claim
Maturidi argues from human limitation and neediness to refute the Dualist position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the limitation of human intellect and senses, and the human condition of need and custom, prevent us from judging God's wisdom or claiming imperfection in creation.

Core Idea
A limited, needy human cannot judge the wisdom of the All-Powerful.

Imam Maturidi further argues that just as the senses are limited to their domains, the intellect is also created and limited. It may be prevented from understanding the true nature of wisdom and folly. Moreover, humans are needy and influenced by custom, making them incapable of gauging divine wisdom. Therefore, the Dualist's claim of imperfection is baseless.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy of senses and intellect
Read how the author compares the intellect to the senses: each has a domain and can be limited. The intellect, as created, is bound by limits and may be hindered.
The point is that the intellect cannot fully grasp divine wisdom.
2
Understand the human condition
Read the part describing humans as needy, poor, and influenced by custom and familiarity. This makes them unable to judge the wisdom of the Lord.
The author uses this to show that humans are not objective judges.
3
See the conclusion
The author concludes that a human who acts with created power and acquired knowledge cannot accuse God of incapacity or ignorance.
This refutes the Dualist's claim of a separate principle of evil.

According to Imam Maturidi, why can a human not judge the wisdom of God?
A) Because humans are created and limited in their abilities.

D) Because God hides His wisdom deliberately.

Key Takeaway
The limits of human intellect and the influence of need and custom prevent any person from claiming that there is real imperfection in God's creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has your own limited perspective or desire caused you to question God's wisdom in a difficulty you faced? How does knowing your intellect is created and biased change how you view that trial?
?
In what areas of your life do you still secretly believe that you know better than God what is good for you? What would it take for you to truly surrender that judgment?
?
How does the admission that you are 'needy and poor' before God sit with your pride? Will you let this lesson transform your sense of self-sufficiency into humble reliance?

God's Essential Attributes and Hidden Wisdom
Maturidi establishes divine attributes and the necessity of hidden wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's essential attributes prevent any disparity in creation and necessitate affirming hidden wisdom in His acts.

Core Idea
God's attributes guarantee wisdom; our failure to see it is our limitation.

Maturidi begins his refutation by grounding the discussion in God's essence. Since Allah is powerful, rich, knowing, and wise by His own essence, nothing in His creation can be without purpose or wisdom. Even when we cannot perceive the wisdom, we must affirm it exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essential attributes
Read the passage and list the four attributes mentioned: powerful by essence, rich by Himself, knowing by essence, wise by essence.
Each attribute negates a deficiency.
2
Understand the logical conclusion
Because of these attributes, there cannot be any disparity or contradiction in creation. The passage states that it is invalid that there be disparity.
Disparity would imply a deficiency in the creator.
3
Grasp the principle of hidden wisdom
Even if our intellects do not perceive the wisdom, we must know that there is profound wisdom that we have not attained.
This is a key principle: our inability to see wisdom does not negate its existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : فما يبعد أن يكون قولك كذا ليس بحكمة ولا رحمة ؟ بل هو سفه وقسوة، إنما كان منك لما شابك من آفات الظلمة فمنعك أن ترى كل شيء بجوهره وصورته. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم الله سبحانه إذ هو القادر بذاته لا يعجزه شيء، الغني بنفسه لا يُحوجه شيء، العليم بذاته لا يجوز أن يجهل شيئًا، الحكيم بذاته لا يجوز الخطأ منه في الفعل، بطل أن يكون في خلقه تفاوت تتناقض لديه الشهادة ويتضادّ فيه التدبير؛ ولزم القول بكل ما لا تبلغه عقولنا بدرك الحكمة ـ بعد أن ثبت أنه مُنشؤه ومحدثه - أن نعلم أن فيه حكمة بليغة لم تبلغها . . على ما لا يعلم أن كل حاسة من حواسنا جعلت لدرك ما تقع هي عليه، وإن كانت تقصر ربما عن الإحاطة به ، وتجيئ حاسة أخرى فتحيط به. فمثله العقل، إذ هو مخلوق محدود لا يجاوز الحد الذي جعل له؛ مع ما كان موجودًا فيه قبح كل شيء يظهر حسنه وفساد شيء يظهر صلاحه. فثبت أنه ربما يعتريه ما يمنعه عن فهم كنه ما يقع عليه من الحكمة والسفه. وبعد، فأنّى تقدير جهة الحكمة ممن هو محتاج فقير يُحبَّب إليه حاجته ويزيَّن في عينه فقره وحسن أشياء قبيحة، بالعادة والإلف، وكذلك أضدادها؟ فإن من هذا وصفه من الإحاطة بحكمة الربوبية، ولتلك الآفات أيضا، عاجز عن إنشاء فعل لا عن شيء؛ إذ هو يتقلب بالجوارح ويستعمل الآلات فأنى يكون لِمَن ذلك مَحلّه في فعله ـ بعد علمه أنه يعمل بقوة أحدثت وعلم أفيد - التحكم بالعجز والجهل على من هو بذاته قادر، عالم بالعجز عن مثله والجهل. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
It was said: What prevents that your statement such-and-such is without wisdom and mercy? Rather it is foolishness and harshness; it was only from you because you are mixed with the afflictions of darkness, preventing you from seeing everything in its essence and form. And there is no power except with Allah. Then Allah, exalted, since He is powerful by essence, nothing incapacitates Him; rich by Himself, nothing makes Him need; knowing by essence, it is not possible that He ignore anything; wise by essence, error in action is not possible from Him; it is invalid that there be in His creation disparity, the testimony would contradict and the governance would be inconsistent; and it is necessary to affirm concerning everything that our intellects do not attain to the perception of wisdom, after it is established that He is its originator and maker, that we know that in it there is profound wisdom that we have not attained. Just as it is not known that each of our senses is made for perceiving what it falls upon, although they may sometimes fall short of encompassing it, and another sense comes and encompasses it. Similar is the intellect, for it is created, limited, not exceeding the limit set for it; along with what exists in it: the ugliness of everything whose beauty appears and the corruption of something whose soundness appears. So it is established that perhaps something afflicts it that prevents it from understanding the essence of what it encounters of wisdom and foolishness. Moreover, how can one who is needy, poor, whose need is made beloved to him and his poverty is adorned in his eyes, and who considers ugly things beautiful, by habit and familiarity, and likewise their opposites, — how can such a one estimate the aspect of wisdom? For one described thus is unable to encompass the wisdom of lordship, and also due to those afflictions; he is incapable of originating an action not from something, since he moves by limbs and uses instruments. So how can one in such a state in his action, after knowing that he works by a power that is originated and knowledge that is given, control by impotence and ignorance over the One who is by essence powerful, knowing, and who is incapable of the like of him and ignorant? And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a dualist objection. It begins by quoting the opponent's claim that God's actions could be devoid of wisdom and mercy, implying foolishness. The refutation proceeds step by step: first, it establishes that God is powerful, rich, knowing, and wise by His own essence, making error and disparity in His creation impossible. Second, it argues that even if we cannot perceive the wisdom in something, we must affirm its existence, using the analogy of the senses—each sense perceives a specific aspect, and what one misses another may grasp. The intellect, similarly, is created and limited, and may be hindered from understanding the true nature of wisdom. Third, it points out that the dualist, being needy and subject to base inclinations, cannot judge divine wisdom, and is himself incapable of independent action, relying on originated power and knowledge. Thus, the dualist's charge of foolishness is baseless. The passage concludes with 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms
القادر بذاته
الغني بنفسه
العليم بذاته
الحكيم بذاته
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If I cannot see the wisdom in a divine action, then the action is foolish or unjust.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that God's essential attributes guarantee wisdom; our intellectual limitation prevents full perception.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from God's essential attributes?
A) Some of God's acts may lack wisdom.
B) There cannot be any disparity in creation, and hidden wisdom must be affirmed.
C) The intellect can fully grasp divine wisdom.
D) The dualist's objection is partially correct.

Key Takeaway
God's essence guarantees perfect wisdom in all His acts, even if we cannot grasp it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or a situation that seems pointless or unjust, do you genuinely believe there is hidden wisdom in it — or does your heart default to doubt and complaint? What would change if you woke up tomorrow fully convinced that every single thing in your life is dense with wisdom you cannot yet see?
?
You say God is wise. But right now, is there an area of your life (a relationship, a loss, an unanswered prayer) where you are implicitly acting as though God made a mistake? How would your daily actions and conversations change if you truly accepted that 'error is not possible from Him'?
?
If you truly knew that the lack of perceived wisdom is always a limit in you, not in God, what would you be emboldened to do or endure that you are currently avoiding? Write down one concrete situation this week where you will consciously affirm, 'There is profound wisdom here that I have not attained' — and then act accordingly.

The Limits of Intellect and the Dualist's Incapacity
Maturidi uses analogy of senses and critique of dualist's limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy of the senses to illustrate intellectual limitation and the dualist's own inability to judge divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Our intellect, like our senses, is limited; the dualist's own dependence disproves his claim.

Maturidi continues his refutation by comparing the intellect to the senses. Just as one sense may miss what another perceives, the intellect may fail to grasp divine wisdom. Moreover, the dualist himself is needy and dependent, incapable of judging the actions of the All-Powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy of the senses
The passage compares the intellect to the senses: each sense has a specific scope and may fall short, but another sense may encompass what was missed.
The analogy shows that limitation does not mean absence of reality.
2
Appreciate the limitations of the intellect
The intellect is created and limited; it may be afflicted by factors that prevent it from understanding the true nature of wisdom and foolishness.
This explains why we may not always perceive divine wisdom.
3
See the critique of the dualist
The dualist is needy, influenced by habit and desire, and cannot even originate an action independently. How can such a one judge the wisdom of the Lord?
The dualist's own condition undermines his objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?

Dualist and Hyle/Clay Views Refuted
Maturidi presents Dualist and hyle/clay views on divine change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of views that attribute change to God through essential transformation or material mixture.

Core Idea
Change in God's essence or from material elements contradicts His transcendence.

This passage begins by examining two early heretical groups: the Dualists, who believed God underwent separation and mixture to become Creator and Merciful, and the proponents of hyle and clay, who claimed God changed from a single state due to temporal events. These views fundamentally misunderstand divine immutability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Read the Arabic passage and locate descriptions of the Dualists and the proponents of hyle and clay. Notice the specific changes they attribute to God.
Dualists: separation by essence then mixture. Hyle/clay: God was one, then changed due to events.
2
Understand the implications
Reflect on why these views are problematic: they imply God was not always Creator or Merciful, which contradicts His necessary attributes.
3
Connect to the broader critique
Note that Maturidi presents these views without immediate refutation; the refutation comes later in the passage. This sets up the comparison with Mu'tazilites.
The next lesson will compare these views to Mu'tazilite ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما كان الله عندهم غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم ثم صار كذلك بحدث الأشياء على ما قالت الثنوية من التباين بالذات ثم الامتزاج وعلى ما قال أصحاب الهيولى والطينة إنه كان واحداً على جهة ثم صار على تلك الحال بما حدث من الحوادث لكن قول أولئك ألزم بحق العقل من قول المعتزلة إذ هم ألزموا التغير بحوادث في الأصل وهؤلاء بحوادث في غير [ ] ولا أحد يتغير في الشاهد عما عليه بما لا يحل به ولا قوة إلا بالله وعلى الحسين والبرغوث وغيرهما في هذا القول الثاني وهؤلاء أيضا ألزموا التغير بالمكان حيث قالوا كان ولا مكان ثم هو موصوف بكل مكان فألزموا الوصف بالمحدث فيبطل معنى التوحيد والمشبهة يقولون له مثال في الخلق في الجسمية والحد والنهاية والحركات والسكون يحققون له ما به عرف حدث العالم ويجعلونه مثالاً له جل الله عن ذلك
Despite God being according to them not Creator nor Most Merciful nor Most Compassionate, then He became such by the occurrence of things, according to what the Dualists said of separation by essence then mixture, and according to what the proponents of hyle and clay said that He was one in a certain manner then became in that state due to what occurred of events. But the statement of those is more binding by the right of reason than the statement of the Mu'tazilites, for they (the Dualists) necessitated change by events in the origin, and these (the Mu'tazilites) by events in non-[ ]. And no one in the witnessed world changes from his state due to what does not inhere in him. And there is no power except with Allah. And al-Husayn and al-Birghuth and others are upon this second statement. And these also necessitated change by place, where they said: He was and no place, then He is described by every place, so they necessitated description by the originated, and thus the meaning of tawhid is invalidated. And the Anthropomorphists say that He has a likeness in creation in corporeality, boundary, end, movements, and stillness; they verify for Him that by which the temporality of the world is known, and they make Him a likeness of it. Exalted is Allah above that.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a critique of several groups that attribute change to God, thereby undermining tawhid. Maturidi begins by describing the Dualists who assert God was not creator or merciful, then became so through a process of separation and mixture. The proponents of hyle and clay say God was one and then changed due to events. He notes that these views are actually more rational than the Mu'tazilites, who claim change occurs through events that do not inhere in God—a contradiction since no observed being changes by what is not in it. He then mentions al-Husayn and al-Birghuth who held a similar view, and the Mu'tazilites who attribute change to place by saying God was without place yet is now described by every place, thus describing Him with originated attributes. Finally, the Anthropomorphists give God a likeness in body, boundary, and movement, making Him like creation. All these lead to the invalidation of tawhid. Exalted is Allah above that.

Key Terms
الثنوية (Dualists)
الهيولى والطينة (hyle and clay)
التباين (separation)
الامتزاج (mixture)
التغير (change)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's becoming Creator after not being Creator is a change in His essence itself.
Correction: Maturidi argues that such a view implies a temporal change in God, which is impossible because divine attributes are eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the Dualists, how did God become Creator and Merciful?
A) Through separation by essence then mixture
B) Through evolution over time
C) By creating the world
D) By taking on human form

Key Takeaway
Attributing change of state to God, whether through essential separation or material process, undermines His eternal perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted God's mercy because of a trial, acting as if His mercy is not eternal but conditional on your circumstances? How does knowing that God is always Al-Rahman, even when you don't feel it, change your reliance on Him?
?
If someone told you that God became your Creator only at the moment of your birth, would you recoil? Yet have you ever treated His attribute of Creator as if it started with you, forgetting He was Creator before you existed? How can you realign your worship to acknowledge His eternal creativity?
?
When you sin, do you fear that you have changed God's disposition toward you from merciful to angry? This lesson teaches that God's attributes never change. What comfort does that give you in repentance, and what accountability does it place on you not to take His mercy for granted?

Mu'tazilite Views on Divine Change
Maturidi critiques Mu'tazilite views on divine change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of Mu'tazilite views that attribute change to God through non-inhering events or spatial relation.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite change through non-inhering events or place is even less rational than Dualist views.

Maturidi now compares the Dualist views with those of the Mu'tazilites, arguing that the latter are even more irrational. The Mu'tazilites claim that God changes due to events that do not inhere in Him or by being described by every place, both of which are impossible for any being in the witnessed world.

Guided Path
1
Understand the comparison
Maturidi says the Dualist view is 'more binding by reason' than the Mu'tazilite view. Read why: Mu'tazilites attribute change to events not inhering in God.
The phrase 'ألزم بحق العقل' indicates a stronger rational argument.
2
Analyze the first Mu'tazilite sub-view
Mu'tazilites claim change by events in non-inhering. Maturidi objects: no being changes by what does not inhere in it.
He gives an example from the witnessed world.
3
Analyze the second Mu'tazilite sub-view
Mu'tazilites also claim God was without place then described by every place. This implies originated attributes and invalidates tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما كان الله عندهم غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم ثم صار كذلك بحدث الأشياء على ما قالت الثنوية من التباين بالذات ثم الامتزاج وعلى ما قال أصحاب الهيولى والطينة إنه كان واحداً على جهة ثم صار على تلك الحال بما حدث من الحوادث لكن قول أولئك ألزم بحق العقل من قول المعتزلة إذ هم ألزموا التغير بحوادث في الأصل وهؤلاء بحوادث في غير [ ] ولا أحد يتغير في الشاهد عما عليه بما لا يحل به ولا قوة إلا بالله وعلى الحسين والبرغوث وغيرهما في هذا القول الثاني وهؤلاء أيضا ألزموا التغير بالمكان حيث قالوا كان ولا مكان ثم هو موصوف بكل مكان فألزموا الوصف بالمحدث فيبطل معنى التوحيد والمشبهة يقولون له مثال في الخلق في الجسمية والحد والنهاية والحركات والسكون يحققون له ما به عرف حدث العالم ويجعلونه مثالاً له جل الله عن ذلك
Despite God being according to them not Creator nor Most Merciful nor Most Compassionate, then He became such by the occurrence of things, according to what the Dualists said of separation by essence then mixture, and according to what the proponents of hyle and clay said that He was one in a certain manner then became in that state due to what occurred of events. But the statement of those is more binding by the right of reason than the statement of the Mu'tazilites, for they (the Dualists) necessitated change by events in the origin, and these (the Mu'tazilites) by events in non-[ ]. And no one in the witnessed world changes from his state due to what does not inhere in him. And there is no power except with Allah. And al-Husayn and al-Birghuth and others are upon this second statement. And these also necessitated change by place, where they said: He was and no place, then He is described by every place, so they necessitated description by the originated, and thus the meaning of tawhid is invalidated. And the Anthropomorphists say that He has a likeness in creation in corporeality, boundary, end, movements, and stillness; they verify for Him that by which the temporality of the world is known, and they make Him a likeness of it. Exalted is Allah above that.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a critique of several groups that attribute change to God, thereby undermining tawhid. Maturidi begins by describing the Dualists who assert God was not creator or merciful, then became so through a process of separation and mixture. The proponents of hyle and clay say God was one and then changed due to events. He notes that these views are actually more rational than the Mu'tazilites, who claim change occurs through events that do not inhere in God—a contradiction since no observed being changes by what is not in it. He then mentions al-Husayn and al-Birghuth who held a similar view, and the Mu'tazilites who attribute change to place by saying God was without place yet is now described by every place, thus describing Him with originated attributes. Finally, the Anthropomorphists give God a likeness in body, boundary, and movement, making Him like creation. All these lead to the invalidation of tawhid. Exalted is Allah above that.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (Mu'tazilites)
الحوادث (events)
غير حال (non-inhering)
المكان (place)
التوحيد (tawhid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Mu'tazilites thought that God's relation to events or places could change without affecting His essence.
Correction: Maturidi argues that any change (even relational) that implies originated attributes is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the Mu'tazilite view less rational than the Dualist view?
A) Mu'tazilites attribute change to events that do not inhere in God
B) Mu'tazilites deny God's mercy
C) Dualists are more consistent
D) Mu'tazilites accept anthropomorphism

Key Takeaway
Attributing change to God through non-inhering events or spatial description is self-contradictory and invalidates tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah as being 'everywhere', do you find yourself visualizing Him in a place, even if you know He is not? How does this lesson expose that as a dangerous step towards invalidating tawhid? Will you actively reject any mental image of Allah in space?
?
Have you ever justified a flawed understanding of Allah's attributes because it felt comforting or made God more relatable? What specific attribute or description do you need to repent of, in light of Maturidi's warning that even non-inhering events or spatial descriptions are impermissible?
?
The Dualists at least had a more rational view, yet they still erred. How does this humility—that even the 'better' wrong view is still wrong—challenge your own intellectual pride when studying theology? Will you submit to the revealed truth even when it seems less subtle?

Anthropomorphist Views Refuted
Maturidi critiques Anthropomorphist attribution of bodily attributes to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of Anthropomorphists who liken God to creation in body, boundary, and movement.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphists make God a likeness of creation, contradicting His uniqueness.

The final group critiqued are the Anthropomorphists (المشبهة), who claim God has a likeness in creation in terms of corporeality, boundary, end, movements, and stillness. They even use the temporality of the world as an argument to establish a likeness for God. Maturidi declares God exalted above such comparisons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Anthropomorphist claims
Read the Arabic: they say God has a 'likeness in creation' in five aspects: corporeality, boundary, end, movements, stillness.
2
Understand their argument
They claim to 'verify for Him that by which the temporality of the world is known' – meaning they use the same arguments for the world's createdness to apply to God.
This is a faulty analogy.
3
Grasp Maturidi's rejection
Maturidi ends with 'Exalted is Allah above that' (جل الله عن ذلك), showing the view is a clear violation of tawhid.
No explanation is given here; the exclamation suffices as refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما كان الله عندهم غير خالق ولا رحمن ولا رحيم ثم صار كذلك بحدث الأشياء على ما قالت الثنوية من التباين بالذات ثم الامتزاج وعلى ما قال أصحاب الهيولى والطينة إنه كان واحداً على جهة ثم صار على تلك الحال بما حدث من الحوادث لكن قول أولئك ألزم بحق العقل من قول المعتزلة إذ هم ألزموا التغير بحوادث في الأصل وهؤلاء بحوادث في غير [ ] ولا أحد يتغير في الشاهد عما عليه بما لا يحل به ولا قوة إلا بالله وعلى الحسين والبرغوث وغيرهما في هذا القول الثاني وهؤلاء أيضا ألزموا التغير بالمكان حيث قالوا كان ولا مكان ثم هو موصوف بكل مكان فألزموا الوصف بالمحدث فيبطل معنى التوحيد والمشبهة يقولون له مثال في الخلق في الجسمية والحد والنهاية والحركات والسكون يحققون له ما به عرف حدث العالم ويجعلونه مثالاً له جل الله عن ذلك
Despite God being according to them not Creator nor Most Merciful nor Most Compassionate, then He became such by the occurrence of things, according to what the Dualists said of separation by essence then mixture, and according to what the proponents of hyle and clay said that He was one in a certain manner then became in that state due to what occurred of events. But the statement of those is more binding by the right of reason than the statement of the Mu'tazilites, for they (the Dualists) necessitated change by events in the origin, and these (the Mu'tazilites) by events in non-[ ]. And no one in the witnessed world changes from his state due to what does not inhere in him. And there is no power except with Allah. And al-Husayn and al-Birghuth and others are upon this second statement. And these also necessitated change by place, where they said: He was and no place, then He is described by every place, so they necessitated description by the originated, and thus the meaning of tawhid is invalidated. And the Anthropomorphists say that He has a likeness in creation in corporeality, boundary, end, movements, and stillness; they verify for Him that by which the temporality of the world is known, and they make Him a likeness of it. Exalted is Allah above that.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a critique of several groups that attribute change to God, thereby undermining tawhid. Maturidi begins by describing the Dualists who assert God was not creator or merciful, then became so through a process of separation and mixture. The proponents of hyle and clay say God was one and then changed due to events. He notes that these views are actually more rational than the Mu'tazilites, who claim change occurs through events that do not inhere in God—a contradiction since no observed being changes by what is not in it. He then mentions al-Husayn and al-Birghuth who held a similar view, and the Mu'tazilites who attribute change to place by saying God was without place yet is now described by every place, thus describing Him with originated attributes. Finally, the Anthropomorphists give God a likeness in body, boundary, and movement, making Him like creation. All these lead to the invalidation of tawhid. Exalted is Allah above that.

Key Terms
المشبهة (Anthropomorphists)
المثال (likeness)
الجسمية (corporeality)
الحد (boundary)
النهاية (end)
الحركات (movements)
السكون (stillness)
حدث العالم (temporality of the world)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some anthropomorphists believe that since the world is known to be created through change and limitation, God must also have change and limitation.
Correction: Maturidi rejects this by asserting God's absolute transcendence; arguments for the world's temporality do not apply to God.

Checkpoint
Which attributes do the Anthropomorphists incorrectly affirm for God according to the passage?
A) Corporeality, boundary, end, movements, stillness
B) Knowledge, power, will, life, speech
C) Mercy, compassion, forgiveness
D) Eternity, omnipotence, omniscience

Key Takeaway
Attributing bodily or spatial attributes to God makes Him a likeness of creation, which contradicts His absolute uniqueness and transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever imagined God in a way that subtly mirrors something you see in creation—like a king on a throne, or a father figure? How does this passage demand you uproot that image?
?
The anthropomorphists thought they were honoring God by giving Him bodily attributes. Is there any way you have unknowingly lowered your concept of God by comparing Him to someone or something you admire?
?
If you truly believe God is exalted above all likeness, how will you guard your tongue and heart this week from describing Him in terms borrowed from creation? Commit to one concrete change in how you speak of God.

Refuting the Obligation of Benefit in Creation
Maturidi refutes the obligation of benefit in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi rejects the idea that God is obliged to create for the most beneficial purpose and how he grounds divine action in wisdom rather than external constraints.

Core Idea
God's actions are not bound by benefit; His wisdom transcends obligation.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi confronts a common theological claim: that God must always create what is most beneficial for creatures. He argues that such a view wrongly imposes an obligation on God and opens His actions to blame. Instead, he insists that God's wisdom is intrinsic to His actions, and the question 'why did He create?' is itself invalid. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage (up to 'wallahu a‘lam') once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from criticism of the opposing view to positive assertion of divine wisdom.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the target claim
Find where Maturidi states the claim he is refuting. What does the opponent hold about God's creation?
Look for phrases like 'لا يزعم أنه لا يفعل إلا الأصلح'.
3
Understand the refutation's structure
Note how Maturidi first shows the absurd consequence of the opposing view (first creation must be tested), then asserts the impossibility of questioning the totality of creation, and finally distinguishes between benefit for others and the act itself.
He uses logical impossibility ('محال') to dismiss the opponent's reasoning.
4
Grasp the positive doctrine
Maturidi states that God is wise and His actions never depart from wisdom. This is the positive alternative: divine wisdom, not benefit for creatures, is the foundation.
Wisdom (حكمة) is a divine attribute, not a standard imposed from outside.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الذي يزعم يوجب أنه لا يجوز أن يُجعل أول الخلق غير الممتحن حتى يكون له في الذي ذكرنا، وإذا جاز غيره بلا مصلحة لذلك الوقت دون غيره لا معنى لما قال. وقد بينا نحن القول بالخلق، وإحالة السؤال عن لِمَ خَلَق؟ وليس لنا أن نزعم أنه لا يفعل إلا الأصلح فيلزمه حق الفعل حتى يلحقه وصف ذم إن أخر أو قدم؛ بل الله تعالى إذ هو حكيم لا يخرج فعله عن الحكمة. وأما اعتبار الأصلح لغيره إنما تقدير الحق عليه لا تقدير الفعل بذاته؛ ومحال كون الحق لغيره عليه ولا غير، أو السؤال عن جملة الخلق. فالقول في أنه يخلق لنفع لهم أو صلاح لهم لا معنى له؛ إذ ليس عليهم فيما لا يخلق لهم ضرر ولا فساد فيكون الخلق لما ذكر. والله أعلم. ثم في الجملة لا يخلو خلق من أن يكون للمتحن به نفع وعبرة من طريق الاستدلال به والاعتبار، سوى المنافع الأخر مما من الله عليهم بها. وبالله التوفيق. وأصل صلاح العبد في الدين إنما هو بفعله، وكذلك فساده. والله تعالى بالأسباب التي بها ينال فعل الصلاح عليه أعظم المنن وأجزل النعم. ومن فسد فهو لإعراض عن الله وإيثاره شهوته على طاعته خلّى الله بينه وبين ما اختار [؟] لنفسه، إذ آثر هواه على أمره وشهوته على طاعته والفعل الذي بيّن له أنه فعل العداوة على ما هو الولاية. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
This which he claims necessitates that it is not permissible to make the first creation other than the tested (being) so that he has in that which we mentioned; and if other than it is permissible without benefit for that time rather than another, what he said has no meaning. We have already stated the statement concerning creation and the impossibility of the question 'why did He create?' It is not for us to claim that He does not do except the most beneficial, so that the right of the act would be incumbent upon Him until a description of blame attaches to Him if He delayed or advanced. Rather, Allah the Most High, since He is Wise, does not take His action out of wisdom. As for considering the most beneficial for other than Him, it is only an estimation of the right upon Him, not an estimation of the act in itself. It is impossible for the right to be upon Him for other than Him, or the question about the totality of creation. So the statement that He creates for their benefit or their welfare has no meaning, since there is no harm or corruption for them in what He does not create for them, such that creation would be for what was mentioned. And Allah knows best. Then, in general, no creation is free from having benefit and a lesson for the one tested by it by way of inference and consideration, besides the other benefits with which Allah has favored them. And with Allah is success. The foundation of the slave's rectitude in religion is only through his own action, and likewise his corruption. Allah the Most High, through the means by which the action of rectitude is attained, has the greatest favors and most abundant blessings upon him. Whoever becomes corrupt, it is because of turning away from Allah and preferring his desire over His obedience. Allah abandons him between himself and what he chose [?] for himself, since he preferred his whim over His command and his desire over His obedience and the action that was made clear to him that it is an act of enmity instead of friendship. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that God must create only for the most beneficial purpose and that His actions are subject to being questioned in terms of benefit. The author begins by stating that the claim under discussion necessitates that the first creation cannot be other than a tested being, but if it is permissible to create something without benefit for a specific time, then the claim is meaningless. He then reaffirms the correct position: the question 'why did He create?' is invalid, and it is impermissible to assert that God only does what is most beneficial, as that would impose an obligation on Him and open the door to blame. Rather, God is wise and His actions never depart from wisdom. The author distinguishes between considering the most beneficial for others and the act itself: the former would impose a right on God, which is impossible. Therefore, claiming that God creates for the benefit or welfare of creatures is meaningless, because there is no harm to them in what He does not create. He concludes this section with 'Allah knows best.' Then he adds a general statement: all creation includes benefit and lessons for the one tested, besides other favors from God. He then shifts to the foundation of faith and corruption: a slave's rectitude or corruption is through his own action, and God grants means for rectitude as a great favor. Corruption comes from turning away from God and preferring desire over obedience, leading God to abandon the person to his choice. The passage ends with a declaration of reliance on God.

Key Terms
الأصلح
الحكمة
المصلحة
الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God is obliged to create only what is most beneficial for creatures.
Correction: Imam Maturidi states that such a view imposes a right on God and opens Him to blame; rather, God acts from wisdom, not from obligation to benefit.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, why is the claim that God creates only for the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) invalid?
A) Because it would impose a right on God and expose Him to blame if He delayed or advanced.
B) Because benefit is impossible for creatures.
C) Because the most beneficial is unknowable.
D) Because God does not act for any purpose.

Key Takeaway
God's actions are grounded in His wisdom, not in an obligation to provide benefit to creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the events of your life—both pleasant and painful—do you secretly believe that God owes you a certain outcome? Where in this lesson do you find a direct challenge to that assumption?
?
If God is not obligated to create for your benefit, how does that reshape your trust in His wisdom when you face suffering? What specific phrase from this passage can you hold onto when doubt creeps in?
?
Think of a time you questioned God's wisdom because something seemed unfair or not 'for your good.' According to this passage, how does that question relate to the impossibility of asking 'why did He create?' How would you now reframe that question in your heart?

Human Responsibility and Divine Grace
Maturidi on benefit of creation and human responsibility for rectitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's teaching that rectitude and corruption in religion stem from human choice, while God provides means and blessings for goodness.

Core Idea
Your rectitude or corruption is your own doing; God grants the means but you choose.

After refuting the idea that God must create for benefit, Maturidi turns to the purpose of creation: all creation contains benefit and lessons for the tested. He then explains the foundation of a person's righteousness or corruption—it is through their own actions. God provides the means for rectitude as a great favor, but when a person turns away, God leaves them to their choice. This lesson explores that balance of divine grace and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the second passage
Read from 'ثم في الجملة' to the end. Notice the shift from refutation to positive explanation. The passage first speaks of general benefit in creation, then focuses on human responsibility.
2
Identify the two main claims
First, Maturidi states that all creation includes benefit and lessons for the one tested. Second, he asserts that a slave's rectitude or corruption is through his own action. Find these points in the text.
Look for 'لا يخلو خلق من أن يكون للمتحن به نفع' and 'أصل صلاح العبد في الدين إنما هو بفعله'.
3
Understand the role of divine grace
Maturidi emphasizes that God grants the means for rectitude as a great favor. But when a person prefers desire over obedience, God abandons him to his choice. This shows human responsibility without negating God's grace.
The phrase 'أعظم المنن وأجزل النعم' indicates God's abundant favor.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله', a declaration of reliance on God. How does this fit with the emphasis on human action? It affirms that ultimately, strength is from God, not our independent power.
This balances the discussion: human choice is real but dependent on divine power.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the foundation of a slave's rectitude in religion?
A) God's direct creation of it without human involvement.
B) The slave's own action (bi-fi‘lihi).
C) The most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) circumstances.
D) The absence of desire.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to follow God, but where in your daily choices—not just your words—do you see your own preference for your desires over His obedience? What will you do about that today?
?
If your rectitude or corruption is truly your own doing, then you cannot blame anyone else for your spiritual state. Who are you most tempted to blame, and how will you take responsibility starting now?
?
God grants the means for rectitude, yet you often feel weak. In what area of your life have you been ignoring those means—knowledge, prayer, good company—and instead relying on your own effort? What step will you take to use those means?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Objection about the First Creation and the Argument of Time
Maturidi responds to an objection about the first creation using the argument from time and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes an objection concerning the timing of the first creation by appealing to the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
No time before the first creation, so the question 'why not earlier?' is meaningless.

The objection suggests that if the first creation had no benefit for anyone, then God could have created something else before it. Maturidi shows that this question relies on the assumption of a specific time, but there is no time prior to the first creation. He then demonstrates that the question leads to an infinite regress, which is impossible. This lesson explores his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the objector says 'why did He not create before it?' and the immediate reply about no time.
2
Understand the time argument
The response states there is no time to point to because the creation is first. Consider why that invalidates the question.
3
Analyze the infinite regress argument
If creation before were possible, it would go on infinitely. That makes the question impossible to answer, except if creation were eternal, which is contradictory.
Eternity would require temporal origination in an eternal entity, which is impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The objection is invalid because it rests on an incorrect assumption about time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعورض بأول خلق خلقه لنفسه وليس ثمة مصلحة. فزعم أنه ليس ثمة وقت ليقال فيه: لم لا خلق قبله؟ وإنما ذلك متى يكون [ف] هو أول، وهو أصلح في التدبير وأولى بالحكمة. وما هو كذلك فيخرج السؤال على أنه لم لا خلق [ما هو] دونه في الحكمة وحسن التدبير؟ قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فما ذكر من الوقت فهو [ك] ما يذكر. على أن السؤال في مثله ساقط، لأنه لا يشار إلى وقت وإلا لو كان الخلق قبل ذلك إلى ما لا يحتمل اللسان من عدد الأوقات ممكن، وفي ذلك بطلان السؤال؛ إلا عن قدمه، وذلك تناقض، لإحالة وقوع التكوين على الكائن في القدم ولا قوة إلا بالله. وما ذكر من الحكمة فذلك حق. وما ذكر من الأصلح لا أدرى ما أراد به. وما قال من دونه أو مثله، فالقول به لا معنى له. والله تعالى أن يفعل الفعل الذي لا يخرج عن الحكمة؛ إذ الخروج عنه يحقق السفه، وذلك يسقط الربوبية. ثم في الحكمة طريقان أحدهما العدل، والثاني الفضل وليس لما يقدر الله من الإفضال نهاية فيتكلّم في الشيء بأفضل ما يبلغه قوته من الفعل مع ما ليس عليه الإفضال، يختص فعله به من شاء. وغير جائز خروج من الحكمة لما ذكرت وكذلك معنى العدل: إنه وضع كل شيء موضعه. لكن له درجات يوصف فعل بعضها إحسانًا وإفضالاً، وفعل بعضها عدلاً وحكمة؛ إذ هما اسمان عامان لكل ما للفاعل فعله، والأول خاص من حيث كان له تركه فيفعله منعما محسنا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وسؤال القدرة على خلق شيء قبل هذا الخلق يخرج على ما بيناه في الوقت. والله على كل شيء.
He was objected to with the first creation that He created for Himself and there is no benefit there. So he claimed that there is no time to say: why did He not create before it? Rather that when it is the first, it is more fitting in management and more appropriate to wisdom. And if it is like that, the question becomes: why did He not create what is inferior to it in wisdom and good management? The shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: What he mentioned about time is like what he mentioned. However, the question in such a case is invalid, because no time is indicated; otherwise, if creation before that were possible to an infinite number of times, that would nullify the question, except concerning its eternity, and that is a contradiction, due to the impossibility of temporal origination occurring on an eternal entity; there is no power except with Allah. And what he mentioned about wisdom is true. And what he mentioned about the most beneficial, I do not know what he meant by it. And what he said about what is inferior or similar, there is no meaning to it. And Allah the Most High does the act that does not depart from wisdom; because departing from it constitutes foolishness, and that negates lordship. Then, wisdom has two ways: one is justice, and the second is grace. There is no end to what Allah can bestow, so one cannot speak about a thing with the best that his power can achieve from the act, while there is no obligation upon Him to bestow; He singles out with His act whom He wills. And it is not permissible to depart from wisdom for what I mentioned. Likewise, the meaning of justice is that it places everything in its proper place. But it has degrees: some of its acts are described as beneficence and grace, and some of its acts are justice and wisdom, since they are two general names for everything that the agent does, and the first is specific in that He could have refrained from it, but He does it as a favor and beneficence. There is no power except with Allah. And the question about the ability to create something before this creation falls under what we explained regarding time. And Allah has power over everything.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the first creation, followed by an explanation of the two aspects of divine wisdom. The objection states: if the first creation was for God Himself without any benefit, then why did He not create something before it? The response begins by noting that there is no 'time' to refer to because the first creation has no prior time. If one asks why not create what is less perfect, the question is invalid because it would imply an infinite regress of possible creations, which is impossible. The shaykh then affirms that the mention of wisdom is correct, but the concept of 'most beneficial' is unclear. God's actions never leave wisdom, as that would be foolishness and negate Lordship. Then the text divides wisdom into two ways: justice (al-‘adl) and grace (al-fadl). Grace has no limit; God may bestow or not without obligation. Justice is placing everything in its proper place, but there are degrees: some acts are beneficence and grace, others are justice and wisdom. Both are general terms, but the first (beneficence) is specific in that He could have omitted it, yet He does it as a favor. The passage concludes that the question about creating something before this creation is answered by the previous discussion on time, and God has power over everything.

Key Terms
الوقت
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first creation can be questioned about its timing.
Correction: The question is invalid because there is no time before the first creation and it would lead to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Maturidi refute the objection about the timing of the first creation?
A) By saying that the first creation is the most beneficial.
B) By stating that there is no specific time to refer to, and the question would lead to an infinite regress which is impossible.
C) By saying that the question is irrelevant.
D) By arguing that creation cannot be questioned.

Key Takeaway
The objection about the timing of the first creation is refuted by the impossibility of infinite regress and the absence of a prior time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you ever find yourself questioning God's timing—wishing He had acted earlier or differently? How does the truth that there is no 'earlier' for God's first act challenge your assumptions about how He should run your life?
?
The objector's question assumes a time before the first creation. What assumptions about God do you hold that might be based on your limited human perspective? Are you ready to let go of those assumptions and trust that His wisdom is beyond time?
?
The lesson declares that infinite regress is impossible. Do you trust that God's plan for your life has a definitive beginning, without being lost in an endless chain of causes? How can this certainty bring rest to your heart when you face uncertainty?

The Two Aspects of Divine Wisdom: Justice and Grace
Maturidi explains the two aspects of divine wisdom: justice and grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's division of divine wisdom into justice and grace, and why God is not obligated to do the 'most beneficial'.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom includes both justice and grace, but grace has no limit and is not obligatory.

Having established that God's actions never depart from wisdom, Maturidi explains that wisdom has two aspects: justice and grace. Justice means placing everything in its proper place, while grace is God's bestowal beyond obligation. This lesson clarifies that God is not required to do the 'most beneficial' for His creatures, as grace is freely given. It also distinguishes between acts of beneficence and acts of justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on wisdom
Read the Arabic passage about wisdom, its two ways, and the clarification about 'most beneficial'.
2
Distinguish justice and grace
Identify the definitions: justice is placing things in their proper place; grace is bestowal without obligation and with no limit.
3
Understand the 'most beneficial' issue
Notice that Maturidi rejects the idea that God must always do what is 'most beneficial' for creation. Grace is not obligatory.
4
Examine degrees of actions
Some acts are described as beneficence (if God could have omitted them) and others as justice/wisdom (if they are due). Both are general terms.
5
Link back to the earlier topic
The final sentence connects the question about creating something before this creation to the earlier discussion on time, showing the overall argument's coherence.

Refuting the Claim of Eternal Created Things
Maturidi answers the objection about God's eternal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that God must eternally create things, and the difference between the thing's eternity and God's eternal act of creation.

Core Idea
God is eternally a creator, but the world is not eternal.

This passage addresses a common objection: if God is all-powerful, why didn't He create everything from eternity? Maturidi unpacks the ambiguity in the statement 'God everlastingly creates things' and shows that one interpretation leads to contradiction, while the other affirms both God's eternal attribute and the temporal nature of creation. Let's follow his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the initial question: why was God not everlastingly creating things? Note the challenge to divine power or will.
The objector assumes that if God can create, He must have always been doing so, implying an eternal universe.
2
Distinguish the two meanings
Identify the two interpretations of 'everlastingly creating things': (1) that the things themselves are eternal, or (2) that the act of creation is eternal.
This distinction is key to the answer.
3
Reject the first meaning
Understand why the first meaning (things eternal) is impossible: it would make the created co-eternal with God, contradicting their temporal origination.
Eternal things cannot be 'originated'; eternity and temporal origination are mutually exclusive.
4
Accept the second meaning
The second meaning (God eternally a creator, each thing created at its time) is true because God is essentially a creator, not dependent on anything else.
This preserves both God's eternal attribute and the temporality of creation.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this answer upholds God's power and the world's contingency without falling into infinite regress or making the world eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عورض بما لم لا كان لم يزل يحدث الأشياء؟ فأجاب بالذي تقدم ذكره من فساد كون شيء قبل شيء إلى ما لا نهاية له. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وجواب هذا عندنا أن يقال: لو أردت بقولك «لم يزل يحدث الأشياء» لتكون هي لم يزل، فذلك محال؛ لما فيه إثبات قدمها، وفي قدمها فساد إحداثها. وإن أردت به الإحداث ليكون كل شيء من ذلك لوقت كونه فذلك حق؛ إذ هو بذاته خالق، لا بغيره. قدير.
Then he was objected to with: why was He not everlastingly creating things? So he answered with what was previously mentioned about the corruption of one thing existing before another infinitely. The shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The answer to this with us is that it should be said: if you mean by your statement 'He was everlastingly creating things' that they are everlastingly existent, that is impossible, because it entails establishing their eternity, and in their eternity is the corruption of their temporal origination. And if you mean by it the act of creation such that every thing is for the time of its existence, then that is true, because He is by His essence a creator, not by another. He is All-Powerful.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding God's relation to creation over time. The objector asks: 'Why did God not eternally create things?' The initial response refers to the earlier discussion about the impossibility of an infinite regress of one thing before another. The shaykh then gives a more precise answer: the statement 'He was everlastingly creating things' can have two meanings. First, if it means that the created things themselves are eternal, this is impossible because eternal things cannot be temporally originated; eternity precludes origination. Second, if it means that God is eternally a creator in the sense that each act of creation occurs at the time of the thing's existence, then this is true, because God is essentially a creator, not through any other cause. The passage concludes by affirming God's power.

Key Terms

الإحداث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's eternal attribute of creating means the created world is eternal.
Correction: God's attribute of creating is eternal, but the world is temporally originated; each creature comes into existence at its designated time.

Checkpoint
What is the correct interpretation of 'God was everlastingly creating things' according to Imam Maturidi?

B) God is eternally a creator, but each thing is created at its proper time.
C) Creation has no beginning.
D) The question is invalid.

Key Takeaway
God eternally creates each thing at its proper time, but the things themselves are temporally originated.

Refutation of Tashbih and Taḥdīd
Maturidi refutes two objections using the distinction between affirmation and negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes claims that negating God's likeness or place leads to anthropomorphism or limitation, and why negation does not imply comparison.

Core Idea
Negation never creates comparison—only affirmation does.

This passage addresses two common objections against the Muslim understanding of divine transcendence. Some argue that denying God's resemblance to creation is itself a form of anthropomorphism. Others claim that denying God a location imposes a limit on Him. Maturidi responds by clarifying the logic of negation versus affirmation, showing that these objections misunderstand how statements function.

Guided Path
1
First objection and response
Read the first objection: that negating God's likeness to creation is itself tashbih. Notice how Maturidi uses the example of black and white to illustrate that negation does not posit similarity.
The black/white analogy is key: denying resemblance between black and white does not make them similar.
2
Principle: negation removes from conception
Understand that negation removes the negated thing from imagination and intellect. Therefore, no similarity is established.
3
Second objection and response
Read the objection that denying God a place (makan) implies limitation (tahdid). Maturidi argues that affirming a place, not negating it, is what imposes a limit.
The one who says 'in every place' or 'in a place' is the one who limits God.
4
Connecting both refutations
Observe that both objections are refuted by the same logic: comparison and limitation arise only from affirmative statements, not from negation. This upholds tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أجاب من سأله: إنكم إذا نفيتم عن الله شبه خلقه، وعن خلقه شبهه فقد شبهتم. فقال: ذلك نفي، وليس في النفي تشبيه؛ ألا ترى أن من قال مثله في السواد والبياض من أنه لا يشبه أحدهما الآخر، إنه لا يوجب التشابه، وإنما يكون ذلك في الإثبات. وما ذكره حسن، ولو كان بذلك تشابه لكان بقوله هذا يشبه ذا إيجاب الخلاف، وفي ذلك قلب الحقائق وإبطال المجاز كله. وجملته أن النفي يرفع المنفي عن الوهم والعقل، وإذا ارتفع ذلك لم يُقدّراه. والتشابه هو الواقع تحت قدر من جوهر أو صفة أو حد؛ فلذلك بطل معناه. و بمثله يجاب لمن يزعم أنكم إذا لم تصفوا الله بمكان فقد حددتم. وأن الحد هو نهاية المكان، ومحال نفي تحديد في الوصف به، وكذلك الأمكنة. بل القائل بكل مكان أو بمكان دون مكان هو الذي حدّه؛ إذ أثبته على ما أثبت المكان المضاف إليه مما يُقدّره العقل والوهم، وعند ذلك التحديد والتشبيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he answered the one who asked him: 'If you negate from God the likeness of His creation, and from His creation its likeness, then you have made a comparison (tashbih).' He said: That is negation, and there is no comparison in negation. Do you not see that the one who says the same regarding black and white, that one does not resemble the other, it does not necessitate similarity, and that only occurs in affirmation. And what he mentioned is good, and if there were comparison in that, then his saying 'this resembles that' would be an affirmation of difference, and in that is the overturning of realities and the invalidation of all metaphor. In summary, negation removes the negated thing from imagination and intellect, and when that is removed, they do not conceive it. And comparison (tashbih) is what falls under a measure (qadr) of essence, attribute, or limit; therefore its meaning is invalid. And with the same is answered the one who claims that if you do not describe God with a place, then you have limited Him (haddad). And that limit is the end of place, and it is impossible to negate limitation by describing with it, and likewise places. Rather, the one who says 'in every place' or 'in a place without a place' is the one who limited Him, because he affirmed Him in accordance with what he affirmed the place attributed to Him, which the intellect and imagination conceive, and at that point there is limitation and comparison. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage refutes two objections: one claiming that negating resemblance between God and creation is itself tashbih, and another that denying a place for God implies limitation (taḥdīd). The response explains that negation does not posit likeness; likeness only arises from affirmative statements. The second objection is refuted by showing that asserting a place for God is what truly limits Him. The conclusion is that negation removes the negated from conception, and tashbih requires a measure of essence, attribute, or limit.

Key Terms
النفي
الإثبات
التشبيه
الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating God's resemblance to creation or denying Him a place is itself a form of anthropomorphism or limitation.
Correction: Negation does not posit any similarity or limit; it merely removes the negated from consideration. Comparison and limitation arise only from affirmative statements.

Checkpoint
Why does negation not constitute tashbih?
A) Because negation removes the negated from conception
B) Because negation affirms similarity
C) Because negation is a form of affirmation
D) Because negation only applies to accidents

Key Takeaway
Negation removes the negated from conception, so comparison and limitation only arise from affirmative statements, not from negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever described God in a way that, without realizing it, limited Him to human categories? What does this passage reveal about your own tendency to imagine God within familiar frames? How can you train your mind to negate those images without substituting new ones?
?
When you say 'God is not like anything,' do you truly mean it, or do you still harbor a mental picture — even an abstract one? This passage says negation removes from imagination and intellect. What would it cost you to actually let go of every mental image of Allah?
?
The second objection shows that affirming 'God is everywhere' (or anywhere) actually imposes a limit. Have you ever used such phrases casually, not realizing they confine God? How can you adjust your speech so that your affirmations about God never become limitations?

Why God's Act Has No 'How'
Maturidi states the rejection of the question 'how' for God's act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the question 'how' regarding God's act based on the principle that everything with a 'how' has similars.

Core Idea
God's act is beyond 'how' because having a 'how' implies comparison.

Maturidi begins by addressing a common but misguided question: 'How does God act?' He asserts that such a question must be rejected outright. The reason is that asking 'how' presupposes similarity to created things, which is impossible for the Creator. This short but profound statement establishes a key principle of divine transcendence in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the core argument.
Notice the concise style: Maturidi states the answer, then gives the reason.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the link between 'how' (kayf) and having similars (dhu amthal). Why does the presence of a 'how' imply similarity?
This hinges on the uniqueness of God (tawhid).
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument protects divine transcendence. Why is it invalid to ask 'how' about any of God's attributes or actions?
This principle applies to all divine names and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الفقيه رحمه الله : جواب هذا السؤال دفعه أن ليس لفعله كيف؛ إذ كل ذي كيف هو ذو أمثال . ثم القول في كون خلق الشيء إنه هو أو غيره اختلاف. فمنهم من يقول: هو هو، وبه يقول؛ والسؤال على مذهبه فاسد، لأنه لا غير لخلقه فيمثل هو به. ومنهم من يقول: خلق الشيء فهو صفته التي وصف بها في الأزل، فالسؤال عن كيفيته هو السؤال عن كيفية ذاته وعلمه وقدرته ، وذلك فاسد ذلك.
And the jurist (may God have mercy on him) said: The answer to this question is to reject it, because there is no 'how' (kayf) to His act; for everything that has a 'how' has similars. Then there is disagreement concerning whether the creation of a thing is it or other than it. Some say: it is it, and that is what he says; and the question according to his doctrine is invalid, because there is no other for His creation that he could resemble it. And some say: the creation of a thing is His attribute with which He described Himself in preeternity, so the question about its 'how' is a question about the 'how' of His essence, His knowledge, and His power, and that is invalid.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage defines and refutes the question about the 'how' of God's act. Maturidi first states that such a question must be rejected because having a 'how' implies having similars, which is impossible for God. Then he presents two opinions on the relation between the act of creation and the created thing: some say they are identical, others say creation is a divine attribute. According to the first, the question 'how' is invalid because there is nothing else to compare; according to the second, asking about the 'how' of creation is like asking about the 'how' of God's essence, which is also invalid. The passage establishes the principle of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Key Terms
كيف (kayf)
ذو أمثال (dhu amthal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can ask 'how' about God's actions and expect a comprehensible answer.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the question itself is invalid because it implies similarity to created things.

Checkpoint
What reason does Maturidi give for rejecting the question 'how' regarding God's act?
A) Because God's act is uncaused.
B) Because everything with a 'how' has similars.
C) Because creation is from nothing.
D) Because it is not mentioned in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
God's act cannot be described by 'how' because that would imply similarity to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself mentally asking 'how' about God's actions—whether in creation, providence, or your own life—will you now stop and reject that question as invalid? What will you replace it with?
?
Do you ever compare God's way of doing things to human ways, maybe feeling that He should act more like a just person would? How does this lesson call you to abandon that comparison and submit to His absolute uniqueness?
?
Think of a recent difficult event where you longed to understand God's 'how'. Would you be willing to say, 'There is no how; I will not ask'? Write a concrete prayer you can use next time that question arises.

Two Views on Creation and Their Implications
Maturidi presents two views on the relation of creation to the created thing and shows both reject the question 'how'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two theological opinions about the relation between creation and the created thing, and how each view independently invalidates the question 'how'.

Core Idea
Whether creation is identical to the thing or an attribute, asking 'how' is futile.

Having established that God's act has no 'how', Maturidi now examines two different theological perspectives on the nature of creation. Some scholars held that the act of creation is identical to the created thing itself, while others considered it a preeternal attribute of God. Maturidi shows that both positions lead to the same conclusion: the question 'how' is logically invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two opinions
Read the Arabic passage that presents the disagreement. Notice the structure: first the disagreement is stated, then each opinion is explained with its consequence.
This passage is a classic example of theological reasoning.
2
Understand the first opinion
The first opinion says creation is identical to the created thing. Why does this make the question 'how' invalid? Think about what 'comparison' requires.
If creation is the thing itself, there is no separate entity to compare.
3
Understand the second opinion
The second opinion says creation is an attribute of God. Explain why asking 'how' about an attribute is like asking about God's essence.
Divine attributes are beyond human comprehension.
4
Compare the conclusions
Notice that both opinions, though different, lead to the same result. What does this say about the invalidity of the question?
This reinforces the principle of tanzih.

Maturidi's Critique of Creation-Identity
Maturidi critiques the view that creation equals the thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of the claim that the act of creation is identical to the created thing.

Core Idea
If creation equals the thing, then either the thing is God or it is self-caused.

Imam Maturidi tackles a subtle theological position that attempted to preserve God's transcendence by collapsing the act of creation into its product. By carefully analyzing the relationship between Creator, act, and creation, he uncovers a devastating dilemma: if creation is identical to the thing, then that thing must be either divine or self-sufficient—both denying God's unique role as Creator. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation without analyzing. Let the argument sink in.
Notice the question mark in the first sentence—it signals the core dilemma.
2
Identify the dilemma
Maturidi asks: if creation and thing are the same, is the thing by God's essence or by its own essence? Think about the implications.
This is a both-this-and-that trap: either choice leads to absurdity.
3
Trace the consequences
Follow how Maturidi develops the absurdity: How can God be Creator if no distinct act issued? Why is the creation not more deserving of being Creator? And finally, how can we infer from the witnessed world to the unseen when causality fails?
The argument uses the principle that priority does not imply causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وقوله : «خلق الشيء هو ذلك الشيء»، فإذا الشيء بذات الله أم بذات نفسه؟ إذ لم يكن من الله إلا ذاته، ولا إلى الخلق منه سوى الخلق بذاته. فكيف صار خالقاً ولم يكن منه غير الخلق دون أن كان الخلق بلا غيره؟ ولم لا كان الخلق في أن يكون خالقا أحق منه؛ إذ لم يكن منه إليه سوى أن كان وقدم الشيء لا يوجب كون آخر به هو إذا لم يكن منه إليه ما به يكون في الشاهد كيف أوجب ذلك في الغائب؟
The jurist (may God have mercy on him) said: And his statement: 'The creation of a thing is that thing itself.' So is the thing by God's essence or by its own essence? Since nothing comes from God except His essence, and from Him to creation there is only the creation by His essence. So how did He become Creator when nothing came from Him other than creation, without creation being without other? And why is not creation more entitled to be Creator than He? Since nothing came from Him to it except that He was and the precedence of a thing does not necessitate the existence of another by which He is, if there is nothing from Him to it that would make Him so in the witnessed world, how did He necessitate that in the unseen?
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the claim that the act of creation is identical to the created thing. Maturidi poses a dilemma: if creation and the thing are identical, then the thing must be either by God's essence or its own essence. Since only God's essence proceeds from Him, and the only thing from Him to the creature is the creation itself, it is unclear how God can be called 'Creator' when no distinct act has issued. He further argues that the created thing itself would be more deserving of the title 'creator' if it is identical to the act. Finally, he uses the principle that precedence (qidam) does not entail causation in the observable world, questioning how it could apply to the unseen.

Key Terms
خلق الشيء هو ذلك الشيء
الذات
الشاهد والغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that identifying creation with the thing avoids anthropomorphism by removing an external act from God.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to absurd consequences: either the thing is divine or self-caused, undermining God's unique role and making causation inexplicable.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what problem arises if the creation of a thing is identical to that thing?
A) It implies that God changes.
B) It becomes unclear whether the creature is by God's essence or its own essence.
C) It negates God's power.
D) It implies that God is a body.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the identity of creation and thing by showing it leads to a logical dilemma about the essence of the created object and an invalid inference from priority to causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological claim that seems to simplify God, do you accept it without testing its logical consequences? How can you train yourself to think like Maturidi, asking 'if this is true, what else follows?'
?
Maturidi uses an analogy from the witnessed world to the unseen. Are you consistent in how you apply reasoning about God? Do you sometimes draw conclusions about God based on weak analogies from your own experience, without checking whether they lead to absurdities?
?
The passage challenges the assumption that priority implies causation. In your own life, do you ever assume that because something came first it must have caused what follows? How might that habit affect your understanding of God's relationship to creation? What step can you take today to deepen your reverence for God's transcendence?
